<a id="paper-2605.04640"></a>
### ⭐ [Neural network modeling of many-body super- and sub-radiant dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04640v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Darrick Chang, Zala Lenarčič  
**Authors:** Gianluca Lagnese, Laurin Brunner, Lorenzo Rossi, Darrick Chang, Markus Schmitt, Zala Lenarčič  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04640v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **5/5** · 🔥 `Dicke superradiance` **4/5** · 🔥 `free-space correlated emission` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

In the inset of (b) we show the difference between ResNet and quantum trajectories results divided by the number of atoms V . In (b) we monitor the cumulative error of the TDVP algorithm R(t). (c) and (d): convergence of time evolution with the NQS method by varying network parameters...</sub>

</details>

Left column: Correlations C(ℓ, t) at different relative sites ℓ. Right column: Absolute difference of correla- tions calculated by NQS or cumulant expansion with respect to the quantum trajectories result.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a neural network-based approach to simulate the many-body dissipative dynamics of large atom arrays. By using ResNet and dCNN architectures, the authors successfully model super- and sub-radiant regimes in 1D and 2D systems beyond the limits of exact diagonalization. The work demonstrates that neural networks can efficiently capture the complex, long-range correlations characteristic of subradiant decay.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful new numerical tool (NQS) for studying the long-time subradiant regimes and many-body correlations in light-matter interfaces, which are otherwise inaccessible to standard methods.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of simulating many-body super- and sub-radiant dynamics in large-scale light-matter interfaces where traditional exact and tensor-network methods fail.

**Main result.** The first successful application of Neural Quantum States (NQS) to capture the dissipative dynamics of 1D and 2D atom arrays up to 40 atoms, demonstrating that dCNN architectures can efficiently capture long-range correlations with fewer parameters than ResNet.

**Method.** Neural Quantum States (NQS) using ResNet and dilated CNN (dCNN) architectures, implemented via a POVM-based representation and evolved using the Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) and Monte Carlo sampling.

**Model / system.** Ordered, densely packed 1D and 2D arrays of cold atoms in free space, modeled by an effective spin Hamiltonian with photon-mediated long-range dipolar interactions and a Lindblad master equation.

**Key observables.** Number of excited atoms (excitation density), connected spin-spin correlators, and cumulative TDVP error (KL divergence).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inter-atom distance (d), decay rate (Gamma_0), network depth (d), number of features (f), and kernel size (k).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The light field is integrated out to provide an effective spin description; the use of a tetrahedral POVM for density matrix representation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 and 2 show benchmarking of NQS against exact quantum trajectories and convergence studies in 1D and 2D; Figure 3 compares excitation density across system sizes; Figure 4 provides a schematic comparison of ResNet and dCNN architectures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of simulating large-scale dissipative dynamics, describes the NQS/POVM methodology and neural architectures (ResNet vs dCNN), presents benchmarking results against exact and semi-classical methods, and concludes with efficiency comparisons and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

There is significant interest in exploring novel phenomena in quantum light-matter interfaces, which are driven by the combination of structured dissipation and long-range interactions that are typical in such systems. To this end, it is important to develop new general numerical simulation techniques, which can access large system sizes and are not based on semi-classical approaches. Here, we report the first application of neural quantum states to obtain the dissipative dynamics of light-matter-coupled systems beyond what is accessible with exact and tensor-network calculations. We specifically apply this method to simulate the many-body emission dynamics of approximately 40 atoms, arranged in dense arrays in one and two dimensions. These systems have been chosen because they can support prominent subradiant dynamics at late times and could be realized with cold atomic quantum simulators.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04136"></a>
### ⭐ [Multiparameter function estimation for general Hamiltonians](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04136v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Alexey V. Gorshkov  
**Authors:** Erfan Abbasgholinejad, Sean R. Muleady, Jacob Bringewatt, Lorcan O. Conlon, Alexey V. Gorshkov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04136v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04136_figures/2605.04136_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04136_figures/2605.04136_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper solves the problem of finding the fundamental quantum limit for estimating functions of multiple parameters in a general Hamiltonian. It proves that this complex task can be reduced to an optimized single-parameter problem and provides a practical, two-step adaptive protocol to reach this limit. This unifies previous results and extends them to the most general case of non-commuting generators.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a fundamental framework for quantum sensing, offering a way to achieve optimal precision when dealing with complex, non-commuting signal generators, which is highly relevant for many-body sensing and quantum metrology.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the ultimate quantum precision limit for estimating a scalar function of multiple unknown parameters encoded in a general, potentially non-commuting Hamiltonian.

**Main result.** The authors derive a tight, saturable lower bound on the variance of the estimator that reduces the complex multi-parameter function estimation problem to an optimized single-parameter quantum Cramér-Rao bound.

**Method.** The problem is mapped to an effective single-parameter estimation problem using a semidefinite programming (SDP) framework and a two-step adaptive protocol involving Hamiltonian reshaping and randomized Trotterization.

**Model / system.** A general time-independent signal Hamiltonian H0 = sum f_j(theta) g_j, where theta are unknown parameters, f_j are smooth functions, and g_j are linearly independent, traceless Hermitian generators, supplemented by a controllable, time-dependent control Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** The variance of the estimator for the target function q(theta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The precision parameter gamma (derived from an SDP), the total interrogation time t, the Jacobian matrix F of the couplings, and the conditioning constant kappa.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Parameters are time-independent, functions are twice continuously differentiable, and generators are linearly independent and traceless; the protocol assumes an asymptotic limit of large total time t.

**Figures summary.** Table I compares optimal precision bounds across various scenarios including single-parameter, commuting, and arbitrary generator sets.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the multi-parameter function estimation problem, derives a tight lower bound via SDP duality, proposes a constructive protocol using Hamiltonian reshaping and adaptive estimation, and provides error analysis and stability theorems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Estimation of physical parameters encoded in a Hamiltonian is a central task in quantum sensing and learning. While the ultimate precision limit for estimating a single parameter coupled to a single generator is well established, the corresponding bound for estimating a function of multiple parameters-each coupled to distinct and possibly non-commuting generators-remains unknown in general. Here, we derive the ultimate quantum limit and present an estimation protocol for any function of parameters in a general Hamiltonian that attains this bound. We show that, although the task is fundamentally a multiparameter problem, our tight bound reduces to an optimized single-parameter quantum Cramér-Rao bound, even for arbitrary generator sets. Our result unifies and extends previous works, providing a general framework for optimal function estimation in quantum systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04540"></a>
### [Hierarchical entanglement transitions and hidden area-law sectors in quantum many-body dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04540v1)

**Authors:** Tarun Grover  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04540v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **5/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04540_figures/2605.04540_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Comparison of the exact expectation val- ues with those obtained from a truncated density ma- trix where we have kept only the leading Schmidt vector of the canonical purification of a Gibbs state subjected to a local quantum quench. Here H = PL−1 i=1 ZiZi+1 + PL i=1(gXi + hZi) + Z1/4 −ZL/4 with L = 14, g = 1.1, h = 0.35. The specific parameters in the Hamiltonian are taken from Ref. [34]. **Summary.** The paper discovers that local quantum quenches in chaotic systems create a hierarchical entanglement structure where different Renyi indices exhibit different scaling laws. It identifies a 'hidden' area-law sector that carries the linear response to the quench, suggesting that parts of the highly entangled state may be efficiently approximable by tensor networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to many-body dynamics as it identifies a specific, hidden structure in entanglement that could potentially allow for more efficient classical simulations of otherwise volume-law entangled states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the entanglement structure of quantum many-body systems following local quenches, specifically looking for hidden area-law sectors and hierarchical entanglement transitions.

**Main result.** The authors reveal a Renyi-index-tuned transition where S_alpha obeys an area law for alpha > 1 and a volume law for alpha <= 1, alongside a recursive, hierarchical structure of entanglement transitions in the Schmidt sectors.

**Method.** The study uses analytical derivations in a circuit model, exact diagonalization of random circuits and chaotic spin chains, and tensor network-based approximation analysis.

**Model / system.** The paper analyzes 1D random unitary circuits (Haar-random, Clifford+T, and U(1) symmetric) and local quantum quenches on the canonical purification of Gibbs states in the mixed-field Ising model.

**Key observables.** Renyi entropies (S_alpha), von Neumann entropy (S_1), Schmidt eigenvalues, and truncation error.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Renyi index (alpha), quench strength (theta or epsilon), inverse temperature (beta), and time (t).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes generic chaotic dynamics and uses a maximally scrambled limit for certain analytical bounds; assumes the existence of a large, time-independent Schmidt eigenvalue.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare expectation values and truncation errors against exact values, show the cumulative sum of Schmidt weights, illustrate the hierarchical scaling of Renyi entropies, and demonstrate the monotonic growth and saturation of entropy in both circuit and Ising models.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of entanglement structure, presents a circuit model to derive the Renyi-index-tuned transition, extends these findings to local quenches of Gibbs states, provides a recursive analysis of the hierarchical structure, and validates results via numerical simulations of random circuits and the Ising model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Chaotic many-body dynamics typically generates volume-law entanglement from initially low-entangled states. We reveal an intricate, hierarchical entanglement structure in local quantum quenches, both in the canonical purification of locally quenched Gibbs states and in a companion pure-state circuit model. In either setting, the full state exhibits a Renyi-index-tuned transition: at long times, $S_{α>1}$ obeys an area law, while $S_{α\le 1}$ is volume-law. More strikingly, the response linear in the quench strength is carried by only an O(1)-dimensional dominant Schmidt sector; the corresponding states exhibit their own area-to-volume-law transitions at critical indices $α_c<1$, implying polynomial-bond-dimension approximability in one dimension. We provide evidence that this hierarchy persists recursively: upon bipartitioning the dominant Schmidt states, their leading Schmidt sectors exhibit analogous structure. We derive the mechanism analytically in the circuit model, prove the $S_{α>1}$ area law for locally quenched Gibbs states, and support the hierarchy by exact diagonalization of random circuits and locally quenched Gibbs states of chaotic spin chains.

</details>

### [Polarization-Controlled Photon Mode Switching and Photon--Magnon Coupling in a Planar Cavity--Magnonic System](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05018v1)

**Authors:** Abhishek Maurya, Sachin Verma, Bojong Kim, Biswanath Bhoi, Rajeev Singh, Sang-Koog Kim

**Summary.** The researchers developed a method to control photon-magnon interactions by simply rotating a resonator in a planar architecture. By changing the orientation, they can switch between different photon modes and redistribute coupling strengths. This provides a scalable and robust way to engineer hybrid quantum-magnonic interfaces.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a versatile mechanism for 'polarization engineering' in hybrid systems, providing a way to control light-matter interaction channels and dissipation pathways via geometric orientation, which is highly relevant to circuit QED and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving precise, reconfigurable control over photon-magnon coupling (PMC) pathways in hybrid systems without relying on complex external tuning like magnetic fields or active components.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that rotating an electric-LC resonator (ELCR) enables polarization-selective switching between two orthogonal photon modes, allowing for the continuous redistribution of interaction strength between competing photon-magnon hybridization channels.

**Method.** The study combines full-wave electromagnetic simulations (CST), an equivalent circuit model (ABCD-matrix formalism), and an effective three-mode Hamiltonian using input-output theory to analyze experimental transmission spectra.

**Model / system.** A planar cavity-magnonic platform consisting of an electric-LC resonator (ELCR) side-coupled to a microstrip transmission line, integrated with a Yttrium Iron Garnet (YIG) thin film.

**Key observables.** Transmission coefficient (S21), photon-magnon coupling strengths (g31, g23), photon mode frequencies, and radiative (extrinsic) damping rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rotation angle (theta), coupling strengths (g), intrinsic and extrinsic damping rates (beta, gamma), and the dimensionless damping order parameter (Phi_gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Application of the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA) to the Hamiltonian and the Markov approximation for treating waveguide coupling strengths.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Device schematic, optical images, and transmission spectra at various angles. Fig 2: Simulated surface-current distributions showing mode-specific loop configurations. Fig 3: Equivalent circuit models. Fig 4: Extracted damping rates vs. mutual inductance and mode-splitting colormaps.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of coupling control, describes the planar ELCR-YIG platform, presents the electromagnetic and circuit models for polarization-dependent excitation, details the three-mode Hamiltonian for hybridization, and concludes with experimental validation of mode switching and coupling redistribution.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work presents polarization-selective photon-magnon coupling (PMC) in a planar cavity-magnonic platform consisting of an electric-LC resonator (ELCR) side-coupled to a microstrip transmission line and integrated with a yttrium iron garnet (YIG) thin film. The ELCR supports two orthogonal photon modes at $\sim 3.93$ GHz and $\sim 5.73$ GHz, whose excitation and radiative damping are governed by the resonator orientation relative to the microwave-field polarization. Rotating the resonator enables controlled switching between these modes and tunable photon-magnon hybridization. An equivalent circuit model including intrinsic and extrinsic damping successfully reproduces the polarization-driven mode switching, while an effective three-mode Hamiltonian accurately captures the coupled-mode evolution. The results reveal strong angular tunability of the PMC strength through redistribution between two competing interaction channels. At $θ= 0^\circ$, only the lower-frequency photon mode is excited, yielding $g_{31}=56.5$ MHz, while the higher-frequency mode remains inactive. As the angle increases, both channels become active: $g_{31}$ increases from $56.5$ to $98$ MHz over $0^\circ$-$60^\circ$ before vanishing at $90^\circ$, whereas $g_{23}$ decreases from $76$ to $30$ MHz over $30^\circ$-$90^\circ$. The observed evolution yields a measured transition near $25.7^\circ$ and a symmetry-related model-predicted transition near $154.3^\circ$. These findings establish resonator-orientation--driven polarization selectivity as a versatile mechanism for controllable photon--magnon interactions in planar architectures.

</details>

### [Establishing Mixed-State Phase Equivalence beyond Renormalization Fixed Points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04159v1)

**Authors:** Yuhan Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04159v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04159_figures/2605.04159_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Mixed-state phase diagram as a function of λ. The intrinsically non-trivial phase corresponds to −1 ≤λ &lt; 0 and the trivial phase to 0 &lt; λ ≤1, with λ = ∓1 being the mixed-state renormalization fixed points, ρ(−1) ∝1⊗N +CZX(N) and ρ(1) ∝1⊗N +X⊗N. The phase equivalence within each phase is proven by constructing the four channel circuits E and R, ¯E and ¯R.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04159_figures/2605.04159_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Configurations of channel circuit (reproduce from [28]). (a) The original local finite-depth channel circuit E. (b) The reorganized circuit is such that two channels in each layer are separated by 2r. (c) The recovery channel circuit R, which is quasi-local and low-depth.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04159_figures/2605.04159_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Two point correlation function Cij with re- spect to |i −j|, for the transition point λ = 0 and non- transition points λ = 0.2, 0.5, 0.8. The system size is N = 24. The simulation is performed with ITensor Soft- ware Library [56] with the maximal bond dimension cho- sen as Dmax = 200.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04159_figures/2605.04159_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a new framework to prove that different mixed-state quantum phases are equivalent by constructing local quantum circuits that transform one into the other. It successfully extends this proof beyond the usual renormalization fixed points to include intrinsically nontrivial phases. The method uses the parent Lindbladian construction to ensure that the circuits preserve the system's underlying symmetries.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a rigorous framework for characterizing the stability and equivalence of non-equilibrium mixed-state phases beyond standard fixed-point approaches.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Establishing phase equivalence for mixed-state quantum phases beyond the renormalization fixed-point paradigm, specifically when conditional mutual information remains finite.

**Main result.** The author analytically establishes phase equivalence within two distinct 1D mixed-state phases by constructing low-depth, quasi-local channel circuits using a parent Lindbladian construction.

**Method.** The work utilizes Matrix Product Operators (MPOs), the parent Lindbladian construction, and the Petz recovery map to generate channel circuits that preserve global symmetries.

**Model / system.** One-dimensional quantum systems described by Matrix Product Density Operators (MPDOs) and a path of mixed states $\rho(\lambda)$ connecting a trivial phase to an intrinsically nontrivial phase with anomalous $Z_2$ or $Z_N$ symmetry.

**Key observables.** Conditional Mutual Information (CMI), Markov length, entanglement entropy, and two-point correlation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The control parameter $\lambda$ (defining the phase transition at $\lambda=0$), bond dimension, and the distance $d$ between regions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction assumes a finite Markov length and relies on the existence of a pre-bialgebraic structure to ensure the locality of the channel circuits.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a mixed-state phase diagram with trivial and nontrivial phases; Figure 2 illustrates the configuration of the constructed channel circuits; Figure 4 shows numerical decay of CMI and Renyi-2 CMI with distance.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining phase equivalence and the problem of finite CMI, introduces a model with a path of mixed states, develops a construction using the parent Lindbladian to create symmetry-preserving channel circuits, and concludes with generalizations to $Z_N$ symmetries and numerical verification of criticality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding mixed-state quantum phases is a central challenge in the era of quantum simulation, where many existing studies focus on renormalization fixed points. In this work, we move beyond the renormalization fixed-point paradigm by constructing a quantum phase transition connecting two distinct one-dimensional fixed points, both exhibiting finite conditional mutual information and one of which is intrinsically nontrivial. We analytically establish phase equivalence within each of the two phases by explicitly constructing low-depth, quasi-local channel circuits that connect states within each phase. Crucially, our approach leverages the parent Lindbladian construction to generate the desired channel circuits. We further demonstrate that this framework generalizes naturally to a broad class of intrinsically nontrivial mixed-state quantum phases. Our method establishes a framework for rigorously analyzing phase equivalence of intrinsically non-trivial mixed states beyond the renormalization fixed points.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04508"></a>
### [Thermodynamic efficiency of self-organisation in nonequilibrium steady states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04508v1)

**Authors:** Qianyang Chen, Mikhail Prokopenko  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04508v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04508_figures/2605.04508_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Schematic of persistent Ising model. (a) We consider the square-lattice Ising model with nearest-neighbour interactions, modified by a non-conservative drive E0 as described in [30]. The transition probability of a single spin is determined by the effective change in the energy ∆˜E = ∆E + E0, where ∆E is the energy difference before and after the spin-flip. (b) When E0 &gt; 0, there is a cost associated with the spin-flip. Transitions in all directions are inhibited. (c) When E0 &lt; 0, the spins are self-excited. Transitions in all directions are promoted.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04508_figures/2605.04508_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Schematic of active Ising model. The model consists of N spin-carrying and self-propelled particles on a lattice of size Lx × Ly, where each site contains an arbitrary number of particles (left panel). The particles undergo two different dynamics (right panel): spin-flipping and hopping. Local density ρi and local magnetisation mi are measured for each site.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04508_figures/2605.04508_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Phase transition in the persistent Ising model. (a) Plot of control parameter J and its conjugate order parameter ⟨P σiσj/N⟩(average interaction) shows that the system undergoes second-order phase transitions for different values of drive E0. When E0 = 0, the system is at equilibrium, while E0 ̸= 0 represents a nonequilibrium steady state (NESS). The value of J where the phase transition occurs depends on E0. (b) The conjugate order parameter is shown as a function of control parameter J and drive E0. The curve separating two phases of the system represents the critical regime. Lattice size: 50 × 50.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04508_figures/2605.04508_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Thermodynamic efficiency. Thermodynamic efficiencies (η(J)) with respect to control parameter J, at different external drives E0. In all cases, η maximises at the critical control parameter, with negligible discrepancies due to numerical simulation. Lattice size: 50 × 50.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04508_figures/2605.04508_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Thermodynamic efficiency (η) compared with inferential efficiency (η▷◁). (a) At equilibrium, η(J) matches η▷◁(J) for all values of J. (b) In NESS, η(J) ̸= η▷◁(J). When E0 &lt; 0 (red and peach curves), the inferential efficiency (dashed lines) is higher than the thermodynamic efficiency (solid lines). The opposite holds for E0 &gt; 0 (light and dark blue curves). In both equilibrium and NESS, η(J) and η▷◁(J) are optimised at the same control parameter value. Lattice size: 50 × 50.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates the efficiency of energy conversion into macroscopic order in nonequilibrium steady states. It demonstrates that while thermodynamic efficiency peaks at phase transitions, it diverges from inferential efficiency due to the violation of the fluctuation-dissipation theorem out of equilibrium.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a fundamental link between thermodynamics and information theory in nonequilibrium systems, which is highly relevant to understanding dissipation and control in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining how efficiently nonequilibrium self-organizing systems convert consumed energy into macroscopic order and investigating the divergence between thermodynamic and inferential efficiency out of equilibrium.

**Main result.** Thermodynamic efficiency maximizes at phase transitions in both equilibrium and nonequilibrium steady states, while the gap between thermodynamic and inferential efficiency serves as a measure of the system's distance from equilibrium.

**Method.** The study uses Monte Carlo simulations and a refined mean-field approximation (treating density and magnetization as independent Gaussian variables) to analyze two models.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on two nonequilibrium models: the Persistent Ising Model, which uses a non-conservative drive to break detailed balance, and the Active Ising Model, which features self-propelled particles on a 2D lattice.

**Key observables.** Thermodynamic efficiency (eta), inferential efficiency (eta_triangular_triangle), Shannon entropy, infinitesimal work, and magnetization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength (J), external drive (E0), self-propulsion (epsilon), external magnetic field (h), and lattice size.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The refined mean-field approach assumes density and magnetization are independent Gaussian random variables, which fails to capture the homogeneous bistable phase and becomes inaccurate in strong-coupling regimes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Persistent Ising model dynamics; Figure 7 shows phase plots and profiles for the Active Ising model; Figure 11 demonstrates efficiency peaks at phase transitions; Figure 12 shows finite-size effects on efficiencies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of energy-to-order conversion, defines the information-theoretic framework, applies it to the Persistent and Active Ising models, analyzes phase transitions and efficiency divergence, and concludes with a discussion on the Principle of Super-efficiency.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Active matter generates order or patterns through nonequilibrium dynamics. An open research challenge is to determine how efficiently a nonequilibrium self-organising system can convert consumed energy into macroscopic order. We study an information-theoretic quantity that directly addresses this challenge by estimating the entropy reduction induced by a small control-parameter perturbation, relative to the generalised work required for the perturbation. This quantity has previously been considered mainly in an equilibrium or near-equilibrium context, and here we extend this framework and apply it to two nonequilibrium self-organising systems: persistent and active Ising models. We observe that the thermodynamic efficiency of nonequilibrium systems maximises at phase transitions, as in equilibrium systems. Furthermore, we compare thermodynamic efficiency and inferential efficiency across control parameters. While these two quantities are equal in equilibrium as a consequence of the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, we report that they diverge out of equilibrium, and the gap reflects how far the system is from equilibrium.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04384"></a>
### [Kitaev chain in synthetic dimension with cavity-controlled Majorana modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04384v1)

**Authors:** Adel Ali, Alexey Belyanin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04384v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `correlated cavity matter` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04384_figures/2605.04384_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic of a disk-shaped 2DEG sample placed in a strong static transverse magnetic field B0 = B0ˆz and proximally coupled to the inductive element of a supercon- ducting LC circuit. Angular harmonics of the circuit field, ALC(r, ϕ) ∼eiℓϕ, obey the selection rule ∆m = ℓ, thereby inducing hopping m →m + ℓalong the synthetic angular- momentum dimension. A loop geometry dominated by ℓ= 0 and ℓ= 1 harmonics produces the nearest-neighbor synthetic hopping needed for a Kitaev-chain-like reduction, while higher harmonics generate longer-range corrections.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04384_figures/2605.04384_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Representative loop geometries for the inductive element of the superconducting LC circuit, shown above a 2DEG sample in a strong perpendicular magnetic field B0ˆz, with dominant angular harmonics ℓindicated. The geometries dominated by ℓ= 0 and ℓ= 1 provide the closest route to a nearest-neighbor Kitaev-chain Hamiltonian, while even- harmonic geometries generate longer-range or two-sublattice couplings.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new platform for topological quantum computing using a 2D electron gas coupled to a superconducting LC resonator. By using the angular momentum states of the lowest Landau level as a synthetic dimension, the authors show how to engineer a Kitaev chain with controllable Majorana zero modes. The platform's main advantage is the ability to perform non-local readout and control of Majorana states using standard circuit QED techniques.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a hybrid architecture combining circuit QED and semiconductor physics to create a programmable synthetic dimension for topological states, which is highly relevant for developing scalable topological quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of achieving unambiguous realization, manipulation, and scalable implementation of Majorana zero modes (MZMs) in 1D topological superconductors, specifically regarding braiding and non-local readout.

**Main result.** The authors propose a tunable synthetic-dimension platform where a superconducting LC circuit induces the necessary hopping and pairing to realize a Kitaev chain, enabling robust non-local readout of Majorana parity.

**Method.** The study uses a Schrieffer-Wolff-like reduction in the dispersive regime to eliminate the LC mode and projects the Hamiltonian onto the lowest Landau level, using angular harmonic expansion to engineer interactions.

**Model / system.** A 2D electron gas (2DEG) in a strong perpendicular magnetic field coupled to a superconducting LC resonator, where angular-momentum states in the lowest Landau level serve as a synthetic 1D lattice.

**Key observables.** The dispersive cavity shift (chi), which allows for the measurement of Majorana fermion parity via the reflected microwave phase.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The off-resonant/dispersive regime (omega_LC >> electronic scales); topological criterion |mu_eff| < 2|t1|; energy scales for GaAs and InSb (e.g., induced gaps of 20-40 ueV for GaAs).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The LC mode is in its vacuum ground state; the LC loop geometry is smooth to avoid high-order harmonics; the system operates in the dispersive regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of a disk-shaped 2DEG coupled to an LC circuit.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical platform, derives the effective Hamiltonian via Landau-level projection and dispersive elimination, demonstrates the mapping to the Kitaev chain model, provides numerical estimates for GaAs and InSb, and discusses readout and geometric engineering.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a tunable synthetic-dimension platform for realizing Kitaev-chain physics with high degree of control over Majorana zero modes. It is based on a generic Landau-quantized two dimensional electron system coupled to the magnetic flux of a superconducting LC circuit. The structured vector potential of a superconducting LC inductor induces attractive interactions between electron angular-momentum states at the lowest Landau level. These states serve as a synthetic dimension for the coveted fermionic Kitaev chain, with Majorana zero modes existing at the boundaries of the angular-momentum lattice. The crucial advantage of this proposal is the possibility of a robust, nonlocal readout and control of the Majorana states by a LC resonator. The platform relies on mature circuit QED and semiconductor technologies and provides a promising pathway to topological quantum computing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.05162"></a>
### [Dynamical correlations in a dissipative XXZ spin chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05162v1)

**Authors:** Cătălin Paşcu Moca, Doru Sticlet, Ovidiu I. Pâţu, Balazs Dóra

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

The data is displayed on a logarithmic color scale to enhance the visibility of the light-cone structure. Dashed lines mark the light-cone boundaries. The dynamical exponent z governing the cone is ballistic (z = 1) for ∆&lt; 1, superdiffusive (z = 3/2) at ∆= 1, and diffusive (z = 2) for ∆&gt; 1. For ∆= 2.0, ζ = 2.0 two distinct fronts are visible, signaling coexistence of ballistic and diffusive modes. Data shown on a logarithmic color scale.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper explores how dissipation modifies the spreading of correlations in an XXZ spin chain. It demonstrates that while dissipation eventually leads to exponential decay, the underlying transport universality classes and light-cone structures remain intact during the early-time dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides critical insights into the robustness of many-body transport phenomena (like KPZ scaling) and the stability of causal light-cone structures in the presence of environmental decoherence, which is central to open quantum systems and many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how dissipation (spin loss and pumping) affects the dynamical spin correlations and transport universality classes in a 1D XXZ spin chain.

**Main result.** The fundamental transport universality classes (ballistic, KPZ, and diffusive) and the light-cone structure are preserved at short times, while dissipation causes exponential damping of correlations at long times.

**Method.** The authors use vectorized Time-Evolving Block Decimation (TEBD) for interacting regimes and a third-quantization framework with a Pfaffian representation for the noninteracting limit.

**Model / system.** A 1D spin-1/2 XXZ spin chain subject to Lindblad dynamics with uniform local spin-loss and pumping, leading to a featureless product state steady state.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal and transverse two-point spin correlators, and longitudinal spin autocorrelation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy (Delta), spin fugacity (zeta), dissipation rate (Gamma), and the crossover time scale (t*).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The steady state is assumed to be a Gaussian product state; numerical results are subject to bond dimension and time-step constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figures show magnetization vs. fugacity, spatiotemporal structures of longitudinal correlations (identifying light cones and dynamical exponents), and the decay of transverse/longitudinal autocorrelations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the dissipative XXZ model, establishes the steady state, analyzes unitary evolution (quench) dynamics, extends the study to full Lindbladian dynamics, and provides exact analytical benchmarks for the noninteracting limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study dynamical spin correlations in a dissipative XXZ spin chain subject to uniform local spin-loss and pumping. Starting from a mixed steady state that is featureless albeit possessing finite magnetization, rich dynamics emerges in time-dependent two-point correlators evaluated on top of it. For unitary evolution in which the reservoir is absent, the longitudinal correlators reproduce the established hierarchy of spin-transport universality classes - ballistic, Kardar-Parisi-Zhang (KPZ) superdiffusive, and diffusive - across the phase diagram. However, for finite magnetization, additional ballistic light cone propagation gets superimposed on the previous universality classes, arising from magnon propagation.The transverse correlator displays very fast, exponential decay of correlations without wavefront propagation in the easy-plane case. At the isotropic point, it follows KPZ scaling due to $SU(2)$ symmetry, while in the easy-axis regime, it is characterized by ballistic spreading of correlations. Under full Lindbladian dynamics, the universality classes are preserved at early times, while the correlations acquire an overall exponential damping in the long-time limit. In terms of methods, we have used vectorized TEBD for numerical simulations and exact analytical results obtained via a Pfaffian representation and the third-quantization framework for the noninteracting XX case.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04161"></a>
### [Quantum state texture of dynamical criticality](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04161v1)

**Authors:** Lucas C. Céleri, Krissia Zawadzki, Ivan Medina, Diogo O. Soares-Pinto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04161v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04161_figures/2605.04161_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Derivative of the time-averaged rugosity with respect to the quench parameter for the LMG model. This figure was constructed by computing the derivative of the data shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04161_figures/2605.04161_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Order parameter for the DQPT (upper panel) and time-average rugosity (bottom panel) for the LMG model as function of the intensity of the quench. For all plots we set h0 = 0 and δ = 1, so the critical point is hf = 1/2. The time averaging was computed by choosing T ≈103ν−1, with ν being the smallest Bohr frequency of the energy eigenpectrum in both h and j considered range.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04161_figures/2605.04161_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Rate function and rugosity density for the LMG model. The upper panel shows the rate function λt, which characterizes the DQPT-II, while the bottom panel shows the rugosity density Rϵ/2j with respect to the pre-quench Hamil- tonian eigenbasis for the same values of the quench. The fluctuations observed in the higher peaks of both panels, es- pecially for the initial times, are due to numerical errors when computing the logarithm of very small numbers. For all plots we set j = 1000.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper connects the concept of quantum state texture, called rugosity, to the identification of dynamical quantum phase transitions. It proves that rugosity can act as an order parameter for Type-I transitions and is directly related to the Loschmidt rate function in Type-II transitions. This establishes rugosity as a useful tool for diagnosing criticality in many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new information-theoretic perspective on nonequilibrium dynamics, linking the generation of quantum resources (texture) to the emergence of thermodynamic phase transitions and complexity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether 'rugosity' (a measure of quantum state texture) can serve as a fundamental diagnostic tool for identifying and characterizing both Type-I and Type-II dynamical quantum phase transitions (DQPTs).

**Main result.** The authors establish that for Type-II transitions, the Loschmidt rate function is equivalent to the density of rugosity in a specific basis, and for Type-I transitions, the time-averaged rugosity in the pre-quench basis acts as an order parameter.

**Method.** The researchers use the resource theory of rugosity to analyze sudden-quench protocols, employing semiclassical analysis, finite-size scaling, and comparisons with other complexity measures like Krylov complexity and entropy production.

**Model / system.** The primary model used is the Lipkin-Meshkov-Glick (LMG) model, a collective spin system undergoing a sudden quench of the transverse field.

**Key observables.** Rugosity (R_epsilon), Loschmidt rate function (lambda_t), magnetization (order parameter), and rugosity density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quench protocol (h0 to hf), transverse field strength (h), and the thermodynamic limit (N -> infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a sudden-quench protocol and relies on the existence of a well-defined semiclassical limit and separatrix structure.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows time-averaged magnetization and rugosity vs. quench intensity; Figure 2 displays the derivative of average rugosity vs. quench parameter; Figure 3 compares the rate function and rugosity density.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of rugosity, applies it to Type-I and Type-II DQPTs using the LMG model, provides a mechanistic explanation via semiclassical structures, and contextualizes the findings within broader complexity and entropy theories.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the role of quantum state texture in dynamical quantum phase transitions by establishing a direct connection between critical nonequilibrium dynamics and the recently introduced notion of rugosity, a measure of the quantum state texture. Considering a generic quench protocol, we analyze both standard formulations of the dynamical quantum phase transition. For type-I transitions, defined through the long-time behavior of an order parameter, we show that the time averaged rugosity, evaluated in the eigenbasis of the pre-quench Hamiltonian, acts itself as an order parameter, sharply distinguishing the dynamical phases. In the Lipkin-Meshkov-Glick model, this behavior is traced back to the underlying semiclassical structure, where the crossing of the excited-state quantum phase transition separatrix controls the redistribution of the state over the pre-quench energy basis. For type-II transitions, characterized by nonanalyticities in the Loschmidt rate function, we demonstrate that rugosity acquires a universal interpretation. For a suitable choice of basis, the rate function is exactly given by the density of rugosity, establishing a model-independent equivalence. Moreover, we show that even in physically motivated bases, such as the pre-quench energy eigenbasis, rugosity provides clear signatures of dynamical criticality. Our results place rugosity within a broader class of quantities diagnosing dynamical quantum phase transitions, including complexity and entropy production, while highlighting its distinct role as a measure of a basis-dependent quantum resource. This work provides an information-theoretic perspective on dynamical critical phenomena and opens new directions for exploring quantum texture in nonequilibrium many-body systems.

</details>

### [SpinTune: Improving the Reliability of Quantum Sensor Networks for Practical Quantum-Classical Utility](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04416v1)

**Authors:** Jason Ludmir, Nicholas S. DiBrita, Jason Han, Tirthak Patel  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04416v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Rydberg arrays` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper introduces SpinTune, an RL-based software framework that autonomously discovers optimal dynamical decoupling sequences for quantum sensors. By learning to adaptively combine different pulse types, it significantly enhances coherence and magnetic field sensitivity in the presence of complex noise. The approach was successfully validated on both simulated NV center environments and physical neutral-atom hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a powerful application of reinforcement learning to the control of open quantum systems, specifically for designing non-intuitive, aperiodic control protocols to mitigate complex, non-stationary environmental noise.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard dynamical decoupling (DD) sequences are suboptimal in realistic, complex noise environments, limiting the reliability and sensitivity of quantum sensors like NV centers.

**Main result.** The SpinTune RL framework discovers adaptive DD sequences that outperform standard protocols (CPMG, UDD) by 35% in coherence and 80% in magnetometry sensitivity, approaching the theoretical Oracle limit.

**Method.** A reinforcement learning (Q-learning) approach that treats DD design as a sequential decision-making problem, using a piecewise representation of pulse sequences and an efficient Fourier-transform-based coherence evaluation pipeline.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Nitrogen-Vacancy (NV) centers in diamond and neutral-atom systems (specifically QuEra's Aquila device), modeling decoherence from a Carbon-13 nuclear spin bath using a Gaussian noise spectral density.

**Key observables.** Quantum coherence (W), AC magnetometry sensitivity (eta), and the noise spectral density (S(omega)).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Evolution time (T) up to 200 microseconds, segment duration (delta t) of 4 microseconds, and noise parameters including amplitude, center frequency, and width.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Pulse changes are modeled as instantaneous (ignoring finite pulse slope), and the RL agent uses offline training to avoid the infeasibility of online learning during sensing.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the quantum-classical pipeline; Figure 2 compares modulation functions of standard DD sequences; Figure 3 shows coherence decay in various noise scenarios; Figure 4-5 detail the SpinTune RL architecture and decision logic; Figure 6-7 demonstrate improvements in sensitivity and coherence compared to baselines and the Oracle.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of decoherence in sensors, presents the SpinTune RL framework and its computational optimizations, evaluates performance via large-scale simulations against standard and Oracle baselines, and validates the approach using a physical neutral-atom hardware platform.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Emerging quantum sensors are increasingly envisioned as components of hybrid quantum-classical high-performance computing, enabling new capabilities in scientific, cyber-physical, and machine-learning pipelines. However, their practical utility is limited by environmental decoherence, which degrades sensing reliability. While dynamical decoupling (DD) pulse sequences can mitigate this, standard methods are often suboptimal in the presence of realistic noise. We present SpinTune, a reinforcement learning software approach that autonomously discovers adaptive, piecewise DD sequences tailored to specific environments. Using a simulation model of a Carbon-13 spin bath, we show that SpinTune significantly outperforms standard DD sequences in preserving coherence.

</details>

### [Nonequilibrium Fluctuation-Response Theory in the Frequency Domain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05038v1)

**Authors:** Euijoon Kwon, Hyun-Myung Chun, Hyunggyu Park, Jae Sung Lee  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05038v1>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a unified, exact fluctuation-response theory in the frequency domain for both continuous Langevin and discrete Markov jump systems in nonequilibrium steady states. It demonstrates that the power spectrum of any observable can be reconstructed from local linear responses, providing a single foundational identity from which several known thermodynamic uncertainty relations and dissipation relations can be derived.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a rigorous framework for understanding frequency-dependent fluctuations and dissipation, which is directly applicable to the study of open quantum systems and the dynamics of driven many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified, exact equality relating spontaneous fluctuations to responses in the frequency domain for nonequilibrium steady states.

**Main result.** The derivation of a unified fluctuation-response theory in the frequency domain that expresses the power spectrum of general observables as a quadratic form of local responses, serving as a parent relation for various uncertainty relations and dissipation relations.

**Method.** The authors use perturbation theory with local impulsive perturbations, functional derivatives, and the mathematical framework of the excess propagator (the projected resolvent of the Fokker-Planck or Master equation generator).

**Model / system.** The theory applies to two classes of nonequilibrium steady states: overdamped Langevin dynamics (continuous-state) and Markov jump processes (discrete-state stochastic networks).

**Key observables.** Power spectrum of general observables, including state-dependent parts, current-like parts, empirical density, and empirical currents.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Finite frequency (omega), nonequilibrium steady states (NESS), mobility (M), temperature (T), transition rates (W), and entropy production rate (sigma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The systems are assumed to be in nonequilibrium steady states, the dynamics are limited to overdamped Langevin or Markov jump processes, and the theory relies on linear response to small perturbations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a hierarchy diagram illustrating how the central frequency-domain FRR acts as a foundation for deriving various inequalities (RUR, TUR) and equalities (FDT, Harada-Sasa). Figure 5 illustrates the continuous interpolation between the covariance spectrum and the entropy production rate in a linear setting.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of frequency-domain FRRs, develops the unified theory for Langevin systems, extends the framework to Markov jump processes, derives various consequences (RUR, TUR, FDT, Harada-Sasa) using the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, and provides rigorous mathematical proofs in the appendix.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a unified fluctuation-response theory in the frequency domain for nonequilibrium steady states governed by overdamped Langevin dynamics and Markov jump processes. The relation expresses the power spectrum of general observables exactly as a quadratic form of local responses measured at the same frequency, thereby extending static nonequilibrium fluctuation-response relations to finite frequencies. The decomposition is spatial for Langevin systems and edge-resolved for Markov jump processes, and applies uniformly to state-dependent observables, current-like observables, and their combinations. As consequences of the same identity, we derive frequency-domain response uncertainty relations, kinetic and thermodynamic uncertainty relations, the equilibrium fluctuation-dissipation theorem, and Harada-Sasa-type relations. Applications to stochastic networks and driven diffusive systems illustrate how the theory resolves fluctuation spectra into edge-wise contributions and reveals frequency-dependent tradeoffs between fluctuations, response, and dissipation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04162"></a>
### [Boson Sampling with a reconfigurable 128 modes 3D integrated photonic circuit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04162v1)

**Authors:** Simone Di Micco, Francesco Hoch, Alessandro Ciorra, Daniel Carvalho de Salles, Nicolò Spagnolo, Taira Giordani, Gonzalo Carvacho, Niki Di Giano, Marco Gardina, Andrea Crespi, Francesco Ceccarelli, Roberto Osellame, Fabio Sciarrino  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04162v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper presents a new 128-mode 3D integrated photonic chip designed for large-scale quantum information processing. By implementing reconfigurable unitary transformations, the authors successfully performed Boson Sampling and demonstrated quantum-based randomness generation. This represents a significant step toward scalable, stable, and programmable photonic quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for quantum optics and quantum information as it demonstrates a scalable hardware platform for high-dimensional quantum computing tasks and complex photonic state manipulation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Scaling integrated photonic hardware to achieve high-dimensional unitary transformations required for quantum advantage tasks like Boson Sampling.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a reconfigurable 128-mode 3D integrated photonic circuit (Qolossus 3D) capable of performing Boson Sampling and generating randomness.

**Method.** The researchers utilized a 3D integrated photonic device with a continuously coupled architecture and thermo-optic programmability, using single photons from a quantum dot source.

**Model / system.** A 128-mode reconfigurable 3D integrated photonic circuit (Qolossus 3D) using a continuously coupled architecture and thermo-optic tuning.

**Key observables.** Output photon distributions for up to 4 photons and randomness generation metrics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 128 modes, up to 4 photons, 3D integrated architecture.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Indistinguishable single photons from a quantum dot source.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to integrated photonics, description of the Qolossus 3D architecture, implementation of Boson Sampling, demonstration of randomness generation, and validation against theoretical predictions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Integrated quantum photonics has emerged as one of the leading platforms for scaling quantum information processing, offering compact, stable, and low-loss hardware with precise phase and mode control. Advances in integrated photonics architectures and active programmability now enable complex, high-dimensional transformations essential for quantum advantage tasks. We introduce an integrated, reconfigurable 3D photonic device with 128 modes for manipulation of single-photon quantum states (Qolossus 3D). Leveraging a continuously coupled architecture and thermo-optic programmability, the platform implements reconfigurable unitary transformations at unprecedented scale for integrated quantum optics. Exploiting indistinguishable single photons demultiplexed from a quantum dot source, we perform Boson Sampling across the large-dimensional chip and analyse the resulting output distributions for up to 4 photons. We then exploit it to demonstrate randomness generation via Boson Sampling. Agreement with theoretical predictions validates both the device's reconfigurable operation and the generation of random numbers. Our results highlight the scalability, stability, and precise control of integrated photonics for quantum information processing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.05106"></a>
### [Kink-kink correlations in nonlinear quenches across a quantum critical point](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05106v1)

**Authors:** Lakshita Jindal, Kavita Jain  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05106v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.05106_figures/2605.05106_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.05106_figures/2605.05106_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2605.05106_figures/2605.05106_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how nonlinear quench protocols affect the spatial correlations of defects in the 1D Transverse Field Ising Model. It identifies that a new dephasing length scale is necessary to describe correlations in sublinear and linear quenches, and shows that the decay behavior follows a compressed exponential pattern.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides new insights into the non-equilibrium dynamics and universality of correlation functions beyond the standard Kibble-Zurek mechanism, which is highly relevant for understanding many-body dynamics and state preparation in quantum simulators.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the universality of kink-kink correlation functions in quantum systems undergoing nonlinear (algebraic) quenches across a second-order quantum critical point.

**Main result.** For superlinear quenches, the correlator depends only on the Kibble-Zurek length, whereas sublinear/linear quenches require an additional dephasing length; additionally, the dephased correlator decays as a compressed exponential with an exponent that varies with the quench exponent.

**Method.** The authors use adiabatic perturbation theory, the method of stationary phase, and saddle-point approximations for analytical results, alongside exact numerical integration of the Bogoli-de Gennes equations.

**Model / system.** The 1D Transverse Field Ising Model (TFIM) on a ring of N sites, where the transverse field g(t) varies algebraically near the critical point.

**Key observables.** Equal-time longitudinal kink-kink correlation function (dephased and off-diagonal components), excitation probability, and mean defect density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quench exponent (omega), quench time (tau), Kibble-Zurek length (xi-hat), and dephasing length (l).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is assumed to be in the slow-quench regime, and the initial state is the ground state of the initial Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the nonlinear quench protocol and the resulting kink-kink correlator decay; Figure 3 compares excitation probability numerical and analytical results; Figure 8 demonstrates data collapse using different length scales; Figure 10 shows the effect of waiting time on dephasing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the nonlinear quench protocol, applies the Jordan-Wigner and Bogoliubov transformations, uses adiabatic perturbation theory and stationary phase approximations to derive scaling laws, provides numerical verification, and discusses the implications for dephasing and optimization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

When a quantum system exhibiting a second order phase transition is quenched across the critical point in large but finite time, the dynamics are not adiabatic in the critical region and the Kibble-Zurek (KZ) mechanism provides a framework to determine local observables such as the mean defect density. However, to find higher-point functions, one has to go beyond the KZ paradigm asshown in recent works on one-dimensional transverse field Ising model (TFIM) following a linear quench. It has been found that (i) besides the KZ scale, the quench dynamics depend on another length scale that arises due to the finite phase difference between the low energy modes, and (ii) contrary to the expectations based on the KZ mechanism, in general, the correlation functions do not decay exponentially with distance. Motivated by these results for the linear quench, we are interested in understanding if these properties are universal, and consider the 1D TFIM when the transverse field varies algebraically in the vicinity of the critical field. We focus on the equal-time,longitudinal kink-kink correlation function at the end of the quench from the paramagnetic to the ferromagnetic phase, and find that (i) the correlator depends only on the KZ length for superlinear quenches, otherwise an additional dephasing length is required to describe it, and (ii) the dephased correlator decays as a compressed exponential with an exponent that changes continuously with the quench exponent. Our results are obtained using an adiabatic perturbation theory, analytical arguments and exact numerical integration of the relevant equations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04338"></a>
### [Robust certification of high-dimensional quantum devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04338v1)

**Authors:** Javier Fernández, Albert Rico, David Viedma, Evelyn A. Ortega, Valerio Pruneri, Adam Vallés, Verònica Ahufinger, Anna Sanpera, Some S. Bhattacharya  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04338v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper presents a new protocol for certifying quantum behavior in high-dimensional systems without needing entanglement or measurement incompatibility. Using the orbital angular momentum of single photons, the authors demonstrate a robust method that works even in noisy environments. This approach is significant for developing scalable and secure high-dimensional quantum communication networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a practical method for validating high-dimensional quantum communication systems and offers a way to certify quantumness without the usual requirements of entanglement or measurement incompatibility.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Certifying quantumness in high-dimensional prepare-and-measure scenarios is difficult due to the need for entanglement or measurement incompatibility and the presence of noise.

**Main result.** The authors implement a protocol that certifies quantumness between two parties without preshared resources or measurement incompatibility, demonstrating robustness via rank-stability analysis.

**Method.** The researchers propose and experimentally implement a protocol using rank-stability analysis of observed correlations to certify non-classicality in high-dimensional spaces.

**Model / system.** The experimental platform utilizes the orbital angular momentum (OAM) degrees of freedom of single photons to provide scalable high-dimensional Hilbert spaces.

**Key observables.** observed correlations and rank-stability analysis

**Important parameters / regimes.** high-dimensional Hilbert spaces, presence of noise

**Assumptions / limitations.** minimal prepare-and-measure scenario (sender encodes information, receiver performs a single measurement)

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of high-dimensional certification, proposes a new protocol, describes the experimental implementation using photon OAM, and demonstrates robustness through rank-stability analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Certifying quantum behavior from classically accessible data is essential for secure communication and scalable quantum technologies. While powerful certification methods such as Bell nonlocality and quantum steering exist, their implementation typically requires entanglement or additional assumptions, and experimental demonstrations mainly focus on low-dimensional systems. In minimal prepare-and-measure scenarios, where a sender encodes information into quantum states and a receiver performs a single measurement, robust certification becomes particularly challenging, especially in the presence of noise and in higher-dimensional Hilbert spaces. Here, we propose, design, and experimentally implement a protocol that certifies quantumness between two distant parties without the need for preshared resources or measurement incompatibility. The experiments are carried out using the orbital angular momentum degrees of freedom of single photons, chosen for providing increased dimensionality that is scalable. We demonstrate the robustness of the protocol through rank-stability analysis of the observed correlations, which enables the certification of non-classicality even in the presence of noise. Our results provide a practical route to validate high-dimensional quantum communication systems and open new possibilities for secure and dimension-efficient quantum information processing.

</details>

### [Seeing the forbidden: overcoming optical selection rules through nanophotonic integration](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04277v1)

**Authors:** Alex H. Rubin, Vytautas Žalandauskas, Pranta Saha, Rubek Poudel, Aurora Teien, Liam Hofmann, Nathan R. Gonzalez, Scott Dhuey, Marianne Etzelmüller Bathen, Marina Radulaski

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that integrating silicon carbide defects into nanopillars can overcome optical selection rules by generating a longitudinal electric field component. This allows researchers to observe 'dark' transitions and use nanophotonic structures as a symmetry-sensitive tool to identify the electronic structure of quantum defects. This approach provides a new way to characterize and manipulate spin defects for quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information because it demonstrates a method to control light-matter interaction at the nanoscale, enabling access to previously inaccessible quantum emitters and providing a tool for characterizing the symmetry of solid-state qubits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses how optical selection rules in bulk 4H-silicon carbide suppress certain defect transitions and seeks to use nanophotonic integration to overcome these rules and resolve the identity of ambiguous spectral lines.

**Main result.** Nanopillars act as polarization converters that generate a longitudinal electric field component, thereby 'unlocking' previously dark transitions like the PL3 divacancy and allowing for the symmetry-based identification of defect states.

**Method.** The researchers combined low-temperature photoluminescence spectroscopy and temperature-dependent measurements with FDTD simulations and first-principles DFT calculations using the r2SCAN functional.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of 4H-silicon carbide (4H-SiC) containing optically addressable spin defects, specifically neutral divacancies (VV0) and nitrogen-vacancy (NV-) centers, integrated into sub-wavelength dielectric nanopillars.

**Key observables.** Photoluminescence (PL) spectra, polarization-dependent emission, signal enhancement factors, and thermal quenching/activation energies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Excitation wavelength (785 nm and 852 nm), temperature range (1.6 K to 300 K), nanopillar diameters (300 nm to 1100 nm), and crystallographic configurations (axial vs. basal).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nanopillars were primarily optimized for photon collection efficiency rather than specific selection-rule engineering, and the $E_z$ component is treated as an incidental byproduct of the geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows SEM images and PL maps of nanopillars; Figure 2 and 3 compare PL spectra and transition dipole moments between bulk and nanopillar geometries; Figure 4 and supplementary figures show temperature-dependent PL and Arrhenius plots for extracting activation energies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of selection rules, describes the nanophotonic platform and experimental/computational methods, presents the activation of dark transitions via polarization transformation, uses symmetry analysis to identify specific defect states, and concludes with the utility of nanophotonics as a spectroscopic tool.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optically addressable spin defects in silicon carbide, including the neutral divacancy (VV$^0$) and the negative nitrogen-vacancy (NV$^-$), are among leading building blocks of solid-state quantum technologies. Integrating these defects into photonic structures such as nanopillars improves photon collection efficiency, but the consequences extend further. We show that the sub-wavelength geometry of nanopillars drastically modifies the local electromagnetic environment, providing optical access to defect transitions that are otherwise suppressed by selection rules in bulk material. Using low-temperature photoluminescence spectroscopy, we observe that emission from the PL3 divacancy, which is nearly absent in planar devices, becomes pronounced in nanopillars owing to a polarization transformation of the excitation field within the pillar. We further leverage the orientation-dependent collection of nanopillars to resolve the origin of previously ambiguous spectral lines. In particular, the NV4$'$ feature displays the signal enhancement expected for axially oriented NV$^-$ centres, consistent with assignment to a higher excited state of the $kh$ defect configuration. Our results establish nanophotonic integration as a symmetry-sensitive probe that can both activate nominally dark transitions and identify the dipole character of poorly understood defect states.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05109"></a>
### [Dimeric Perylene-Bisimide Organic Molecules: Fractional-Time Control of Quantum Resources](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05109v1)

**Authors:** Abdessamie Chhieb, Chaimae Banouni, Sliha Abdessamie  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05109v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**
