</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how fractional-time dynamics can control quantum resources in organic PBI molecular dimers. It demonstrates that the fractional order can suppress decoherence in highly entangled states and catalyze the creation of entanglement in weakly coherent states. This suggests that engineering memory effects in the environment is a viable strategy for robust quantum information processing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel perspective on using non-Markovianity and memory effects (via fractional calculus) as a practical tool for engineering and stabilizing quantum correlations in open molecular systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how fractional-time dynamics and memory effects can be used to control, preserve, and generate quantum resources like coherence, entanglement, and nonlocality in molecular dimers.

**Main result.** Decreasing the fractional order (tau) slows decoherence for strongly correlated states and acts as a catalyst for generating correlations in weakly coherent states, suggesting fractional dynamics as a control mechanism.

**Method.** The study employs the time-fractional Schrödinger equation with Caputo fractional derivatives and utilizes Mittag-Leffler functions to model non-Markovian, power-law relaxation dynamics.

**Model / system.** A dimeric arrangement of organic Perylene-Bisimide (PBI) molecules acting as two-level emitters, coupled via long-range dipole-dipole interactions within an effective Hamiltonian framework.

**Key observables.** Relative entropy of coherence, logarithmic negativity, concurrence, and CHSH inequality violation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fractional order (tau), transition frequencies (nu), dipole-dipable interaction strength (V12), detuning (Delta), and initial state purity (p).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes identical emitters with parallel dipole moments, neglects external driving fields, and uses the Born-Markov approximation for the initial effective Hamiltonian derivation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the molecular dimer schematic; Figure 2 compares fractional vs. standard evolution; Figure 3 shows temporal evolution of resources for various tau values; Figure 4 and 5 illustrate the dependence of resources on transition frequency and detuning.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the molecular system and the fractional-time framework, presents the mathematical solution using Mittag-Leffler functions, analyzes the impact of the fractional order on decoherence and resource generation, and explores the influence of frequency detuning and interaction strength.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we explore the dynamics of quantum correlations, namely coherence, entanglement, and nonlocality associated with a Bell state, in a dimeric arrangement of organic PBI molecules, mediated by dipole-dipole interactions, under time-fractional dynamics. Within the framework of the time-fractional Schrödinger equation (TFSE) with Caputo fractional derivatives, we explore system dynamics for different values of the fractional order $τ$, transition energies, interaction strength, and purity $p$. We employ the relative entropy of coherence, logarithmic entanglement entropy and concurrence, and CHSH inequality to estimate system dynamics associated with coherence, entanglement, and nonlocality, respectively. These findings highlight the role of the fractional order $τ$ in system dynamics, including memory effects and relaxation, and thereby bring together ideas from fractional calculus and quantum information theory perspectives and discuss methodologies to control and utilize these molecular quantum correlations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04546"></a>
### [Measurement-Device-Independent Entanglement Quantification in a Fully Connected Time-Bin Quantum Network](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04546v1)

**Authors:** Lu Liu, Ling-Xuan Kong, Ze-Yang Lu, Xu-Jie Peng, Xiao-Xu Fang, He Lu  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04546v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<sub>Figure 3. Time-shift attack on entanglement witness measurement. (a) Schematic illustration of the time-shift attack. In the absence of manipulation, photons at nodes u and v produce valid coincidence events within the selected time window. Under attack, a temporal delay of ∼5 ns is introduced on the photon at node v, while the coincidence window remains fixed. As a result, coincidence events fall outside the window and are not registered, leading to biased correlation estimates. (b) Measured entanglement witness values ⟨W⟩for the separable state |euev⟩for all six user pairs. The dashed line at F = 0.5 indicates the classical limit [16, 29]. (b) Measured entanglement wit- ness values for the same links. The dashed line at ⟨W⟩= 0 marks the entanglement bound.</sub>

<sub>Figure 4. Results of MDI entanglement Verification and quantification for the time-bin entangled states |Φ(θ)⟩. (a) The measured MDI witness values I(ρu,v) for all six links. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates a way to verify and measure entanglement in a multi-user quantum network without needing to trust the measurement hardware. By using polarization to encode trusted information, the researchers successfully protected the network against time-shift attacks across 20 km of fiber.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information as it provides a practical solution for scalable, secure entanglement distribution in fiber-based networks, addressing a critical security loophole in quantum communication.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Reliable entanglement characterization in large-scale quantum networks is difficult because conventional entanglement witnesses are vulnerable to measurement-device attacks, such as time-shift attacks, which can falsely certify entanglement.

**Main result.** The authors experimentally demonstrated a measurement-device-independent (MDI) protocol for entanglement verification and quantification in a four-user, six-link fully connected time-bin quantum network, maintaining high fidelity over 20-km fiber channels.

**Method.** The researchers used a broadband PPLNOI waveguide source with wavelength-division multiplexing to distribute entangled photons and implemented an MDI approach by encoding trusted input states in the polarization degree of freedom to eliminate reliance on measurement device characterization.

**Model / system.** A four-user fully connected quantum network (Alice, Bob, Chloe, and David) using time-bin encoded photons generated via type-0 spontaneous parametric down-conversion in a periodically poled lithium niobate on insulator (PPLNOI) waveguide.

**Key observables.** Entanglement fidelity (F), entanglement witness values (W and MDI witness I), trace-distance entanglement measure (E_Tr), and coincidence-to-accidental ratio (CAR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** 20-km total fiber distribution, 100 MHz repetition rate, 1126 nm SPDC bandwidth, and 5 ns temporal delay for attack simulation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes that the input states prepared in the polarization degree of freedom are trusted, even if the measurement devices are untrusted.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the network architecture and source schematic; Figure 3 illustrates the time-shift attack mechanism and the failure of conventional witnesses; Figure 4 details the wavelength-multiplexed distribution and the MDI verification results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of measurement vulnerabilities, describes the PPLNOI source and network architecture, demonstrates the vulnerability of conventional witnesses to time-shift attacks, presents the MDI protocol implementation, and concludes with the experimental verification and quantification of entanglement across all network links.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fully connected quantum networks enable scalable quantum communication, yet reliable entanglement characterization without trusting measurement devices remains challenging. Here we experimentally demonstrate measurement-device-independent (MDI) entanglement verification and quantification in a time-bin-encoded fully connected quantum network. Using a broadband periodically poled lithium niobate on insulator source combined with dense wavelength-division multiplexing, we distribute all six pairwise entangled links among four users over 20-km fiber channels, preserving high-fidelity entanglement without active stabilization of the long-distance fiber links. We show that conventional entanglement witnesses can fail under untrusted measurement conditions. By encoding trusted input states in the polarization degree of freedom of the same photons, we realize MDI measurements without ancillary photons or additional experimental resources. Both entanglement verification and quantification are obtained from the same measurement dataset. Our results establish a practical and scalable approach for reliable entanglement characterization in quantum networks.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [The unique, universal entropy for complex systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04493v1)

**Summary.** The paper establishes a new axiomatic foundation for entropy in complex systems, proposing a 'coupled entropy' that uniquely measures uncertainty at a consistent informational scale. It demonstrates that this entropy is universal across various scaling classes and corrects misalignments found in previous models like Tsallis statistics. This provides a more robust tool for modeling non-equilibrium phenomena in complex adaptive systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a new framework for quantifying uncertainty and entropy in non-equilibrium systems characterized by long-range correlations and heavy-tailed distributions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of an axiomatic foundation for entropy in complex, non-equilibrium systems and the failure of existing generalized entropies (like Tsallis or Renyi) to maintain a consistent informational scale and extensivity.

**Main result.** The derivation of a unique, universal 'coupled entropy' that satisfies new axioms regarding uncertainty measurement at the informational scale and covers the full Hanel-Thurner universality scaling classes.

**Method.** An axiomatic derivation using a generalized algebraic framework (pseudo-algebra) and information geometry to establish requirements for entropy in the Coupled Exponential Family (CEF).

**Model / system.** The Coupled Exponential Family (CEF), specifically the Coupled Stretched Exponential Distribution (CSED), which models complex adaptive systems with both short-range (stretching) and long-range (coupling) dependencies.

**Key observables.** Entropy (trace and non-trace forms), partition function (Z), and the informational scale (sigma).

**Important parameters / regimes.** kappa (coupling/long-range dependence), alpha (straining/short-range dependence), sigma (informational scale), and d (dimensionality).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the existence of a coupled exponential family derived from nonlinear differential equations and assumes statistical independence for the composition axiom.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares normalization behavior in the Coupled Gaussian; Figure 2 shows multiplicative noise processes; Figure 3 compares score derivatives for q-exponential vs. coupled distributions; Figure 4 maps various entropies onto the density curve; Figure 5 shows entropy convergence as coupling increases; Figure 6 demonstrates entropy scaling with respect to scale and coupling.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying the gap in existing entropy axioms, introduces the Coupled Exponential Family and its mathematical framework, provides an axiomatic derivation of the new entropy, proves its uniqueness and universality across scaling classes, and concludes with applications to information thermodynamics and complexity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

An axiomatic foundation regarding the entropy for complex systems is established. Missing from decades of research was the requirement that entropy must measure the uncertainty at the informational scale of the maximizing distribution, where the log-log slope equals $-1$. Additionally, entropy must be extensive across the full universality scaling classes defined by Hanel-Thurner. The coupled entropy, maximized by the coupled stretched exponential distributions, is proven to be the unique, universal entropy that satisfies these requirements. The non-additivity of the entropy is equal to the long-range dependence or nonlinear statistical coupling. The entropy-matched extensivity is a function of the coupling, stretching parameter, and dimensions. Evidence is provided that the Tsallis $q$-statistics creates misalignment in the physical modeling of complex systems. Information thermodynamic applications are reviewed, including measuring complexity, a zeroth law of temperature, the thermodynamic consistency of the coupled free energy, and a model of intelligence in non-equilibrium.

</details>

### [Transit Noise in Spin Squeezing Experiments with Coated Rubidium Vapor Cell](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04914v1)

**Authors:** Yujie Ji, Peiying Li, Yanhong Xiao, Yuzhuo Wang, Junlei Duan  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04914v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04914_figures/2605.04914_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>Figure 3. Atomic spin noise spectra at different Larmor frequencies and probe beam sizes. Experiment (left panels) for Larmor frequencies of (a) 30 kHz; (b) 100 kHz; (c) 500 kHz. Monte Carlo simulation (right panels) for Larmor frequencies of (d) 30 kHz; (e) 100 kHz; (f) 500 kHz.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 4. Variation in atomic transit noise with Larmor frequency in experiments.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04914_figures/2605.04914_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Calculated and measured spin squeezing at Larmor frequency of 500 kHz for different probe beam diameters. Both the experimental and theoretical values of the effective atom–light interaction strength κ are 1.61 ms−1/2, with total laser power fixed. Here, the squeezing was evaluated using a retrodictive estimator based on the past quantum state techniques, which improved the inference of the conditional atomic state from the measurement record. </details>

**Summary.** This paper quantifies how the motion of atoms through a Gaussian probe beam creates transit noise that degrades spin squeezing in rubidium vapor cells. By comparing different beam sizes and Larmor frequencies, the authors provide practical guidelines for optimizing probe parameters to reach the standard quantum limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics and open quantum systems as it quantifies a fundamental decoherence-like mechanism (transit noise) that limits the precision of QND-based quantum metrology.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how transit noise, caused by atoms moving through the inhomogeneous intensity profile of a Gaussian probe beam, limits the performance of spin squeezing in coated rubidium vapor cells.

**Main result.** The researchers found that transit noise increases as the probe beam area decreases and becomes more significant when the Larmor frequency is below the noise linewidth; experimental squeezing degradation matched theoretical predictions.

**Method.** The work combines Monte Carlo simulations of atomic trajectories and collision models with experimental measurements of atomic spin noise spectra and spin squeezing at varying beam diameters and Larmor frequencies.

**Model / system.** The system consists of 87Rb atoms in a coated vapor cell (3x3x20 mm) at 58 degrees Celsius, using a Gaussian probe beam and a Faraday interaction Hamiltonian to perform QND measurements.

**Key observables.** Spin squeezing (in dB), atomic spin noise spectra, Faraday rotation signal, and atom-light coupling strength (kappa).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Probe beam diameter (0.6 mm to 2 mm), Larmor frequency (30 kHz to 500 kHz), and atom-light coupling strength (kappa).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model uses the Holstein-Primakoff approximation, neglects the tensor and scalar terms of the Hamiltonian, and approximates the rectangular cell as a cylindrical one in simulations.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of atomic motion, plots of spin squeezing vs. beam size, atomic spin noise spectra comparing Gaussian and Tophat beams, and theoretical studies of noise at high coupling strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the transit noise problem, describes the experimental setup and theoretical framework (Monte Carlo and Hamiltonian), presents results on noise scaling and Larmor frequency dependence, and concludes with implications for optimizing probe parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin squeezing can suppress quantum projection noise via interparticle entanglement, therefore enabling measurement sensitivities beyond the standard quantum limit. In practice, however, the Gaussian and finite intensity profiles of the optical probe beam induce spatially inhomogeneous atom-light interactions. As polarized atoms move within a vapor cell, they experience position-dependent optical intensities, generating transit noise that limits spin squeezing performance. Here, we investigate the transit noise in a coated rubidium vapor cell through combined theoretical analysis and experimental measurements. By varying the probe beam diameter, we quantify the dependence of transit noise on beam size and atomic Larmor frequency. Our results show that, for a vapor cell with fixed dimensions, the transit noise increases as the probe beam spot area decreases. Moreover, when the Larmor frequency is below the characteristic linewidth of the transit noise, the noise contribution becomes larger. We further calculated and measured spin squeezing for different beam sizes and found an experimental difference of $2.7 \pm 0.2$ dB between 2~mm and 0.6~mm, similar to the theoretical prediction of $3.0 \pm 0.3$ dB. Theoretical analysis under conditions of stronger squeezing shows that transit noise becomes an even more critical limiting factor. These results provide practical guidance for optimizing probe beam parameters and suppressing transit noise in spin squeezing experiments.

</details>

### [Unconditional Authentication in Quantum Key Distribution via Hybrid Entangled Physical Unclonable Functions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04650v1)

**Authors:** Nicolas Laurent-Puig, Mina Doosti, Adriano Innocenzi, Eleni Diamanti  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04650v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<sub>Figure 1. Experimental setup. A Ti:Sapphire laser (Coherent Mira-HP) with an average power of 3.4 W emits 2 ps pulses at a wavelength of 775 nm with a repetition rate of 76 MHz. The spatial mode of the laser is shaped into a Gaussian pro- file. The light entering the Sagnac interferometer is diagonally polarized to maximize the fidelity of output states with respect to the Bell state. Photon pairs are probabilistically generated via type-II Spontaneous Parametric Down Conversion in a pp- KTP crystal (30mm-long, 46.2 µm poling period, provided by Raicol) and entangled in polarization in the Sagnac loop, re- sulting in the output state p|Hys |V yi ` eiθ |V ys |Hyiq{ ?</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04650_figures/2605.04650_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Experimental performance of the HEPUF- authenticated QKD protocol. Upper: Evolution of QBER as a function of the raw-key length for attenuation levels of 30, 40, and 50 dB. Solid lines represent Scenario 1, while dashed lines represent Scenario 2. Bottom: SKR in bits per second versus channel attenuation. Blue markers denote Scenario 1 and red markers denote Scenario 2. <img src="2605.04650_figures/2605.04650_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Experimental density matrix of the state produced with our source, estimated via Quantum State Tomography, at 300 kHz. The fidelity with respect to the Bell state is 99.17 ˘ 0.14 % for an acquisition time of 10 s per basis.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a method to achieve unconditional authentication in QKD without needing pre-shared secret keys by using a Hybrid Entangled Physical Unclonable Function. By leveraging the unique physical disorder of hardware, the authors experimentally demonstrate a secure, entanglement-based QKD protocol that remains robust under high channel attenuation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum networks as it demonstrates a practical method to implement secure communication protocols using lightweight hardware primitives, bridging the gap between physical hardware properties and information-theoretic security.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the 'chicken-and-egg' problem in QKD where an authenticated classical channel is required to prevent Man-in-the-Middle attacks, which typically necessitates a pre-shared secret key.

**Main result.** The first experimental realization of a fully information-theoretically secure (ITS) authenticated entanglement-based QKD protocol using a Hybrid Entangled Physical Unclonable Function (HEPUF), achieving low QBER even at 50 dB attenuation.

**Method.** The researchers integrated a hardware-based HEPUF protocol to generate an initial secret key, which is then used for either secret basis selection (Scenario 1) or Wegman-Carter authentication (Scenario 2) in an entanglement-based QKD setup.

**Model / system.** An experimental platform featuring a high-fidelity polarization-entangled Bell-state source produced via Type-II Spagnac-configuration SPDC in a ppKTP crystal, integrated with a Hybrid PUF architecture for authentication.

**Key observables.** Quantum Bit Error Rate (QBER), Bell-state fidelity, Secret Key Rate (SKR), and security parameter (epsilon).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Attenuation levels of 30, 40, and 50 dB; Bell-state fidelity of 99.17%; HEPUF security parameter epsilon < 2.5e-11; experimental bias delta of 0.0016.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Relies on the physical unclonability of the hardware-based HEPUF and the existence of a device with unique, stochastic manufacturing disorder.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup schematic; Figure 2 displays the evolution of QBER and the comparison of SKR vs. attenuation for two scenarios; Figure 3 presents the experimental density matrix via quantum state tomography.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the authentication bottleneck in QKD, describes the HEPUF architecture and the two proposed QKD integration scenarios, details the experimental setup and hardware, presents the security analysis and finite-key rate optimization, and concludes with experimental validation across various attenuation regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Key Distribution (QKD) enables Information-Theoretically Secure (ITS) key exchange, robust even against future quantum computing threats. However, a fundamental limitation of QKD is the requirement for an authenticated classical channel, which necessitates a pre-shared secret key. In this work, we address this challenge by adopting a Hybrid Entangled Physical Unclonable Function (PUF) protocol for authentication. We demonstrate that this PUF-based method generates an ITS initial key under minimal explicit hardware assumptions. This approach allows us to experimentally perform a fully ITS-authenticated entanglement-based QKD protocol that relies solely on such assumptions, effectively eliminating the need for pre-shared secrets. This represents a significant step towards the practical realization of quantum network protocols using lightweight, readily available hardware assumptions, without weakening security guarantees.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05015"></a>
### [Dephasing Effects on the Dynamical Evolution of Quantum Correlations and Coherence in Neutrino Oscillations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05015v1)

**Authors:** Omar Bachain, Elhabib Jaloum, Mohamed Amazioug, Nazek Alessa, Wedad R. Alharbi, Rachid Ahl Laamara, Abdel-Haleem Abdel-Aty  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05015v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Fig. 1(a) shows that quantum steering increases monotonically with the parameter ϕ, reaching its maximum at ϕ = π/2, which corresponds to optimal quantum correlations. Beyond this point, the steering decreases symmetrically and vanishes at ϕ = π, reflecting the oscillatory dependence of quantum correlations on ϕ. The different peak amplitudes observed across the experiments highlight the influence of their specific parameters on the strength of quantum steering. As illustrated in Fig. 1(b), the logarithmic negativity LN varies with the phase ϕ for the KamLAND, MINOS, Daya Bay, T2K, and JUNO experiments. For all cases, LN initially increases with ϕ, reaching a peak at intermediate values....</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.05015_figures/2605.05015_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2605.05015_figures/2605.05015_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Plot of the quantum steering SA→B (a), logarithmic negativity LN (b), and quantum coherence Cl1 (c) against τ under PF channel for various experiments presented in Table ??.</sub>

<img src="2605.05015_figures/2605.05015_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Plot of the quantum steering SA→B (a), logarithmic negativity LN (b), and quantum coherence Cl1 (c) versus τ under PD channel for various experiments presented in Table ??.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores the degradation of quantum correlations in neutrino oscillations due to environmental decoherence. It identifies a clear hierarchy of robustness among quantum steering, entanglement, and coherence and demonstrates how non-Markovian memory effects can lead to the revival of certain quantum resources.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a detailed study of how different types of environmental noise (dissipative vs. dephasing) and memory effects (non-Markovianity) affect a hierarchy of quantum correlations, which is directly applicable to open quantum systems and quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how environmental decoherence and non-Markovian memory effects impact the dynamical evolution and robustness of quantum resources (steering, entanglement, and coherence) in neutrino oscillation systems.

**Main result.** The study establishes a hierarchy of robustness where quantum steering is the most fragile, followed by entanglement, while quantum coherence is the most resilient. Non-Markovian effects induce partial revivals of entanglement and coherence through information backflow, though steering remains largely suppressed.

**Method.** The authors use an open quantum system approach with the Kraus operator formalism to model three noise channels (Amplitude Damping, Phase Flip, and Phase Damping) and compare Markovian and non-Markovian dynamics.

**Model / system.** A two-flavor neutrino oscillation framework is modeled as an effective bipartite two-qubit system using an occupation number representation and the PMNS mixing matrix.

**Key observables.** Quantum steering (S_A to B, S_B to A, and steering asymmetry), Logarithmic Negativity (entanglement), and l1-norm of quantum coherence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Oscillation phase (phi), decoherence strength (tau), mass-squared difference (delta m^2), mixing angle (theta), and environmental correlation time (chi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a two-flavor approximation, the ultra-relativistic limit, and that neutrinos are produced with equal momentum.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the oscillatory dependence of quantum resources on oscillation phase, the monotonic decay of resources under amplitude damping, and the symmetric decay-revival dynamics and linear decay under phase flip and phase damping channels across different experimental setups (KamLAND, Daya Bay, JUNO).

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by mapping neutrino oscillations to a two-qubit system, then derives analytical expressions for quantum resources under specific noise channels, followed by a comparative analysis of different noise models (AD, PF, PD) and dynamical regimes (Markovian vs. non-Markovian), and concludes with an experimental comparison using data from major neutrino collaborations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neutrino oscillations confirm the presence of mode entanglement, as each flavor eigenstate is composed of a coherent superposition of distinct mass eigenstates. In this work, we investigate the dynamics of quantum resources in neutrino oscillation systems by analyzing quantum steering, logarithmic negativity, and quantum coherence within a two-flavor framework. Treating neutrino oscillations as an effective two-level quantum system, we study the influence of environmental decoherence on these nonclassical features by modeling the system as an open quantum system. Three representative noise channels are considered, namely amplitude damping (AD), phase flip (PF), and phase damping (PD), allowing us to capture both dissipative and dephasing mechanisms. We examine the evolution of quantum resources in both Markovian and non-Markovian regimes, highlighting the role of memory effects in the system-environment interaction. The results reveal a clear hierarchy in the robustness of quantum resources under decoherence. Steering is the most sensitive correlation in the hierarchy under decoherence effects. while logarithmic negativity exhibits intermediate robustness. Quantum coherence displays the highest resilience, persisting over a wider range of parameters. In the PF and PD channels, logarithmic negativity and coherence are shown to exhibit identical dynamical behavior, reflecting their common dependence on phase-related noise. In contrast, the non-Markovian regime leads to delayed decoherence and partial revivals of entanglement and coherence due to information backflow, whereas quantum steering remains strongly suppressed. These findings provide a comparison of different quantum resources in neutrino oscillation systems and offer new insights into the interplay between decoherence mechanisms and quantum correlations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04823"></a>
### [Expectation values after an integrable boundary quantum quench](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04823v1)

**Authors:** Zoltán Bajnok, Dávid Fülepi, Máté Lencsés  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04823v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04823_figures/2605.04823_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. We work on the strip of width L, boundary condition α or β on the left and change the right boundary condition from α to β by inserting ψβα at t = 0, and monitor the effect of the change in the bulk by inserting the operator Φ(x, t) after the change.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04823_figures/2605.04823_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. By mapping the strip to the upper half plane we arrive at the arrangements of operators.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3. 3D plot of the correlation functions ⟨¯ψIϕ|Φ(z, ¯z)ψϕI(1)|0⟩ (left) and ⟨0|Φ(z, ¯z)ψϕI(1)ψIϕ(0)|0⟩(on the right). We can recognize the change in the boundary condition at z = 1 and at z = 0, 1 respectively.</sub>

<sub>Figure 4. The correlator of the bulk field after the boundary quench in the Minkowski strip. The volume is L = 10 and we focused on the −1 ≤x ≤0 and 0 &lt; t &lt; 1 domain. Quenching from II to Iϕ is on the left, while from Iϕ to II is on the right.</sub>

<img src="2605.04823_figures/2605.04823_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Space dependence of the amplitude for different times for the quench Iϕ →II in volume L = 10. The light-cone singularity is visible for x = −t, as expected.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a mathematical framework to calculate the real-time evolution of bulk operators after an integrable boundary quench. By studying the Lee-Yang model, the authors demonstrate how particle interference leads to specific decay laws and provide a method to reconcile numerical TCSA results with continuum field theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into non-equilibrium dynamics and relaxation in integrable systems, specifically how boundary-induced excitations propagate and interfere, which is highly relevant to many-body dynamics and the study of quench-induced steady states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the real-time dynamics and expectation values of bulk fields following an integrable boundary quantum quench, where one boundary condition is suddenly switched to another.

**Main result.** The authors develop a general framework using form factor expansions to calculate post-quench vacuum-to-vacuum matrix elements and identify a modified $t^{-1}$ decay law due to destructive interference in the massive theory.

**Method.** The study employs a double form-factor expansion approach, conformal mapping to the Upper Half Plane, and the Truncated Conformal Space Approach (TCSA) for numerical validation and renormalization.

**Model / system.** The primary model used is the (1+1)-dimensional Lee-Yang model, covering both its Conformal Field Theory (CFT) point and its massive integrable perturbation on a strip or semi-infinite plane.

**Key observables.** Expectation values (VEVs) of local bulk fields, correlation functions, and the time evolution of the vacuum amplitude.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Boundary conditions (identity and primary field), strip width $L$, time $t$, spatial position $x$, and the mass scale $m$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to integrable boundary conditions and integrable bulk perturbations, and assumes the left boundary condition remains fixed or follows specific simplified scenarios.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the physical setup and geometry mapping, 3D plots of correlation functions, energy spectra from TCSA, and comparisons between analytical predictions and numerical data including light-cone effects.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the boundary quench protocol and conformal analysis to developing the massive theory framework via form factors, followed by numerical validation using TCSA and error analysis/renormalization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate an integrable boundary quench, in which one integrable boundary condition is suddenly switched to another. We develop a general framework for analyzing the resulting real-time dynamics based on form factors of bulk and boundary-changing operators. We first study the problem at the conformal point of the Lee-Yang model and then extend the analysis to its massive perturbation, where we examine the time evolution of the pre-quench vacuum and compute the vacuum-to-vacuum matrix elements of local operators inserted after the quench. The analytical results are validated by numerical calculations using the truncated conformal space approach adapted to boundary-changing situations.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a successful implementation of a quantum machine learning algorithm for graph classification using a 256-qubit neutral atom simulator. By optimizing the quantum pulse sequence via Bayesian optimization, the researchers achieved performance that exceeds classical benchmarks on protein datasets. The work highlights the potential of using programmable Rydberg atom arrays for real-world quantum computing tasks despite current hardware noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a practical application of Rydberg-based quantum simulators for complex tasks like graph classification and shows how pulse optimization can mitigate hardware noise in many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Implementing Quantum Machine Learning (QML) for graph classification on real-world hardware, specifically overcoming the constraints of noise and limited connectivity in neutral atom simulators.

**Main result.** The optimized Quantum Evolution Kernel (QEK) on the 256-qubit Aquila platform achieved an F1 score of 46.3% on the PROTEINS12 dataset, outperforming both classical Shortest Path Kernels and unoptimized quantum protocols.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical pipeline using a Quantum Evolution Kernel (QEK) based on Jensen-Shannon divergence, optimized via Bayesian Optimization to find the best pulse sequence parameters.

**Model / system.** A 256-qubit neutral atom simulator (QuEra Aquila) using Rubidium atoms trapped in optical tweezers, where qubits are defined by Rydberg and ground states and interactions are governed by the Rydberg blockade and an Ising Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Energy distributions derived from atom measurements and the resulting probability distributions used for kernel computation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pulse parameters (Rabi frequency, detuning, and duration), kernel scaling factor (mu), and the number of measurement shots.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Graphs must be representable as Unit Disk Graphs to fit the physical connectivity of the atom array; the study assumes the effectiveness of the DEN model for graph embedding.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the layered time evolution of the Hamiltonian; Figure 2 shows dataset statistics; Figure 3 illustrates graph embedding onto the register; Figure 4 is a flowchart of the hybrid approach; Figure 5 compares energy distributions; Figure 6 shows a kernel matrix heatmap; Figure 7 compares optimized vs. non-optimized waveforms; Figure 8 compares energy distributions under different parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QML problem for graph classification, details the physical platform and hardware constraints, describes the graph embedding and QEK algorithm, presents the Bayesian optimization of waveforms, and concludes with performance benchmarking against classical methods and noise analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neutral atom platforms are analogue quantum simulators that offer the possibility to map graphs onto a 2D qubit register using programmable Rubidium atoms arrays, whose valence electrons' energy state is used as qubits, using optical tweezers. This makes it possible to implement algorithms for solving graph combinatorial optimization and Quantum Machine Learning (QML) tasks, such as graph classification. However, the restrictions of real hardware, as well as the very low number of publicly available machines, make such implementation non-trivial. In this work, we manage to compute the Quantum Evolution Kernel (QEK) to extract the features from graphs of the PROTEINS dataset using the 256-qubits Aquila platform (available through AWS) and then we apply classical Machine Learning (ML) techniques for the final classification. The method is benchmarked against classical kernels, resulting in slightly better performance, proving the effectiveness of the method, even in the case of a noisy quantum simulator.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04155"></a>
### [Nonstabilizerness Mpemba Effects](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04155v1)

**Authors:** Zhenyu Xiao, Hao-Kai Zhang, Shuo Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04155v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04155_figures/2605.04155_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Schematic of the U(1)-symmetric brickwork ran- dom circuit. Each time step consists of two layers of nearest- neighbor two-qubit gates with periodic boundary conditions. (b),(c) Schematic illustration of magic dynamics without and with an inverse Mpemba effect, respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04155_figures/2605.04155_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Initial and late-time second stabilizer R´enyi entropy densities. (a) Initial value M2(0)/N, which is identical for the families of tilted ferromagnetic states (TFS), tilted N´eel states (TNS), and tilted domain-wall states (TDWS). (b) Late-time global-U(1) Haar prediction for M (∞) 2 /N for TFS (solid lines) and TNS (dashed lines); TDWS coincides with TNS in this limit. Colors indicate system sizes N = 12, 16, 20, 24.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04155_figures/2605.04155_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Magic dynamics under U(1)-symmetric circuit evolution [(a)–(c)] and mixed-field Ising Hamiltonian (MFIM) evolution [(d)] for N = 20 and several tilt angles θ ∈[π/4, π/2]. Panels (a) and (c), corresponding to tilted ferromagnetic states (TFS) and tilted domain-wall states (TDWS), respectively, exhibit inverse-Mpemba crossings, whereas panel (b), corresponding to tilted N´eel states (TNS), shows no inverse Mpemba effect. (d) In contrast to the U(1)-symmetric circuit dynamics in (b), the same TNS initial states under MFIM evolution exhibit Mpemba-type magic dynamics.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper identifies a quantum Mpemba effect where the generation of nonstabilizerness (magic) follows counterintuitive trajectories, where less magical states can become more magical than their counterparts over time. This phenomenon is driven by the spatial arrangement of initial charges and is a general feature of symmetric and non-integrable quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It connects the resource theory of magic with non-equilibrium statistical mechanics, showing how symmetry and spatial configuration can lead to anomalous relaxation phenomena in many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the existence of a quantum Mpemba effect in the dynamical generation of nonstabilizerness (magic), specifically whether states with lower initial magic can overtake states with higher initial magic during evolution.

**Main result.** The authors discover an inverse Mpemba effect where magic growth trajectories cross, driven by the interplay between charge conservation and the spatial structure of initial states. This effect is shown to be robust across U(1) and SU(2) symmetric random circuits as well as non-integrable Hamiltonian dynamics.

**Method.** The study uses analytical derivations for late-time steady-state magic and numerical simulations of random circuits and the mixed-field Ising model to track early-time dynamics.

**Model / system.** The study examines U(1)-symmetric and SU(2)-symmetric brickwork random circuits, as well as the non-integrable mixed-field Ising model, using various tilted product state initial conditions.

**Key observables.** Second stabilizer Renyi entropy (M2), which quantifies nonstabilizerness or magic.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Tilt angle (theta), system size (N), time (t), and charge-sector distribution p(q).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The equivalence of certain state families is treated as an asymptotic property of the steady-state limit where intra-sector scrambling is complete.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the circuit structure and the concept of the Mpemba crossing; Figure 2 shows the evolution of magic for different initial states; Supplemental figures show dynamics in SU(2) circuits and the Ising model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the quantum Mpemba effect in magic generation, provides analytical derivations for steady-state magic in U(1) circuits, explores the role of spatial structure via TNS and TDWS states, extends the findings to SU(2) and non-integrable Hamiltonian systems, and concludes with a detailed mathematical appendix.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum state preparation can be strikingly counterintuitive: the fastest route to a target state need not start from the apparently closest initial condition. We uncover such a quantum Mpemba effect in the dynamical generation of quantum magic (nonstabilizerness), quantified by the stabilizer Rényi entropy, in $\mathrm{U(1)}$-symmetric random circuits initialized from tilted product states. States with lower initial magic can generate magic faster than states with higher initial magic. The acceleration is not determined solely by the conserved-charge distribution. Two initial-state families with identical initial magic and identical charge distribution exhibit qualitatively different magic-growth dynamics, depending also on the spatial structure of the initial state within each charge sector. Analogous magic Mpemba effects in $\mathrm{SU(2)}$-symmetric circuits and under nonintegrable Hamiltonian dynamics further show that the phenomenon is tied neither to Abelian symmetry nor to random-circuit dynamics, establishing quantum magic as a distinct arena for Mpemba physics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.05111"></a>
### [Time-Dependent Dynamical Dimensional Transmutation in the $SU(2)$ Gross-Neveu Model with Time-Dependent Interaction Strength](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05111v1)

**Authors:** Parameshwar R. Pasnoori  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05111v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.05111_figures/2605.05111_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Figure depicts adiabatic regime and fast driving regime of the integrability preserving RG protocol. In the RG protocol, the time-dependent coupling strengths are cho- sen such that their trajectories in time are same as that of the RG trajectories of the corresponding static model. In the adiabatic regime, which identifies with the scaling regime of the static SU(2) Gross-Neveu model, the system dynamically generates a time-dependent mass gap m(t). In the fast driv- ing regime, the coupling strength decreases at a much faster rate and the system becomes asymptotically free reaching the UV fixed point, which is the SU(2)1 WZNW model.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that a time-dependent SU(2) Gross-Neveu model can be solved exactly if its coupling strength evolves according to the Renormalization Group flow. It demonstrates that time evolution in this model mimics the change in energy scale, leading to a dynamically generated, time-dependent mass gap.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a profound theoretical link between the temporal evolution of a driven many-body system and the energy-scale evolution of the Renormalization Group, which is highly relevant for understanding non-equilibrium dynamics in correlated systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the connection between time-dependent integrability and Renormalization Group (RG) flow manifests at a deeper physical level in the SU(2) Gross-Neveu model.

**Main result.** The study establishes that the progression of time in the time-dependent model is equivalent to the RG flow in the static model, demonstrating a dynamical dimensional transmutation where a time-dependent mass gap is generated.

**Method.** The author uses the generalized Bethe ansatz framework, solving quantum Knizhnik-Zamolodchikov (qKZ) equations and evaluating the Yang-Yang action via a saddle-point approximation.

**Model / system.** The SU(2) Gross-Neveu model, a quantum field theory of interacting fermions with a time-dependent spin-exchange interaction strength g(t).

**Key observables.** Time-dependent mass gap m(t), instantaneous energy E(t), and the Yang-Yang action.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Drive rate alpha, characteristic time scale t0, coupling strength g(t), and the UV fixed point (SU(2)1 WZNW model).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The integrability of the system is maintained by assuming the time-dependent coupling strength follows a specific 'RG protocol' trajectory.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the RG protocol, comparing the adiabatic regime (mass gap generation) with the fast-driving regime (approach to the UV fixed point).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the time-dependent model, constructs an exact N-particle wavefunction using the generalized Bethe ansatz, reduces the problem to qKZ equations, and analyzes the adiabatic and fast-driving regimes using the Yang-Yang action and saddle-point methods.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work we consider the time-dependent $SU(2)$ Gross-Neveu model, which is a quantum field theory of fermions which interact with each other through spin exchange interaction with time-dependent coupling strength $g(t)$. Using the recently formulated generalized Bethe ansatz framework, we show that the system is integrable provided the time-dependent coupling strength is such that its trajectories in time are exactly same as that of the renormalization group (RG) flow equations corresponding to the static model, where time `$t$' of the time-dependent model is identified with the logarithm of the cutoff `$\ln Λ$' of the static model. In the scaling regime $Λ\rightarrow\infty$, the above relation between time and the logarithm of the cutoff provides a characteristic time scale $t_0$. We analyze the exact time-dependent wavefunction in the case of coupling strength decreasing with time and show that in the adiabatic regime, which corresponds to $t\sim t_0$ for drive rate $α_0=1$, the system exhibits a time-dependent dynamical dimensional transmutation where a time dependent mass gap is generated, which at time $t=t_0+Δt$ is given by $m(Δt)=m_0 e^{-πα_0Δt}$, where $m_0=Λe^{-πα_0 t_0}$. Comparing this with the mass gap of the static model, we identify the adiabatic regime of the time-dependent model with the scaling regime of the static model. In the case of very large time scales $t\gg t_0$ for drive rate $α_0$ or for very fast drive rates $α$ such that $αt \gg α_0t_0$, for any $t<L$, we argue that the system is asymptotically free and approaches the $SU(2)_1$ Wess-Zumino-Novikov-Witten (WZNW) model, which corresponds to the UV fixed point of the $SU(2)$ Gross-Neveu model. Hence we establish that progression of time in the time-dependent model is equivalent to RG flow in the corresponding static model.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Floquet quantum multiparameter estimation with periodic-driving-induced topological phase transition](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04463v1)

**Authors:** Yu Yang, Yuyang Tang, Pei Zhang, Fuli Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04463v1>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new strategy for simultaneous multi-parameter estimation in time-periodic quantum systems using Floquet theory. It demonstrates that operating near a topological phase transition significantly enhances sensing precision, even achieving super-Heisenberg scaling for the driving frequency.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel framework for quantum sensing in non-equilibrium driven systems, demonstrating how topological phase transitions can be leveraged to surpass standard precision limits in multi-parameter estimation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a quantum multiparameter estimation strategy for general time-periodically driven (Floquet) systems where a static effective Hamiltonian cannot be easily constructed.

**Main result.** The study reveals that near a periodic-driving-induced topological phase transition, estimation precision for magnetic fields reaches the Heisenberg limit, while the frequency parameter can achieve even higher super-Heisenberg scaling (t^4).

**Method.** The authors utilize Floquet theory within the Sambe space expansion and exact diagonalization of a truncated Floquet Hamiltonian to decompose the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix into contributions from eigenmodes, quasienergies, and multi-photon processes.

**Model / system.** A ring-shaped Rashba spin-orbit interferometer (modeled as a 2-level system) subject to a time-periodic Hamiltonian involving Rashba and Zeeman magnetic fields and a driving frequency.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM), Classical Fisher Information (CFI), winding number, measurement incompatibility, and transition probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rashba field (B0), Zeeman field (B1), driving frequency (omega), Heisenberg limit (t^2), and super-Heisenberg scaling (t^4).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes the system can be effectively modeled by a truncated Floquet matrix in Sambe space and focuses on the stroboscopic measurement regime.

**Figures summary.** Schematic of the interferometer, winding number plots, energy spectra near TPT, QFI scaling analysis over time, decomposition of QFI components, and measurement incompatibility plots.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Floquet multiparameter estimation, develops a mathematical framework using Floquet theory and Sambe space, applies this to a Rashba spin-orbit interferometer undergoing a topological phase transition, analyzes precision scaling and measurement incompatibility, and concludes with experimental feasibility mapping.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Periodically driven systems provide a powerful platform for quantum multiparameter estimation. Constructing a static effective Hamiltonian in a proper rotating frame is commonly employed to assess the attainable precision. However, such an approach becomes nonfeasible for more general time-periodically driven systems. To tackle this dilemma, we develop a quantum multiparameter estimation strategy in the Floquet theory framework. The contributions of Floquet eigenmodes, quasienergies, and multi-photon processes to the quantum Fisher information matrix and measurement incompatibility are determined, respectively. Moreover, this approach is applied to a ring-shaped Rashba spin-orbit interferometer model exhibiting the topological phase transition (TPT). In the vicinity of the TPT boundary, we reveal a pronounced enhancement in the estimation precision of multiple parameters with the Heisenberg limit scaling and even higher. Meanwhile, the measurement incompatibility vanishes in an oscillatory manner, and the stroboscopic projective measurement enables the highest estimation precision achievable. This work provides a complete Floquet picture for time-dependent critical quantum multiparameter estimation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04533"></a>
### [Online Riemannian Gradient Descent for Quantum State Tomography with Matrix Product Operators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04533v1)

**Authors:** Jian-Feng Cai, Jingyang Li, Xiaoqun Zhang, Yuanwei Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04533v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04533_figures/2605.04533_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Tensor network diagram connecting the matrix product operator representation of a density matrix to its corresponding Pauli coefficient tensor. The transformation between the computational basis and Pauli basis only involves the contraction of the Pauli matrices with core tensors.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04533_figures/2605.04533_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Reshape the n-th order tensor T into a 3-rd order tensor Z.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04533_figures/2605.04533_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. (Left-Top) Random state with n = 16, r = 4, δ = 0.1. (Right-Top) Random state with n = 32, r = 4, δ = 0.1. (Left-Bottom) The ground state of 1D Ising model with n = 16, δ = 0.1. (Right-Bottom) The phase transition behaviour of the reconstruction fidelity as a function of n (from 7 to 16) for a fixed rank r = 4. The initial error δ = 0.7 corresponds to a fidelity around 0.82. Dashed lines represent quadratic fits to data points with the same fidelity.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04533_figures/2605.04533_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. (Left) Random state with n = 12, r = 4, using noisy measurements with M = 4000 shots per observable. We set B = 50 and vary the step size parameter α in oRGD. (Right) Random state with r = 4 and different n, we set B = 100, α = 10−2 in oRGD.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04533_figures/2605.04533_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. (Left) Total runtime required for RGD and oRGD to achieve D(ρ∗, ρrec) ≤10−3. (Middle) Per-iteration runtime of RGD and oRGD with batch size B = 500. (Right) Reconstruction error of different quantum states using the RSGD algorithm.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an efficient online algorithm for reconstructing quantum states that have a Matrix Product Operator structure. By treating tomography as a tensor completion problem, the authors reduce the required measurement complexity from exponential to polynomial scaling. This allows for the scalable characterization of large-scale quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a scalable, efficient framework for reconstructing many-body density matrices, which is crucial for characterizing the states produced in experiments involving cold atoms or trapped ions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of performing efficient Quantum State Tomography (QST) for many-body systems without the exponential scaling of measurements and computation associated with traditional methods.

**Main result.** The authors develop an online Riemannian Gradient Descent (oRGD) algorithm that achieves linear convergence and requires a number of measurement settings that scales only quadratically with system size.

**Method.** The problem is reformulated as a low-rank tensor completion task on a real Tensor Train (TT) manifold, using an online gradient descent approach with a tailored spectral initialization.

**Model / system.** The target is an n-partite d-level quantum system (e.g., qubits) whose density matrix is represented as a Matrix Product Operator (MPO) with a bounded bond dimension.

**Key observables.** Pauli measurements for qubits and generalized Gell-Mann measurements for qudits.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (n), MPO bond dimension (r), spikiness (nu), incoherence (mu), and measurement noise level (sigma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The target state must be representable by an MPO with bounded bond dimension, and the algorithm requires a 'warm start' initialization within a specific error bound.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a tensor network diagram of the MPO-Pauli connection; Figure 3 shows convergence of relative error for random and Ising states; Figure 4 shows the impact of step size and measurement shots on error; Figure 5 compares runtime and error between RGD and oRGD.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the MPO-based QST problem, establishes a mathematical equivalence between MPO reconstruction and low-rank tensor completion, proposes the oRGD algorithm and a spectral initialization method, provides theoretical convergence proofs, and validates the approach with numerical experiments on random and Ising model states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Matrix product operators (MPOs) provide a scalable approach for quantum state tomography (QST) by offering a compact representation of many-body mixed states with limited entanglement, using only a number of parameters that scales polynomially with the system size. In this paper, we study QST for quantum density matrices that can be represented by MPOs. We first derive an equivalent characterization of Hermiticity in terms of the MPO core tensors and show that the coefficient tensor of an MPO under the Pauli or generalized Gell-Mann basis admits a real-valued low tensor-train (TT) rank structure. This establishes an explicit connection between MPO-based QST and noisy low-rank tensor completion. Motivated by this formulation, we develop an online Riemannian gradient descent (oRGD) algorithm that sequentially incorporates measurement data during the reconstruction process. With a proper initialization, we prove that oRGD converges linearly to the target MPO and succeeds with a number of distinct measurement settings that scales quadratically with the system size. As a byproduct, our analysis also yields a significantly improved sample complexity bound for the low TT rank tensor completion task. Furthermore, we propose a tailored spectral initialization method and establish its theoretical guarantee. Numerical experiments on several classes of quantum states validate the effectiveness and scalability of the proposed method.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04588"></a>
### [Single-photon scattering by a giant molecule asymmetrically coupled to parallel waveguides](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04588v1)

**Authors:** Ze-Quan Zhang, Guang-Zheng Ye, Wei-Xin Chen, Yong Li, Huaizhi Wu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04588v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **4/5** · 🔥 `interference shaping light` **4/5** · `Dicke superradiance` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04588_figures/2605.04588_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>Figure 5. Non-Markovianity induced transition from weak to strong coupling regime. </details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how a molecule of multi-point coupled 'giant atoms' can be used to route single photons between parallel waveguides. It shows that non-Markovian delay effects and asymmetric coupling can be precisely tuned to switch between different quantum coupling regimes and achieve deterministic photon routing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into how non-Markovianity and engineered dissipation can be used as tools for controlling quantum networks and light-matter interfaces in waveguide QED.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating single-photon scattering and routing in a waveguide-QED setup using a 'giant molecule' to understand how asymmetric coupling and non-Markovian retardation effects can control photon-path interference.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that by tailoring decay-rate asymmetry, atomic detuning, and propagation delay, one can achieve deterministic photon routing and transition between weak- and strong-coupling regimes.

**Method.** A real-space scattering approach within the single-excitation subspace is used, solving the stationary Schrödinger equation with a linear dispersion relation.

**Model / system.** A 'giant molecule' consisting of two frequency-detuned atoms, each coupled to multiple discrete points in two parallel waveguides, including a model for chiral coupling and interatomic exchange interaction.

**Key observables.** Transmission and reflection coefficients (probabilities), effective decay rates, Lamb shifts, and scattering amplitudes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cooperativity (C), asymmetry ratio (R), atomic detuning (delta), interatomic coupling (Omega), and propagation delay (Gamma*tau).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Rotating-wave approximation (RWA), linear dispersion relation, and the use of the single-excitation subspace.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the setup and decay rates; Fig 3 displays scattering probabilities in the critical coupling regime for different asymmetry ratios; Fig 4/6 illustrates the transition between coupling regimes induced by non-Markovian delays.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the giant molecule setup, defines the Hamiltonian and scattering framework, analyzes the Markovian regime (asymmetry and tuning), explores the strong-coupling regime, investigates non-Markovian retardation effects, and discusses chiral routing applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate single-photon scattering in a waveguide-QED setup, where a giant molecule composed of two frequency-detuned giant atoms is coupled to two parallel waveguides via multiple connection points. The competition between coherent atom--atom coupling and the effective decay rates dictates the splitting of a single resonance into a doublet in the transmission (reflection) spectra. By tailoring the asymmetry of the decay rates and the atomic detuning, one can engineer photon-path interference to optimize the transfer between waveguides; under chiral coupling conditions, this interference can be further harnessed to realize fully deterministic routing. In the non-Markovian regime, retardation effects can reshape the spectra and actively drive transitions between the weak- and strong-coupling regimes, converting an unsplit Markovian resonance into a clearly separated doublet, or conversely merging a split doublet back into a single resonance. For sufficiently long time delays, it further generates multiple resonances and avoided crossings, enriching the spectral response. Our results demonstrate how atomic detuning, decay-rate asymmetry, and non-Markovian retardation cooperate to provide versatile, interference-based control over single-photon routing in multi-port quantum networks.

</details>

### [Symmetric estimator for discrete self-energy of discrete many-body systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04974v1)

**Authors:** Aleksandrs Zacinskis, Frank T. Ebel, Mathias Pelz, Fabian B. Kugler, Karsten Held, Jan von Delft, Maurits W. Haverkort, Andreas Gleis

**Summary.** The paper presents a new numerical method to calculate the self-energy of discrete many-body systems without the instability and causality issues found in standard Dyson equation subtraction. By using a symmetric estimator based on the Schur complement, the method ensures physical spectral weights and improves accuracy in DMFT calculations. It is compatible with various many-body solvers including NRG, DMRG, and Krylov-based methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and open quantum systems because it provides a numerically stable way to extract spectral properties and self-energies from discrete representations (like MPS or NRG) without introducing unphysical broadening or violating causality.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Calculating the single-particle self-energy in discrete many-body systems is numerically unstable due to the subtraction of nearly canceling quantities in the Dyson equation, often leading to causality violations and unphysical negative spectral weight.

**Main result.** The authors derive a symmetric estimator for the discrete self-energy that is guaranteed to remain causal at the numerical level and avoids the need for artificial broadening. This method achieves significantly improved accuracy for impurity properties and allows for a consistent discrete DMFT self-consistency loop.

**Method.** The method uses a discrete realization of Kugler's symmetric improved estimator, treating the self-energy as the Schur complement of an augmented Green's function. It involves a three-step process of inversion, projection, and re-inversion of a matrix-valued propagator.

**Model / system.** The method is applicable to any discrete many-body Hamiltonian, specifically focusing on quantum impurity models (like the Single-Impurity Anderson Model) and the Hubbard model on a Betra lattice.

**Key observables.** Single-particle self-energy, Green's functions, hybridization function, quasiparticle weight (Z), scattering-rate coefficient (C), and double occupancy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Discretization parameter (Lambda), interaction strength (U/D), temperature (T), and Krylov space size (K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the system is a discrete many-body system with a finite number of bath poles; the Schur complement formula requires the existence of the involved matrix inverses.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the discrete self-energy spectrum; Figure 3 compares double occupation across different codes; Figure 4 shows quasiparticle weight vs interaction strength; Figure 5 displays the self-energy spectrum in metallic and insulating phases; Figure 6 illustrates the effect of regularization on the quasiparticle weight.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of numerical instability in self-energy extraction, derives the symmetric estimator using the Schur complement of an augmented propagator, demonstrates implementation across various solvers (TaSK, NRG, ED), validates the approach using the Anderson impurity model and DMFT, and discusses regularization of spurious poles.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We derive a discrete spectral representation of the single-particle self-energy using a discrete evaluation of Kugler's symmetric improved estimator. Our construction can be used on both the real and the complex (Matsubara) frequency axis. It is guaranteed to remain causal at the numerical level, in contrast to standard approaches that may generate unphysical negative spectral weight or require additional broadening. Our representation can be used for any Hamiltonian; here we apply it to quantum impurity models and in dynamical mean-field theory. The latter is formulated with a discrete hybridization function throughout its self-consistency loop. In both cases and across various numerical methods, we obtain significantly improved accuracy for a range of impurity properties.

</details>

<sub>Figure 4. **Summary.** The study shows that the capacitance network in transmon arrays induces non-local interactions that significantly speed up the scrambling of quantum information. While these interactions degrade single-qubit state transfer, they facilitate faster OTOC saturation and move the system toward partial ergodicity.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides insights into how parasitic or network-induced non-localities can be leveraged to accelerate information scrambling and create non-local correlations without entering a fully chaotic regime, which is crucial for scalable superconducting architectures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how non-local, network-mediated capacitive interactions in superconducting transmon arrays affect quantum information scrambling and spectral statistics.

**Main result.** Non-local couplings accelerate the saturation of Out-of_Time-Ordered Correlators (OTOCs) and drive the system toward partial ergodicity, though they do not reach full quantum chaos (GOE).

**Method.** Analytical derivation using Chebyshev polynomials of the second kind to invert the capacitance matrix, combined with exact numerical diagonalization and OTOC calculations.

**Model / system.** A 1D array of N superconducting transmon qubits with interactions derived from the inverse capacitance matrix, including nearest-neighbor and non-local 'collateral' couplings.

**Key observables.** Out-of-Time-Ordered Correlators (OTOCs), level-spacing ratio (r-parameter), and single-excitation state transfer efficiency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Capacitance ratio (Cc/Cq), Manhattan distance between qubits, and the weak-coupling limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Two-level system (TLS) approximation, Rotating Wave Approximation (RWA), and the transmon approximation (expanding cosine potential to fourth order).

**Figures summary.** Figures 4a and 4b show OTOC dynamics for 10 and 12 qubit chains, illustrating early-time N.N. dominance and late-time non-local effects; Figure 4c shows the transition of the level-spacing ratio with increasing qubit number.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-local capacitive coupling, provides an analytical framework for the capacitance network, analyzes the impact on single-excitation transport, investigates many-body scrambling via OTOCs, and concludes with spectral statistics analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the dynamical and spectral consequences of capacitance-network-mediated interactions in superconducting transmon arrays beyond effective nearest-neighbor descriptions. While weak coupling regimes are well captured by an effective nearest-neighbor interacting models, we show that increasing capacitive connectivity induces a pronounced departure from this approximation in dynamical observables. Using Out-of-Time-Ordered Correlators (OTOCs), we demonstrate that such network-mediated couplings significantly accelerate operator scrambling, leading to rapid saturation compared to the nearest-neighbor limit. This dynamical crossover is accompanied by a shift in spectral statistics away from Poissonian behavior toward level repulsion, with the ratio parameter remaining intermediate between Poisson and Gaussian Orthogonal Ensemble (GOE) limits. This indicates the emergence of partial ergodicity rather than fully developed quantum chaos. As this behavior arises within experimentally realistic regimes of current superconducting transmon devices, identifying when network-mediated couplings qualitatively alter information dynamics is directly relevant for scalable superconducting architectures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04429"></a>
### [Unitary dynamics and resource trade-offs in a four-qubit isotropic Heisenberg XXX chain with tunable next-nearest-neighbor coupling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04429v1)

**Authors:** Seyed Mohsen Moosavi Khansari  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04429v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**
