**Summary.** This paper provides exact analytical solutions for the evolution of quantum resources in a four-qubit Heisenberg chain. It reveals that a tunable coupling parameter can be used to either freeze quantum dynamics or rapidly generate entanglement and coherence. These findings offer a controlled framework for benchmarking small-scale quantum processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides exact analytical benchmarks for small quantum devices and demonstrates a precise mechanism for controlling and stabilizing quantum resources via tunable coupling.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the unitary dynamics and resource trade-offs between fidelity, coherence, and entanglement in a small-scale quantum spin chain.

**Main result.** The authors derive closed-form expressions showing that all quantum resource dynamics are governed by a single phase variable phi = (alpha + 1)t, identifying a 'frozen' regime at alpha = -1 where resources are stabilized.

**Method.** Analytical derivation using the unitary evolution formalism, partial tracing of the density matrix, and application of entanglement and coherence measures.

**Model / system.** A four-qubit isotropic Heisenberg XXX spin chain featuring nearest-neighbor and tunable next-nearest-neighbor coupling (alpha), initialized in a Bell-type product state.

**Key observables.** State fidelity, l1-norm of coherence, and Entanglement of Formation (Ef) for two-qubit subsystems.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The tunable next-nearest-neighbor coupling alpha, the composite phase phi = (alpha + 1)t, and the frozen regime at alpha = -1.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is closed and finite (four qubits), the dynamics are purely unitary, and the initial state is a specific Bell-type product state.

**Figures summary.** Surface plots illustrating the periodic, banded structures of fidelity, coherence, and entanglement of formation as functions of alpha and time.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the Hamiltonian and initial state, derives analytical expressions for the density matrix and observables, analyzes the resulting periodic dynamics and sensitivity, and discusses future extensions to open systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We derive the unitary dynamics of a four-qubit isotropic Heisenberg XXX chain with tunable next-nearest-neighbor coupling $α$, initialized in a Bell-type product state. Closed-form expressions are obtained for the state fidelity $F(ρ(0),ρ(t))$, the $l_1$-norm coherence $C_{l_1}(ρ(t))$, and the entanglement of formation $E_F^{12}(t)$ and $E_F^{34}(t)$ for the two-qubit subsystems (12) and (34). All quantities depend exclusively on the composite phase $φ= (α+1)t$. Fidelity obeys $F = |\cos(φ/2)|$ and remains frozen at $F \equiv 1$ for $α= -1$. Coherence follows $C_{l_1} = \sin^2(φ/2)$, vanishing identically at $α= -1$ and exhibiting sensitivity proportional to $|α+1|$. The entanglement of formation is an entropic function of $φ$, displaying banded oscillations and freezing at $α= -1$. The phase $φ$ unifies all observables, linking the rate of resource variation to $|α+1|$ and identifying maximal sensitivity along $(α+1)t = π/4 + kπ/2$. This framework provides exact benchmarks for few-qubit quantum devices and a controlled pathway for extensions to noise, finite temperature, and larger systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04271"></a>
### [Quantum Compression for Distributed Entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04271v1)

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04271_figures/2605.04271_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Uncompressed transmission of two three qubit systems with channel noise. Compressed transmission of the systems with and without</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents a method to jointly optimize quantum states and compression schemes to maximize entanglement distribution in networks with uncertain topologies. It provides mathematical bounds on entanglement achievable under a fixed transmission budget and demonstrates that compression can be beneficial even in the presence of noisy hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for quantum network design and open quantum systems, as it addresses the practical trade-off between reducing channel noise exposure via compression and the error introduction from noisy quantum gates.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the distribution of multipartite entanglement in quantum networks when the partitioning of qubits among parties is unknown at the time of state preparation.

**Main result.** The authors derive non-asymptotic upper and lower bounds on achievable average entanglement and identify a crossover point where compression benefits outweigh the noise introduced by compression circuits.

**Method.** A joint optimization framework using quantum source coding theory, symmetry-based optimization of weighted Dicke states, and local lossless isometric compression maps.

**Model / system.** A system of n qubits distributed among m parties, where the assignment of qubits to parties is random. The states are restricted to the permutation-invariant (symmetric) subspace, such as Dicke and Comb states.

**Key observables.** Average entanglement (Geometric Multipartite Entanglement and von Neumann entropy), average coding rate, and Logarithmic Negativity under noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Average coding rate (transmission budget), number of qubits (n), number of parties (m), and channel depolarizing probability (p).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The compression is lossless for states in the symmetric subspace, and the assignment of qubits to parties is independent of the prepared state.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the compression of subsystems to reduce communication costs; Figure 2 and 3 show entanglement-rate curves for bipartite and multipartite cases; Figure 4 compares uncompressed, ideal, and noisy compression performance under channel noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of entanglement distribution under uncertainty, formulates it as a quantum source coding problem, derives analytical bounds for bipartite and multipartite settings, proposes an efficient optimization method using Dicke states, and evaluates performance under circuit and channel noise.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study compression strategies for multipartite entanglement distribution under uncertainty in the partitioning of the quantum state. When the partition is not known at the time of state preparation, we show that a joint design of the resource state and a family of compression schemes can increase the entanglement across partitions under a fixed transmission budget. We formulate this as a source coding problem and derive non-asymptotic upper and lower bounds on the achievable average entanglement subject to an average coding rate. We furthermore design an efficient method for jointly optimizing states and lossless compression maps by exploiting the inherent symmetry of weighted Dicke states. In the bipartite case, we propose practical constructions that closely approach the derived upper bound, and more generally we provide practical constructions for multipartite settings.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<sub>Figure 4 shows the measured and simulated scattering parameters along with the mode profiles. **Summary.** The researchers developed a new type of 'release-free' optomechanical crystal that stays attached to its substrate to improve heat dissipation. By integrating lithium niobate with silicon, they created a device capable of converting microwave signals to optical signals. This is a significant step toward building practical interfaces for connecting quantum computers to optical networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for quantum optics and quantum networks as it provides a hardware pathway for efficient, thermally-anchored interfaces between microwave-frequency superconducting qubits and optical-frequency communication fibers.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Traditional suspended optomechanical crystals suffer from thermal noise due to optical absorption and lack efficient thermal anchoring, which limits their use as transducers between superconducting qubits and optical fibers.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated a functional release-free electro-optomechanical transducer that achieves efficient microwave-to-optical transduction with coupling rates compatible with quantum-level operation.

**Method.** The device was fabricated by integrating thin-film lithium niobate onto a silicon-on-insulator platform via micro-transfer printing and characterized using tunable lasers, vector network analyzers, and high-speed photodetectors.

**Model / system.** A release-free, chip-scale electro-optomechanical crystal transducer on a silicon-on-insulator (SOI) platform, combining silicon for optomechanics and lithium niobate for piezoelectricity.

**Key observables.** Optical resonance, mechanical linewidth, optomechanical coupling rate (g_om), electromechanical efficiency, and microwave-to-optical scattering parameter (S_oe).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Resolved-sideband regime (omega_m > kappa_o), low cooperativity regime (C_om << 1), mechanical frequency of ~4.32 GHz, and total microwave-to-optical efficiency of 1.5e-7.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The transduction is assumed to be of piezo-optomechanical origin rather than purely electro-optic; simulations assume an isotropic loss factor to account for intrinsic material loss.

**Figures summary.** Fig 2 shows the mechanical spectrum and phonon number vs microwave power; Fig 4a compares measured and simulated microwave-to-optical scattering parameters; Fig 4b visualizes mechanical mode profiles.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of thermal noise in suspended crystals, describes the fabrication of the release-free LN-on-SOI platform, details the experimental characterization of optical and mechanical properties, presents the results of microwave-to-optical transduction, and discusses the potential for scaling to qubit-level operation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electro-optic modulation is central to classical optical communications and emerging quantum technologies. High-confinement optomechanical crystal modulators enable microwave-optical transduction through strong optomechanical interactions and offer a promising interface between superconducting qubits and optical fibers. However, their performance is limited by thermal noise from optical absorption. Release-free optomechanical crystals provide improved thermal anchoring but have not yet been integrated into a microwave-optical transducer. Here, we demonstrate a release-free electro-optomechanical transducer combining strong optomechanical interactions in silicon with the efficient piezoelectricity of lithium niobate via micro-transfer printing. We observe electro- and optomechanical coupling rates compatible with quantum-level operation when co-integrated with a superconducting microwave circuit. This advance moves release-free electro-optomechanical devices toward practical microwave-optical interfaces.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04736"></a>
### [Neural-powered unit disk graph embedding: qubits connectivity for some QUBO problems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04736v1)

**Authors:** Chiara Vercellino, Paolo Viviani, Giacomo Vitali, Alberto Scionti, Andrea Scarabosio, Olivier Terzo, Edoardo Giusto, Bartolomeo Montrucchio  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04736v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Rydberg arrays` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04736_figures/2605.04736_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Representation of a qubit register com- posed of neutral atoms arranged in a 2D array. In- teraction between qubits is regulated by the Rydberg blockade effect: only atoms falling within the Ryd- berg radius are subject to interaction.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04736_figures/2605.04736_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: GEAN architecture: 5 qubits embedding in 2D, the initial coordinates (x1, y1), . . . (x5, y5) are transformed into feasible coordinates (x ′ 1, y ′ 1), . . . (x ′ 5, y ′ 5). The output layer of the model, DistL, provides all pairs’ distances between nodes.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04736_figures/2605.04736_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 represents both these properties. To embed a clique, all the vertices of the graph, KN, must lie</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04736_figures/2605.04736_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Representation of the maximal clique, K7, fig. 3a), and of the maximal degree, fig. 3b), that can be embedded on the register. The baseline embed- ding is the regular hexagonal packing with l = 4 µm side and the Rydberg blockade radius is the maxi- mum available on the machine ˜rb ≈10.26 µm.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04736_figures/2605.04736_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Transformation into feasible 2D embeddings of the Ga, Gc and Gd connected components for the QUBO MIS antennas problem.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a neural-network-based method called GEAN to solve the difficult task of embedding optimization problems onto the physical layout of a neutral-atom quantum computer. By learning to transform qubit coordinates to satisfy Rydberg blockade constraints, the method outperforms traditional classical solvers. This enables more efficient mapping of complex combinatorial problems onto programmable quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a novel way to use machine learning to automate the hardware-software mapping process, specifically addressing the geometric constraints inherent in Rydberg-based quantum simulators.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of mapping Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) problems onto the fixed physical connectivity of a neutral-atom quantum processor, specifically satisfying Unit Disk Graph (UDG) constraints.

**Main result.** The proposed GEAN neural network approach outperforms the Gurobi solver in finding feasible embeddings and can efficiently handle complex 3D 87-qubit configurations in under 2 minutes.

**Method.** A Graph Embedding Autoencoder Network (GEAN) uses a specialized architecture with distance computation layers to transform initial qubit positions into a feasible configuration that satisfies all hardware and adjacency constraints.

**Model / system.** The system is an optical quantum simulator based on neutral Rubidium atoms in optical tweezers, utilizing the Rydberg blockade effect to create an Ising-like Hamiltonian where interactions are determined by the blockade radius.

**Key observables.** Maximum clique size, maximum degree, minimum and maximum inter-qubit distances, and the loss function components representing constraint violations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rydberg blockade radius, minimum separation distance (4 um), maximum pair distance (100 um), and the 2D/3D placement domain radius (50 um).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The QUBO matrix diagonal elements must be identical and off-diagonal elements must share the same sign due to the nature of the 1/d^6 interaction.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Rydberg blockade mechanism; Figure 2 details the GEAN architecture; Figure 3 illustrates physical limits like hexagonal packing; Figures 4-7 visualize the transformation of 2D and 3D embeddings for various problem instances.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the graph embedding problem in the context of Rydberg atoms, introduces the GEAN neural network architecture and its loss function, presents experimental benchmarks against Gurobi, and demonstrates scalability from 2D to 3D embeddings.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Graph embedding is a recurrent problem in quantum computing, for instance, quantum annealers need to solve a minor graph embedding in order to map a given Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) problem onto their internal connectivity pattern. This work presents a novel approach to constrained unit disk graph embedding, which is encountered when trying to solve combinatorial optimization problems in QUBO form, using quantum hardware based on neutral Rydberg atoms. The qubits, physically represented by the atoms, are excited to the Rydberg state through laser pulses. Whenever qubits pairs are closer together than the blockade radius, entanglement can be reached, thus preventing entangled qubits to be simultaneously in the excited state. Hence, the blockade radius determines the adjacency pattern among qubits, corresponding to a unit disk configuration. Although it is straightforward to compute the adjacency pattern given the qubit coordinates, identifying a feasible unit disk arrangement that matches the desired QUBO matrix is, on the other hand, a much harder task. In the context of quantum optimization, this issue translates into the physical placement of the qubits in the 2D/3D register to match the machine's Ising-like Hamiltonian with the QUBO formulation of the optimization problems. The proposed solution exploits the power of neural networks to transform an initial embedding configuration, which does not match the quantum hardware requirements or does not account for the unit disk property, into a feasible embedding properly representing the target optimization problems. Experimental results show that this new approach overcomes in performance Gurobi solver.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Intelligent Optimal Control of Rydberg Gates with Incremental-Update Deep Reinforcement Learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04628v1)

**Authors:** Yue Cai, Hanlin Zhang, Keye Zhang, Jing Qian

<sub>FIG. 1. Implementation of Rydberg gates via the IU-DRL intelligent optimization. (a) In the DRL cycle, agent receives the state information ⃗S(ti) from environment at every time step ti and determines the action ⃗A(ti) according to the learned policy. Meanwhile, the policy is iteratively updated under the guidance of reward signal R(ti). Then the environment evolves over the next time step based on the selected action, yielding updated state ⃗S(ti+1) and updated reward R(ti+1), which are fed back to the agent as inputs for the ti+1 time step. (b) The synchronously-optimized pulse amplitudes (Ωc(t), Ωt(t)) and phases (ϕc(t), ϕt(t)) for a given duration tN = 0.4µs with N = 100, which are...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. EIT-based piecewise adiabatic gate protocol. STAY: If the control atom is in |0⟩c the target atom adiabatically follows the EIT dark state |Ψ⟩t = (1/ √</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an incremental-update deep reinforcement learning framework to optimize Rydberg-based CNOT gates. By simultaneously modulating pulse amplitudes and phases, the method achieves extremely high fidelities and significantly faster gate operations than traditional methods. The approach is particularly notable for its ability to autonomously discover efficient, non-intuitive control strategies that are robust to experimental noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a novel AI-driven approach to control complex, non-adiabatic dynamics in Rydberg systems, offering a way to bypass the performance ceilings of traditional adiabatic protocols in neutral-atom quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Designing high-fidelity, high-speed Rydberg CNOT gates in neutral-atom arrays while overcoming the fidelity-speed trade-off and the limitations of traditional piecewise adiabatic protocols.

**Main result.** The proposed IU-DRL framework achieves a peak average gate fidelity of 0.9991 and reduces gate duration by 38.9% compared to conventional methods, while remaining robust against thermal motion.

**Method.** Incremental-Update Deep Reinforcement Learning (IU-DRL) using a Trust Region Policy Optimization (TRPO) architecture to synchronously modulate pulse amplitudes and phases.

**Model / system.** A system of two trapped 87Rb atoms modeled as four-level systems interacting via van der Waals forces, including dynamics for spontaneous decay and atomic thermal motion.

**Key observables.** Average gate fidelity (F_avg), gate duration, and population leakage into Rydberg and intermediate states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rydberg interaction strength V0 = 2pi x 450 MHz, temperature T = 10 uK, and smoothing coefficients xi = 0.1.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary error source in the control atom is assumed to be spontaneous decay during its residency in the Rydberg state.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the DRL cycle and optimized pulse profiles; Figure 3 illustrates the learning of the early-cutoff strategy; Figure 4 compares the proposed method against suboptimal traditional update methods; Figure 6 shows population dynamics and pulse sequences for different smoothing regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Rydberg gate optimization, describes the IU-DRL framework and its incremental update mechanism, presents results on fidelity and speedup, demonstrates robustness against thermal noise, and compares the performance against traditional adiabatic and non-adiabatic benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Deep reinforcement learning (DRL), acting as a novel and powerful paradigm for quantum optimal control, offers transformative opportunities for advancing neutral-atom quantum computing. In this work, we theoretically demonstrate a DRL-based framework for realizing Rydberg controlled-NOT gates that achieve both high speed and high fidelity through the synchronous modulation of multiple pulse parameters without any prior heuristic ansatz. By introducing an incremental-update learning policy, our framework effectively regularizes the exploration of the control landscape, ensuring the generation of smooth, experimentally feasible pulse profiles while significantly reducing computational overhead compared to conventional schemes. Crucially, the framework autonomously discovers an early-cutoff policy by optimally reconciling operation speed with high-precision coherent control. Our optimized protocol achieves a peak average fidelity of 0.9991, significantly outperforming conventional methods and surpassing the critical fault-tolerant threshold. This work establishes a generalizable, AI-driven pathway for designing high-performance quantum gates and provides a robust paradigm for autonomous control field optimization across diverse qubit platforms.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04915"></a>
### [Optimal Error Exponents for Composite Sequential Quantum Hypothesis Testing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04915v1)

**Authors:** Jacob Paul Simpson, Efstratios Palias, Sharu Theresa Jose  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04915v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04915_figures/2605.04915_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Illustration in the eigenvalue plane of Hd for d = 2. The line Ad represents the trace-one constraint λ1 +λ2 = 1, while Sd is the line segment where λ1, λ2 ≥0 and λ1 + λ2 = 1. The line Vd represents the associated translation space, corresponding to the trace-zero constraint λ1 + λ2 = 0.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04915_figures/2605.04915_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. Illustration of the second part in the proof of Lemma 14. The scalar a comes from the norm-equivalence bound in Lemma 12, while for a fixed σ(k) ∈int(D), ε is chosen so that (47) is satisfied.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper solves the problem of finding the best possible error rates for sequential quantum testing when the alternative hypothesis is a set of states rather than a single state. It introduces an adaptive measurement strategy that achieves the optimal trade-off between error types. The results show that the complexity of this task is fundamentally linked to the measured relative entropy between the states.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental limits on the efficiency of adaptive quantum measurements, which is critical for quantum sensing and state estimation tasks in quantum optics and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Characterizing the optimal error exponent region for composite sequential quantum hypothesis testing (SQHT) under an expected sample size constraint.

**Main result.** The authors establish that the achievable error exponent region is bounded by the minimal measured relative entropies between the null state and the alternative set, and prove that this strategy is optimal.

**Method.** The study proposes a mixture-sequential quantum probability ratio test (mixture-SQPRT) that uses adaptive measurements based on a mixture estimate of the alternative set and employs martingale theory and information-theoretic inequalities.

**Model / system.** The model involves sequential testing between a known null quantum state (rho) and a compact, convex set of alternative quantum states (D) in a finite-dimensional Hilbert space.

**Key observables.** Type-I and Type-II error probabilities, measured relative entropy, and the mixture log-likelihood ratio.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Expected sample size (n), error exponents (R0, R1), and the dimension of the Hilbert space (d).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum states are assumed to be full rank (or uniformly full rank) and the set of alternative hypotheses is compact and convex.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the testing framework; Figure 2 shows a geometric representation of the space of density matrices in a 2D eigenvalue plane; Figure 3 depicts the geometric mapping between Euclidean and trace-norm balls.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SQHT problem, proposes an adaptive mixture-SQPRT strategy, establishes the error exponent region via achievability and converse proofs, and provides mathematical lemmas regarding the martingale properties and continuity of the divergence measures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the composite sequential quantum hypothesis testing (SQHT) problem, where the objective is to distinguish a null quantum state from a compact, convex set of alternative quantum states. We propose a mixture-sequential quantum probability ratio test that adaptively selects measurements based on the current mixture estimate of the alternative set, and stops upon the first threshold crossing of the mixture log-likelihood ratio. Under an expected sample size constraint, we show that our proposed adaptive strategy simultaneously achieves the optimal Type-I and (worst-case) Type-II error exponents. These exponents are characterized by the minimal measured relative entropies between the null state and the alternative set. We further establish a matching converse, thereby characterizing the optimal error exponent region. Finally, our results show that achieving vanishing error probabilities in composite SQHT requires an expected sample complexity at least as large as that of sequential testing between two fixed quantum states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04892"></a>
### [Real-time Surface-Code Error Correction Using an FPGA-based Neural-Network Decoder](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04892v1)

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. Real-time QEC. a, Logical memory circuit with repeated stabilizer measurements and real-time feedback. A delay is inserted after each measurement to accommodate the feedback latency, and a final PFU corrects residual errors detected during logical measurement. The right panel shows logical |0L⟩fidelity (inset, n = 1) versus the delay time. Delays shorter than the closed-loop latency (550 ns) prevent the decoder output from reaching the AWG in time, corresponding to an infeasible operating regime (purple). b, Logical-state fidelities as a function of QEC rounds n with final-round feedback. </details>

<sub>FIG. 4. Real-time QEC in a non-Clifford logical cir- cuit. a, Circuit (blue background) implementing a logi- cal rotation about the XL axis. **Summary.** This paper presents a hardware-integrated architecture using an FPGA-based neural network to perform real-time surface-code error correction. By achieving sub-microsecond closed-loop latency, the system enables active mid-circuit feedback necessary for non-Clifford operations. This provides a scalable pathway toward the low-latency control required for large-scale fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for real-time, low-latency quantum error correction (QEC) to prevent error accumulation and enable mid-circuit feedback for non-Clifford gates in fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Main result.** Demonstrated a hardware-integrated FPGA-based neural-network decoder that achieves a 550 ns closed-loop latency, providing real-time surface-code QEC with performance comparable to offline decoding.

**Method.** An LSTM-based neural network decoder is implemented on an AMD Kintex-7 FPGA using 6-bit integer quantization and a pipelined architecture to process syndrome data in real-time.

**Model / system.** A 17-qubit subset of a 66-qubit superconducting quantum processor implementing a distance-3 rotated surface code.

**Key observables.** Logical error rates, logical state fidelity, oscillation amplitude of the expectation value <Z_L> during logical rotations, and closed-loop latency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Closed-loop latency of 550 ns, QEC cycle time of 1.25 us, decoding latency of 124 ns, and 6-bit quantization for the NN weights.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scalability of the decoder is limited by FPGA resource consumption (DSP slices) as the code distance increases; the decoder accuracy assumes the effectiveness of 6-bit quantization and piecewise linear activation approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup and QEC workflow; Figure 2 details the FPGA-integrated LSTM architecture and compares logical fidelity of the NN decoder against offline MWPM; Figure 3 demonstrates mid-circuit feedback effectiveness in non-Clifford circuits.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to QEC challenges, description of the hardware-integrated architecture (FPGA/NN decoder), experimental implementation on a superconducting processor, performance evaluation (latency and error rates), demonstration of non-Clifford gate feedback, and discussion on scalability and hardware limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum error correction (QEC) is essential for achieving low error rates required for fault-tolerant quantum computation. In stabilizer-based codes such as the surface code, errors are inferred from repeated syndrome measurements and corrected by a classical decoder. To prevent error accumulation, decoding must be performed with both high throughput and low latency to keep pace with the QEC cycle and enable real-time feedback for universal logical operations. Here we report a hardware-integrated control architecture featuring an FPGA-based neural-network (NN) decoder and experimentally demonstrate real-time surface-code (distance-3) QEC on a superconducting quantum processor. The system achieves a deterministic closed-loop latency of 550 ns, including 124 ns for NN decoding, enabling feedback corrections within a 1.25 us QEC cycle. We show that real-time decoding and feedback correction achieve logical performance comparable to offline decoding while maintaining robustness against varying error conditions. We further demonstrate mid-circuit feedback correction in non-Clifford logical circuits, where Pauli-frame updating alone becomes insufficient. Our results establish a low-latency hardware architecture for embedded QEC control and provide a pathway towards scalable fault-tolerant quantum computing systems.

</details>

### [The nonlocal magic of a holographic Schwinger pair](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04210v1)

**Authors:** Sebastian Grieninger  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04210v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04210_figures/2605.04210_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04210_figures/2605.04210_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04210_figures/2605.04210_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04210_figures/2605.04210_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04210_figures/2605.04210_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the creation of quark-antiquark pairs in strong gauge fields generates 'nonlocal magic,' a type of quantum correlation beyond simple entanglement. Using holographic duality, the author shows that this effect is a consequence of the non-flatness of the entanglement spectrum in dimensions greater than two. The study provides a geometric link between gravitational backreaction and the emergence of quantum resources.

**Why it may be interesting.** It connects high-energy holographic dynamics to quantum resource theory, specifically showing how particle production can generate non-classical resources like non-stabilizerness (magic) that are central to quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the production of a Schwinger pair in a strong non-Abelian electric field generates nonlocal magic, defined as non-stabilizerness that cannot be removed by local unitaries.

**Main result.** The study finds that for spacetime dimensions d > 2, the entanglement spectrum is non-flat, implying the dynamical generation of nonlocal magic during the pair creation process.

**Method.** The author uses holographic duality (AdS/CFT) and the Casini-Huerta-Myers (CHM) map to relate entanglement properties to a thermal state on a hyperbolic plane, computing Rényi entropies via the Nambu-Goto action of a probe string.

**Model / system.** A holographic Schwinger pair (heavy quark-antiquark) modeled as a probe string/brane in a (d+1)-dimensional AdS background, subject to strong non-Abelian (chromo)electric fields.

**Key observables.** Entanglement entropy (S_EE), refined Rényi entropy, capacity of entanglement (C_E), and nonlocal magic (M_NL).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spacetime dimension (d), 't Hooft coupling (lambda), electric field strength (E), and the large-Nc, large-lambda limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculation assumes the probe-brane/string approximation, the large-Nc and large-lambda limit, and focuses on spherical entangling surfaces.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of nonlocal magic in pair production, establishes the holographic framework using the CHM map, develops the information-theoretic diagnostics (capacity of entanglement), proves the equivalence of computing C_E via replica construction versus gravitational backreaction, and concludes with the dimensional dependence of the results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We analyze the emergence of nonlocal magic in Schwinger pair creation in strong non-Abelian (chromo)electric fields using holography. The produced quark--antiquark pair is entangled into a color singlet, yet accelerates into causally disconnected Rindler wedges. Using the Casini--Huerta--Myers conformal mapping and the probe-brane framework, we compute the refined Rényi entropy and its derivative, which captures the antiflatness of the entanglement spectrum for a spherical bipartition. We find that for boundary spacetime dimension $d>2$, the entanglement spectrum is non-flat, implying the dynamical generation of nonlocal magic in the pair creation process. Interestingly, the nonlocal magic in the holographic dual can be obtained from the free energy of the probe action.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05167"></a>
### [Entanglement-Rank Duality in Quadratic Phase Quantum States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05167v1)

**Authors:** Zakaria Dahbi, Amelle Zair  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05167v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.05167_figures/2605.05167_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. CRT structure for square-free d. N parties × r prime factors grid. Horizontal: CRT recombines a row to one d-level qudit. Vertical: each column is a prime-sector state.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces an algebraic method to construct and verify highly entangled (AME) states by mapping entanglement properties to the rank of matrices over finite fields. By using the Chinese Remainder Theorem, the authors reduce the complexity of verifying large-scale systems from exponential to polynomial time. This allows for the explicit construction of massive entangled states that were previously computationally inaccessible.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful algebraic framework for generating highly entangled states, which are essential resources for quantum error correction and quantum communication protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of finding a systematic approach for constructing Absolutely Maximally Entangled (AME) states and the exponential complexity involved in verifying their entanglement properties.

**Main result.** The authors establish a Rank-Purity Duality that links the Rényi-2 purity of subsystems to the rank of submatrices in a phase matrix, enabling the efficient construction and certification of large-scale AME states like AME(17, 10001).

**Method.** The study uses finite-field algebraic tools, including the Chinese Remainder Theorem for decomposing composite dimensions and a parallel tempering optimization algorithm to minimize rank deficits.

**Model / system.** A class of multipartite quadratic-phase quantum states (including stabilizer states) defined by symmetric matrices over finite fields or rings.

**Key observables.** Rényi-2 purity, Rényi-2 entropy, and submatrix rank.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of parties (N), local dimension (d), and the rank of the phase matrix submatrices.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results primarily focus on the class of quadratic-phase quantum states and assume square-free dimensions for the CRT decomposition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the CRT structure for square-free dimensions, showing how N-party states are represented across prime-sector states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of AME construction, defines the quadratic-phase model, proves the Rank-Purity Duality and CRT decomposition, presents a numerical search algorithm, and demonstrates the successful construction and certification of a large-scale AME state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Absolutely Maximally Entangled (AME) states are important resources in quantum information processing; however, a general systematic approach for constructing these states remains a formidable challenge. We identify a finite-field rank structure underlying multipartite entanglement in a class of quadratic-phase quantum states defined by symmetric matrices over $\mathbb{F}_p$. We prove an exact Rank-Purity Duality: the Rényi-2 purity of any subsystem is determined solely by the rank of the phase matrix. Within this ansatz, the existence of an AME state is equivalent to the existence of a generating phase matrix $P$ whose bipartition submatrices are of full rank, reducing the condition for maximal entanglement to a rank constraint on $P$. This establishes a direct correspondence between entanglement and cut-rank geometry in finite-field matrices. Furthermore, for square-free local dimensions, we show that the entanglement structure factorises via the Chinese Remainder Theorem into independent prime-field contributions, yielding an exact additive decomposition of Rényi-2 entropies. These results provide an algebraic characterisation of entanglement in the quadratic phase formalism and enable the systematic construction of highly entangled states.

</details>

### [Quantum criticality beyond thermodynamic stability](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04153v1)

**Authors:** Mariam Ughrelidze, Vincent P. Flynn, Emilio Cobanera, Lorenza Viola

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>Figure 5. Correlation functions and Krein gap for the double harmonic chain. (a) Log plot of |⟨pjpj+r⟩| with a fixed ∆Krein = 0.001 and varying choice of parameters (Ω1, Ω2) (see also panel d): from darkest to lightest colors, the values of Ω2 (Ω1) are varied from 10−3 to 1, with increments of 10−2. **Summary.** This paper generalizes the concept of quantum criticality to systems that are dynamically stable but lack a traditional ground state. By introducing the Krein gap as the fundamental energy scale, the authors show that critical behavior, such as long-range correlations and entanglement scaling, can be rigorously defined for a much broader class of bosonic models.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a unified framework for studying phase transitions in non-equilibrium or thermodynamically unstable systems, which is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics, photonics, and many-body dynamics where energy spectra are not bounded from below.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Extending the theory of quantum criticality to quadratic bosonic Hamiltonians (QBHs) that are dynamically stable but lack thermodynamic stability (i.e., no ground state).

**Main result.** The authors establish that criticality is well-defined for all dynamically stable QBHs via the closing of the 'Krein gap,' and that this gap serves as the relevant many-body gap for characterizing phase transitions, exceptional points, and long-range correlations.

**Method.** The study employs a generalized criticality framework using Bogoliubov transformations, pseudo-Hermitian matrix analysis, covariance-matrix formalism, and information geometry (quantum metric tensor and fidelity).

**Model / system.** Translationally invariant, finite-range quadratic bosonic Hamiltonians (QBHs) on a D-dimensional lattice, which include physically relevant platforms like photonics, cavity-QED, and magnonics.

**Key observables.** Krein gap, entanglement entropy, two-point correlation functions, Quantum Metric Tensor (QMT), and quantum fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dynamical stability vs. thermodynamic stability, Exceptional Points (EP), Krein Collisions (KC), and the Krein gap.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on translationally invariant systems and the specific class of quadratic bosonic Hamiltonians; it does not fully account for finite-size effects or open boundary conditions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates a landscape of QBHs, distinguishing between thermodynamic and dynamical stability and showing how the quasiparticle vacuum remains relevant across different stability regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of stability, defines the quasiparticle vacuum (QPV) and the Krein gap, analyzes the nature of spectral singularities (EPs and KCs), proves the uniqueness of the QPV, and demonstrates the scaling of entanglement and correlations near critical points.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

For a many-body system in equilibrium, described by a thermodynamically stable Hamiltonian, quantum criticality is associated with structural changes of the many-body ground state. However, there exist physically relevant models, notably, certain quadratic bosonic Hamiltonians (QBHs), which fail to have a ground state. QBHs can be dynamically stable or unstable. We show the notion of criticality is meaningful for the entire class of QBHs that are dynamically stable or at the boundary of instability, regardless of thermodynamic stability, and that the key state for such QBHs is a naturally and unambiguously defined quasiparticle vacuum (QPV). This state is Gaussian, and coincides with the ground state if the QBH is thermodynamically stable. We identify a relevant spectral gap, the Krein gap, associated to the minimal spectral separation between creation and annihilation operators, and show that the QPV is unique when the Krein gap is positive. We prove that, for dynamically stable QBHs with finite-range couplings, correlations are exponentially bounded unless the Krein gap closes, which is associated with one of two spectral degeneracies: an exceptional point or a Krein collision. Consequently, long-range QPV correlations can ensue. Thus, the Krein gap takes the role of the spectral gap for dynamically stable QBHs, and the boundary of dynamical stability and criticality (associated to exceptional points) or multicriticality (associated to Krein collisions) are the same. We also find that bosonic critical behavior beyond thermodynamic stability is witnessed by the scaling of the entanglement entropy and other indicators of equilibrium criticality from information geometry. Our framework opens the door to investigating all dynamically stable QBHs through the lens of critical phenomena, including thermodynamically unstable ones from photonics, cavity-QED, and magnonics.

</details>

### [GHZ is All You Need: Quantum Sensing with VISTA](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04203v1)

**Authors:** Oskar Novak, Christos N. Gagatsos, Narayanan Rengaswamy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04203v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04203_figures/2605.04203_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. A diagram summarizing the VISTA metrology pro- tocol. First, the probe state is prepared and allowed to pas- sively evolve under the influence of the magnetic field and noise of its surroundings. The quantum state of the probe is then teleported to the registers of a quantum computer, while the ans¨atz state evolves, thereby mimicking the probe state’s evolution using a variational quantum circuit that acts as a digital twin. The quantum computer then performs the swap test over multiple shots, each with a fresh probe state, to compute the Hilbert-Schmidt Inner Product between the circuit ans¨atz and the probe state. Gradients for this quan- tity are computed using the Stochastic...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04203_figures/2605.04203_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. For a moderate noise rate of γ = 0.005, and θ = 0.05, we can achieve near-Heisenberg scaling by employing a shot budget of 105 shots. This gives a scaling law of δθ ≈N −0.88, but this scaling can be improved to N −1 with more shots. However, in the asymptotic limit as N increases, the scaling is expected to converge towards the SQL due to noise. Re- gardless of the large-N behavior, this demonstrates that the VISTA protocol, by leveraging shots to stabilize the gradi- ents of a variational quantum algorithm, can achieve a clear metrological advantage in certain regions. Note that the em- pirical error dips beneath Heisenberg scaling due to variance in the optimizer convergence.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04203_figures/2605.04203_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Averaged Absolute error for a typical range of θ values for different-sized GHZ states with a more aggressive noise rate of γ = 0.05. We use a shot scheduler ranging from 10K to 40K shots per epoch. We notice that due to the higher noise rate, there are diminishing performance returns past N = 10, with an N = 20 GHZ state performing worse than an N = 3 GHZ state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04203_figures/2605.04203_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. FFT for extracting ˆθ from traditional protocol. For the N = 3 probe with noise rate γ = 0.11, θ = 0.23, 2500 shots per time step, and 200 timesteps for a total time of t = 1. We find ˆθ = 0.75, which is within the SQL for an N = 3 GHZ state. In comparison, our protocol, using the same parameters, yields ˆθV IST A = 0.223.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04203_figures/2605.04203_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. A diagram summarizing the cascade approach for scaling to large N.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents VISTA, a new variational protocol for quantum sensing that uses a 'quantum twin' circuit to mimic noisy system dynamics. By using physics-informed optimization and a new quasi-normalization technique, it overcomes barren plateaus and achieves near-Heisenberg scaling even in the presence of environmental noise. This approach allows for the simultaneous and efficient extraction of both signal and noise parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a practical, resource-efficient framework for near-term quantum sensors by combining passive evolution with variational optimization, specifically addressing the challenge of parameter estimation in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Improving the precision of quantum parameter estimation (metrology) in the presence of environmental noise, which typically degrades scaling from the Heisenberg Limit to the Standard Quantum Limit.

**Main result.** The introduction of the VISTA protocol, which achieves near-Heisenberg scaling for moderate noise and enables simultaneous estimation of signal and noise parameters using a physics-informed variational approach.

**Method.** A closed-loop variational protocol (VISTA) using a 'quantum twin' ansatz and a Swap Test to compare a passively evolving probe to a parameterized circuit, enhanced by a 'Quasi-Normalization' technique to sharpen gradients.

**Model / system.** An N-qubit GHZ state evolving under a Lindbladian master equation involving dephasing and amplitude damping channels, with a Hamiltonian proportional to the signal parameter theta.

**Key observables.** Hilbert-Schmidt inner product, Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), parameter error (delta theta), and purity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Signal parameter (theta), noise rate (gamma), number of qubits (N), and the Heisenberg vs. Standard Quantum Limits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The achievement of near-Heisenberg scaling may not persist in the asymptotic N to infinity limit; assumes availability of high shot counts for gradient stability.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: VISTA protocol diagram; Fig 2: Error scaling vs N; Fig 5: FFT spectrum for traditional protocol; Fig 6-7: Dephasing and Amplitude Damping ansätze; Fig 9-10: Impact of Quasi-Normalization on loss gradients and convergence; Fig 14: Vector metrology results.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the metrology problem and limitations of existing methods; description of the VISTA framework and quantum twin ansatz; introduction of the Quasi-Normalization technique; demonstration of performance in dephasing and amplitude damping models; extension to multi-parameter vector metrology; mathematical derivation of QFI lower bounds in the appendix.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum metrology holds the potential to enhance magnetic field sensing beyond current limits. However, in the presence of realistic noise, this advantage degrades to the Standard Quantum Limit. While recent algorithmic and variational techniques attempt to recover this scaling, they are hindered by stringent control requirements on the probe state that are infeasible in the near term, or by barren plateaus and interpretability issues inherent to black-box variational quantum circuits. Here, we introduce Variational Inference and Sensing with Twin Ansätze (VISTA), a closed-loop protocol that combines passive sensing, or where the probe state is left to evolve without any active control, with physics-informed variational optimization. In the VISTA framework, a probe state evolves under a Lindbladian master-equation, and is compared, via the Swap test, to a parameterized ``quantum twin", a shallow quantum circuit designed to mimic the underlying pure-state or Lindbladian master-equation dynamics. By restricting the optimization space to the physical parameters of interest, VISTA circumvents barren plateaus. We demonstrate that by coupling the protocol with a classical optimizer and high shot counts, VISTA can temporarily achieve near-Heisenberg scaling for moderately noisy qubits over a finite range of system sizes. Furthermore, we introduce a Quasi-Normalization technique that sharpens the loss gradients, enabling simultaneous extraction of both the coherent signal $θ$ and the environmental noise rate $γ$ with low absolute error. Finally, we extend VISTA to the multi-parameter vector metrology regime, enabling simultaneous parameter extraction from a transverse-magnetic-field Hamiltonian. By eliminating the need for complex, open-loop control and processing, VISTA offers a highly practical, resource-efficient framework for near- to intermediate-term quantum sensors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04571"></a>
### [Causal-Order Identification of Memoryless Sequential Quantum Processes from Restricted Projective Data](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04571v1)

**Authors:** Masahito Hayashi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04571v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04571_figures/2605.04571_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Individual (product) strategy: Charlie prepares two independent input states ρ1 and ρ2 and sends them separately to Alice and Bob. No correlation or sequential mediation is introduced between the two players.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04571_figures/2605.04571_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Parallel strategy: Charlie prepares a bipartite input state ρ12 (possibly entangled) and distributes its two subsystems to Alice and Bob in parallel. The correlation comes only from the shared input state, not from a sequential influence between the parties.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04571_figures/2605.04571_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Sequential memoryless strategy (1 →2): Charlie first sends an input state ρ1 to Alice, then transforms Alice’s output into Bob’s input through a single CPTP map Λ1→2. The defining feature is that the mediation from Alice to Bob is described by one fixed channel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04571_figures/2605.04571_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Sequential strategy with maximized spacing: ρ12 and Charlie are centered exactly between Alice and the joint processor.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper provides a complete set of criteria to identify whether a quantum process is memoryless and sequential when only partial measurement data is available. It introduces a new algebraic consistency requirement that is essential to distinguish true sequential dynamics from processes with hidden memory. The findings are demonstrated through a tractable two-qubit Pauli model.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum networks, as it provides fundamental limits on how much causal structure can be inferred from incomplete experimental data.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the causal order and memory properties of quantum processes when available measurement statistics are not tomographically complete.

**Main result.** The author establishes that a distribution is compatible with a memoryless sequential process if and only if it satisfies directional Markovianity, positivity of the reconstructed Choi matrix, and a new algebraic consistency condition (C1).

**Method.** The paper uses the process-matrix framework, pseudo-density matrix (PDM) formalism, and distribution-level analysis to identify set-membership for different strategy classes.

**Model / system.** A sequential quantum process involving two players (Alice and Bob) interacting via an unobserved environment, specifically analyzed within a two-qubit Pauli setting.

**Key observables.** Local measurement statistics, joint input-output probability distributions, and temporal correlations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Two-qubit Pauli regime, restricted projective measurements, and the dimension of the quotient space of the process matrix.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes restricted projective measurements and a non-tomographic regime where the underlying process cannot be reconstructed exactly.

**Figures summary.** Not specified.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of causal identification under restricted data, develops a mathematical framework using PDMs and Markovian chains, proposes a new algebraic consistency condition (C1), applies the theory to the two-qubit Pauli setting to prove the necessity of C1, and concludes with a discussion on the impossibility of full process tomography in this regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Identifying causal order from restricted projective data is generally nontrivial. When two quantum players interact only through an unobserved environment, the available local measurement statistics are typically not tomographically complete, so the underlying process cannot in general be reconstructed exactly from the observed distribution. As a result, causal direction can be statistically identifiable in some cases but fundamentally indistinguishable in others.   In this work, we determine necessary and sufficient conditions for deciding when an observed distribution is compatible with a memoryless sequential quantum process in a fixed direction. We show that directional conditional-independence structure and the positivity criterion based on the pseudo-density matrix, as developed in recent work by Liu, Qiu, Dahlsten, and Vedral, are not sufficient by themselves. The missing ingredient is an additional algebraic consistency requirement, and together these conditions yield a complete criterion for membership in the memoryless sequential class.   We then specialize to the two-qubit Pauli setting, where the problem remains non-tomographic but becomes explicitly tractable. In this regime, we characterize when the two sequential directions are statistically indistinguishable, and we show by example that positivity alone does not exclude more general memoryful strategies, whereas the additional algebraic consistency requirement does.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04598"></a>
### [Two-site Bose-Hubbard hopping and Schrödinger cat states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04598v1)

**Authors:** Madeline Berezowski, Artur Sowa, Jonas Fransson  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04598v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04598_figures/2605.04598_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Eigenvalues and eigenvectors of H|H⊙k for k = 0,1,2,3,... .</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04598_figures/2605.04598_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. A visualization of how a vector |x⟩can be represented in the orthonormal eigenbasis of H, i.e., how the coefficients can be calculated by subsequent applications of ˆck and ˆck.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides an exact analytical solution for the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the hopping term in a two-site Bose-Hubbard model. It shows that the square of this hopping Hamiltonian can periodically transform coherent states into Schrödinger cat states. Additionally, it uncovers a new method for computing the properties of the spin x-projection operator.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides an analytical framework for generating non-classical, macroscopically distinguishable superpositions (cat states) using a simple two-site model, which is highly relevant to quantum state engineering and many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the hopping term in a two-site Bose-Hubbard model and study the generation of Schrödinger cat states.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the square of the dimer hopping Hamiltonian induces periodic evolution of coherent states into macroscopically distinguishable Schrödinger cat states and provide a new method for diagonalizing the spin x-projection operator.

**Method.** An inductive argument using bosonic canonical commutation relations is used to derive eigenvalues and eigenvectors, alongside an analysis of the dynamical evolution under the H^2 operator.

**Model / system.** A Bose-Hubbard dimer consisting of two lattice sites containing k spinless bosons, where the Hamiltonian focus is on the hopping term.

**Key observables.** Energy eigenvalues, probability distribution of energy measurements, and the formation of Schrödinger cat states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of particles (k), hopping amplitude (t), on-site interaction strength (U), and chemical potential (mu).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the validity of Bose-Hubbard-type commutation relations for transformed operators and focuses specifically on the dynamics induced by the square of the Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the relationship between the number of particles k and the resulting system properties.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the dimer model, presents an inductive proof for the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the hopping Hamiltonian, establishes the equivalence to the spin x-projection operator, and concludes with the dynamical analysis of coherent state evolution into cat states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Bose-Hubbard Hamiltonian can be simplified to have only two lattice sites, in which case the system being described is referred to as a dimer. Due to its structure, the hopping term of the dimer Hamiltonian enjoys invariance in a family of subspaces indexed by a whole number $k$, each subspace corresponding to a system of only $k$ particles. We have invented an inductive argument using the bosonic canonical commutation relations to find the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the dimer hopping Hamiltonian in its $k$-particle subspaces. In particular, this Hamiltonian, when restricted to one of the $k$-particle subspaces, is exactly the spin projection operator along the $x$-axis, where the number of particles $k$ in the dimer system yields the projection matrix for spin quantum number $s=k/2$. Thus, a new method for computing the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the $x$-axis spin projector has been unearthed. We use the explicit construction to study the dynamics of coherent states induced by the square of the dimer hopping hamiltonian. We find that it generates Schrödinger cat states in the two-site setting.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Finite steps optimise dissipation in stochastically controlled quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04681v1)

**Authors:** Theodore McKeever, Harry J. D. Miller, Ahsan Nazir  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04681v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04681_figures/2605.04681_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

Illustration of the quench process with each quench step having two stages, sudden perturbation and then ther- malisation over characteristic timescale δt, where work and heat are exchanged respectively. The controls follow the red line, while faint red lines indicate alternative stochastic tra- jectories. Double-arrowed lines denote small changes.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

</details>

</details>

<sub>Figure 5. Wdiss [∆] against number of protocol steps N for different zero-mean Gaussian noise sources. **Summary.** The paper demonstrates that classical noise in quantum control protocols prevents the use of infinitely slow processes by introducing new dissipative costs. It identifies a finite optimal number of control steps that balances deterministic and stochastic dissipation, providing a new guideline for energy-efficient quantum machine design.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a practical limit on how 'slow' a quantum control protocol can be before noise-induced heating outweighs the benefits of quasi-static driving.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the optimal number of steps in a quantum step-equilibration protocol to minimize thermodynamic dissipation when the control fields are subject to classical stochastic noise.

**Main result.** The authors show that while deterministic dissipation decreases with more steps, stochastic noise causes dissipation to increase linearly with the number of steps, leading to a finite optimal number of steps (N_opt) and overturning the intuition that slower protocols are always more efficient.

**Method.** The study utilizes stochastic linear response theory, quantum thermodynamic geometry, and the Cumulant Generating Function (CGF) to derive bounds on dissipated work and work fluctuations.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to a driven qubit with strong system-bath coupling (Landau-Zener model) and a 1D transverse-field Ising model (TFIM) undergoing a Landauer erasure protocol.

**Key observables.** Dissipated work (W_diss), work variance (sigma^2_W), thermodynamic length (L), and magnetization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of steps (N), noise variance (Phi), inverse temperature (beta), and noise processes (Gaussian White Noise, Wiener, and Ornstein-Uhlenbeck).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Weak Gaussian noise approximation, linear response regime, and a timescale separation between control and thermalization.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the quench process; Figure 5 compares dissipation scaling across different noise models (GWN, AR(1), Wiener); Figure 9 shows temperature dependence of optimal work and dissipation rates.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of stochastic control, develops a geometric framework for dissipation and fluctuations, applies the theory to specific noise models, and validates results using qubit and TFIM models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Motivated by the need for precise, energy-efficient, and experimentally realistic quantum control protocols, we investigate the thermodynamic cost of performing quantum step-equilibration processes under the influence of classical stochastic control fields. Whereas purely deterministic protocols exhibit dissipation that scales inversely with the number of steps, we show that weak Gaussian noise in the control variables induces dissipative contributions that grow linearly with the number of steps. Consequently, we derive the finite optimal number of steps and minimal achievable average dissipated work and its variance using the quantum thermodynamic length. These results are demonstrated using two paradigmatic examples: a Landau-Zener sweep of a qubit strongly coupled to a thermal bath and the erasure of a transverse-field Ising model.

</details>

### [Geometrical control of topology with orbital angular momentum modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05002v1)

**Authors:** Yunjia Zhai, Anselmo M. Marques, Ricardo G. Dias, Verònica Ahufinger, David Viedma  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05002v1>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. (a) Winding number W as a function of the angle ϕ. The vertical dashed line marks the critical angle ϕc ≈5π/16 where W changes and the red crosses mark ϕ = π/10 and ϕ = 9π/20. Energy spectrum for Nc = 10 unit cells with (b) ϕ = π/10, showing a case with W = −2 and four topological edge states (hollow symbols) and (c) ϕ = 9π/20, with W = −1 and two topological edge states (hollow symbols). **Summary.** This paper explores how the geometry of a 1D lattice, specifically the angle between sites, can be used to control topological phases in systems with orbital angular momentum. By mapping the system to a Creutz ladder, the authors show that tuning the angle induces transitions between different winding numbers. They propose and simulate a photonic waveguide implementation where these transitions are visible through changes in light localization.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a novel way to achieve geometric control over topological phases using OAM, which is highly relevant for designing robust topological photonic circuits and synthetic dimension engineering in quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the topological properties of a 1D staggered lattice can be geometrically controlled by tuning the relative angle between lattice sites using orbital angular momentum (OAM) modes.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that tuning the interleg angle can induce topological phase transitions between different winding number regimes (W=0, -1, -2) and that these transitions are accompanied by observable band inversion and changes in light localization.

**Method.** The authors use analytical derivation of the Bloch Hamiltonian and winding number, numerical exact diagonalization for energy spectra, and finite-element simulations (COMSOL) for a photonic waveguide implementation.

**Model / system.** A 1D staggered lattice loaded with OAM states (l=1) that can be mapped to a Creutz ladder model in a synthetic dimension, with a proposed experimental implementation in a zig-zag array of coupled cylindrical optical waveguides.

**Key observables.** Winding number (W), number of topologically protected edge states, band inversion signature (sublattice-exchange operator expectation value), and light propagation/localization behavior.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Relative angle (phi), hopping strengths (J2, J2', J3, J3'), and the ratio of coupling strengths.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on the regime phi in (0, pi/2) and assumes the winding number is well-defined (i.e., the bulk energy gap does not close).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the lattice and Creutz ladder mapping; Fig 2 presents phase diagrams of the winding number; Fig 3 and 6 show energy spectra and edge state emergence; Fig 7 and 8 demonstrate band structures for different lattice geometries; Fig 9 simulates electric field propagation in a waveguide with a varying angle.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical model and its mapping to a Creutz ladder, provides analytical and numerical derivations of topological invariants and phase transitions, proposes a photonic waveguide implementation, and validates the theory with light propagation simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study how the topological properties of a one-dimensional staggered lattice, loaded into states with orbital angular momentum $l=1$, can be controlled simply by tuning the relative angle between sites. The original system under consideration can be depicted as a Creutz ladder model when unwrapping the different state circulations in a synthetic dimension. Depending on the hopping strengths of the chain, different topological regimes may be accessed by changing the ladder angle, as determined by the value of the winding number of the chain. We analytically and numerically explore the different available regimes, and determine the number of topologically protected edge states that exist in each case. We also study the emergence of band inversion across topological transitions and show that it agrees with the winding number calculations, thus serving as an additional topological marker. Then, we propose a realistic experimental implementation in a photonic waveguide system, where the topological transition manifests as a sudden change of the behavior of the propagation of light in the system.

</details>
