**Summary.** This paper provides an analytical framework to calculate how oscillating quantum states tunnel through a barrier. It reveals that instead of a smooth decay, coherent states undergo tunneling in periodic, intense bursts of probability current. This work bridges the gap between stationary decay models and the dynamic tunneling seen in systems like Josephson junctions or cosmological bubble nucleation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics, as it provides an analytical tool to understand how phase coherence and initial state dynamics drive non-exponential, time-dependent decay in metastable systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the time-dependent decay rate and tunneling probability of general, non-stationary initial states (specifically coherent/oscillating states) out of a metastable potential well.

**Main result.** The authors derive a closed-form expression for the probability current that captures interference effects and show that tunneling for coherent states occurs in narrow, periodic spikes when the wave packet reaches the classical turning point.

**Method.** The study uses a resonant state expansion (Gamow-Siegert states) combined with semiclassical WKB approximations and the saddle-point method, validated against numerical solutions of the Schrödinger equation using a complex absorbing potential.

**Model / system.** A quantum mechanical particle in a metastable potential well (such as a superconducting Josephson junction or a cosmological scalar field) separated from a free region by a potential barrier.

**Key observables.** Probability current (tunneling rate), time-dependent decay rate, and total leaked probability.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Semiclassical limit (large action), narrow-resonance regime, oscillation period, and the amplitude of the coherent state.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial state is localized in the well; the expansion neglects the non-resonant continuum; the potential is approximated quadratically near the well bottom; and the results are valid for energies well below the barrier top.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the potential geometry and turning points; Figure 2 displays resonant states; Figure 3 compares different boundary conditions; Figure 4 and 5 demonstrate the accuracy of the proposed formula and the Gaussian shape of tunneling spikes.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the problem of non-vacuum decay, introduces the resonant state expansion and WKB framework, applies this to coherent states to derive periodic tunneling spikes, validates the analytical results with numerical simulations, and discusses the limits of the approximation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the decay of general initial states out of a metastable potential well in quantum mechanics. We provide a closed-form expression for the probability current that tunnels through the barrier in terms of the resonant states into which the initial state can be decomposed. All ingredients in the equation are computed analytically to first subleading order in the semiclassical limit. Specializing to a coherently-oscillating initial state, we derive an approximation to the time-dependent decay rate and demonstrate its accuracy by comparing it to a numerical solution of the Schrödinger equation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04654"></a>
### [Efficient Multi-Controlled Gate Implementation in Trapped-Ion Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04654v1)

**Authors:** Minhyeok Kang, Taejin Kim, Jungsoo Hong, Joonsuk Huh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04654v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04654_figures/2605.04654_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Energy level structure of the 171Yb+ ion relevant to the Cirac–Zoller scheme. The hyperfine clock states define the qubit basis |g⟩and |e⟩, while an additional Zeeman sublevel is used as the auxiliary state |f⟩. Two Raman beams couple the 2S1/2 ground-state manifold via virtual excitation to the 2P1/2 level, inducing transitions |g⟩↔|e⟩and |g⟩↔|f⟩. When tuned to the RSB, the Raman beams also drive spin–motion coupling with the vibrational mode. Adapted from Ref. [9].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04654_figures/2605.04654_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Implementation of the 4-Toffoli gate using the Cirac–Zoller scheme. (a) Circuit diagram of the 4- Toffoli gate. (b) Standard Cirac–Zoller scheme for 4-Toffoli gate, constructed solely from RSB π pulses [9]. (c)– (e) Alternative implementations obtained by exploiting the RSB gauge freedom, yielding equivalent operations with different pulse sequences.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04654_figures/2605.04654_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Implementation of a 3-controlled gate using the Cirac–Zoller based scheme. (a) Circuit diagram of a 3-controlled gate. (b–e) Decompositions of the circuit in (a) using four different types of RSB sequence variants. In (b) and (c), a Z gate (indicated by the black dashed box) is placed at one of two symmetric locations to compensate for the residual phase. In (d) and (e), where the encoding and decoding RSB sequences have opposite signs, the Z gate is not required.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04654_figures/2605.04654_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Implementation of N-CSWAP gate. (a) The N-CSWAP gate can be decomposed into three (N + 2)- Toffoli gates. (b) Implementation of three (N + 2)-Toffoli gates. 4N RSB pulses inside the red dashed-line boxes can be eliminated by choosing an appropriate quantum circuit from Fig. 2 (b–e) for each (N + 2)-Toffoli gate. (c) The resulting circuit for the N-CSWAP gate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04654_figures/2605.04654_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Implementation of two multi-controlled gates with different control conditions. (a) Circuit diagram of the two gates, which can be decomposed into multi-controlled gates with the same control condition and X gates. (b) The first and second multi-controlled gates are implemented using the quantum circuit shown in Fig. 3 (d). The RSB pulses within the red dashed-line box can be eliminated. (c) The resulting circuit corresponding to (b).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes an efficient way to implement multi-controlled gates in trapped-ion systems by exploiting the sign freedom in red-sideband pulses. By canceling redundant pulses, the authors significantly reduce gate time and error accumulation, specifically improving the scalability of the Linear Combination of Unitaries (LCU) method.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it demonstrates how hardware-native pulse engineering can directly mitigate decoherence channels like motional heating and dephasing in trapped-ion processors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Implementing multi-controlled gates in trapped-ion systems is resource-intensive due to high gate depth and susceptibility to noise like motional heating and dephasing.

**Main result.** The authors developed a pulse-level implementation that exploits RSB gauge freedom to cancel redundant pulses, reducing gate time by ~40% and improving fidelity, while also reducing LCU select operator complexity from O(L log L) to O(L).

**Method.** The study uses pulse-level engineering of Red-Sideband (RSB) pulses in the Cirac-Zoller scheme, applying numerical simulations via the Lindblad master equation and QuTiP to evaluate performance under realistic noise.

**Model / system.** The system is a trapped-ion platform (specifically modeled after 171Yb+) where qubits are encoded in hyperfine clock states and entanglement is mediated via collective vibrational modes using RSB pulses.

**Key observables.** State fidelity of computational basis output states, gate execution time, and RSB pulse count scaling.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rabi frequency Omega/2pi = 0.2 MHz, Lamb-Dicke parameter eta = 0.1, motional heating, and motional dephating rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The pulse cancellation scaling derivation assumes L = 2^N (full binary control space); the model assumes a single shared motional mode and a truncated Fock space (n_max = 10).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the energy level structure; Figure 2 illustrates RSB gauge freedom; Figure 3 shows the ancilla-free circuit; Figure 4 demonstrates N-CSWAP pulse cancellation; Figures 7-11 detail LCU implementation and pulse count scaling; Figure 12 compares state fidelities across different iterations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of multi-controlled gate overhead, presents the discovery of RSB gauge freedom, details the pulse cancellation and ancilla-free construction methods, applies these to the LCU method, provides numerical validation under noise, and concludes with scaling analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multi-controlled gates are essential primitives in quantum algorithms, yet implementing them via standard gate-level decompositions remains resource-intensive. We develop efficient pulse-level implementations of multi-controlled gates in trapped-ion systems using the Cirac-Zoller scheme. We first show that the Cirac-Zoller construction admits a freedom in the sign choice of red-sideband (RSB) pulses, which leaves the logical operation invariant up to a local Pauli-$Z$ correction. By exploiting this freedom, we construct equivalent realizations of multi-controlled gates and develop pulse cancellation for more efficient implementations of successive gates. We perform numerical simulations and show that pulse cancellation reduces the gate time and improves the state fidelity. Furthermore, we propose ancilla-free circuits for general $N$-controlled gates that use a single-controlled gate primitive and $\mathcal{O}(N)$ RSB pulses. As a key application, we apply our pulse cancellation to the linear combination of unitaries (LCU) method for block encoding. We show that the RSB-pulse cost of the select operator over $L$ unitaries can be reduced from $\mathcal{O}(L\log L)$ to $\mathcal{O}(L)$, which improves the efficiency and scalability of LCU-based quantum circuits.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04981"></a>
### [Exact identification of unknown unitary processes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04981v1)

**Authors:** Santiago Llorens, Arnau Diebra, Michal Sedlák, Ramon Muñoz-Tapia  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04981v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04981_figures/2605.04981_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic representation of the most general causal sequential tester. Tested devices are shown in blue and tester elements in orange. Causal order proceeds from left to right.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04981_figures/2605.04981_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 7. Bratteli diagram for n = m = 3 and d = 2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a mathematical framework for detecting unknown faults in a chain of quantum devices. It proves that for qubits, the ability to identify faulty components depends only on how many are broken, not how many healthy ones are in the chain, and provides an exact formula for the success probability.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The exact identification of faulty quantum devices within a series of n channels, where a subset of k devices applies an unknown, Haar-random unitary transformation.

**Main result.** The authors derive optimal success probabilities for zero-error anomaly detection, finding that for qubits, the success probability depends only on the number of anomalies k and is independent of the total number of devices n.

**Method.** The problem is framed using quantum testers and the Choi-Jamiołkowski isomorphism, solved via Semidefinite Programming (SDP) and advanced representation theory including mixed Schur-Weyl duality and the Walled Brauer Algebra.

**Model / system.** A sequence of n quantum channels, each acting on a d-dimensional Hilbert space, where k devices are anomalous and apply an unknown unitary W, while the others apply a known unitary V.

**Key observables.** Success probability (Ps) of the zero-error identification protocol.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of total devices (n), number of anomalous devices (k), and local dimension of the systems (d).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The unknown unitary is assumed to be Haar-random (complete ignorance); the number of anomalies k is known; and the error is purely unitary and agnostic.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the problem setup; Figure 2 illustrates the optimal parallel protocol using entangled states; Figure 3 displays Young diagrams for irreducible representations; Figure 4 and 5 demonstrate the Walled Brauer Algebra diagrammatic calculus.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the anomaly detection problem, analyzes the single-anomaly case, extends to the two-anomaly case for qubits, provides a general analytical expression for arbitrary k and d using representation theory, and concludes with an algebraic framework in the appendix.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The accurate identification of faulty hardware is a fundamental requirement for reliable quantum information processing. We address this problem in a quantum setting, where a series of $n$ devices is intended to apply the same unitary operation, but $k$ malfunctioning devices among them apply a different, unknown unitary action. Under the assumption of complete ignorance regarding the specific unitary transformation applied, we model our hypotheses using representation-theoretic tools and study the zero-error protocol for identifying these faulty devices. We derive the optimal success probability for the single- and two-anomaly scenarios, demonstrating that it is independent of the total number of devices in the series. Furthermore, we present a simple protocol that makes use of ancillary systems that achieves this optimal limit. Notably, this protocol offers significant operational advantages, such as allowing us to test each device independently. Finally, we extend our analysis to the general scenario in which both the number of anomalies and the local dimension of the systems are arbitrary, evaluating our protocol's performance and conjecturing its global optimality in the general case.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04414"></a>
### [Uniform Mixing in Chiral Quantum Walks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04414v1)

**Authors:** Luke Levine, Jessy Jacob Mesapam, Benjamin Mustico, Christino Tamon, Gabriel Tucker, Hanmeng Zhan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04414v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04414_figures/2605.04414_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: The chiral ghost trick: K6 morphs into K1,5.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04414_figures/2605.04414_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: A tale of three uniform mixing times: (a) K4 at π/4. (b) K1,3 at 2π/3 √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04414_figures/2605.04414_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Oriented graphs with average uniform mixing: (a) Transitive tournament Tn. (b) Oriented odd-cycle ⃗C2n+1.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that by using oriented (chiral) graphs, one can achieve uniform mixing in quantum walks where it was previously thought impossible. It provides new mathematical tools to reduce global mixing analysis to local measurements and identifies specific families of graphs that violate classical 'no-go' theorems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is interesting for researchers in quantum information and open quantum systems because it provides new methods for controlling state distributions and explores how broken symmetry (chirality) can be used to achieve mixing properties impossible in symmetric systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the conditions for uniform mixing and average uniform mixing in continuous-time quantum walks, specifically looking to overcome existing 'no-go' theorems by using chiral (oriented) graphs.

**Main result.** The authors prove that signed complete graphs can achieve probabilistic uniform mixing and that certain oriented circulants exhibit average uniform mixing, violating Godsil's no-go theorem.

**Method.** The study employs unitary signings, complex equitable partitions, and a stopping rule based on weak measurements to reduce global mixing problems to local ones.

**Model / system.** The model consists of continuous-time quantum walks on graphs (including complete, Hamming, and circulant graphs) where the Hamiltonian is defined by the adjacency matrix, potentially with complex edge weights (chiral/oriented graphs).

**Key observables.** Uniform mixing, average uniform mixing (Cesaro limit of the mixing matrix), and mixing time.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Unitary signing (sigma), vertex multiplicity, and the time (t) at which the distribution becomes uniform.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the use of complex edge weights (unitary signing) to bypass restrictions present in standard undirected graphs; it also notes a limitation regarding non-abelian groups due to irreducible representation degrees.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 is a comparison table of quantum uniform mixing, and Figure 5 shows small examples of circulant graphs exhibiting average uniform mixing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of uniform mixing, presents a reduction technique via a stopping rule, analyzes specific graph families (complete, Hamming, and circulant graphs), and concludes with a no-go theorem for non-abelian Cayley graphs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper studies uniform mixing in continuous-time quantum walks. We show that for some unitary signing $σ$ the complete graph $K^σ_n$ has probabilistic uniform mixing. In contrast, it is known {\em no} complete graph has uniform mixing except for $K_2$, $K_3$, and $K_4$. Our technique is based on a stopping rule for quantum walks which reduces global to local uniform mixing. As a special case, we found an orientation of $H(n,4)$ that mixes to uniform faster than any other Hamming graphs. We also show that there are infinite families of oriented circulants with {\em average} uniform mixing. This is a chiral violation of Godsil's {\em No-Go} theorem which states that no graph has average uniform mixing except for $K_2$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04855"></a>
### [W-state graphs: Structure and Algorithms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04855v1)

**Authors:** Rishikesh Gajjala, Saurabh Ray, Dimitrios M. Thilikos  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04855v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Dicke superradiance` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04855_figures/2605.04855_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Coloured examples of W-cones.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04855_figures/2605.04855_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: A 6-vertex W-state graph. Vertices 11′ and 22′ act as the 2-vertex cut and the decomposition along this cut gives two new W-state graphs shown in Figure 3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04855_figures/2605.04855_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Block decomposition of the W-state in Figure 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04855_figures/2605.04855_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: A retained W-union of the graphs in Figure 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04855_figures/2605.04855_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Construction illustrated on H = P3. Vertices v0, v1 are the two copies of each v ∈VH; vertices a, b are virtual.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper provides a complete structural and algorithmic characterization of graphs that can realize multipartite W-states in photonic experiments. It introduces a polynomial-time recognition algorithm and proves that generalizing these structural properties to Dicke states is computationally hard (coNP-complete).

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for quantum optics and photonic quantum computing as it provides the fundamental combinatorial rules for designing experimental setups that generate specific multipartite entangled states like W-states and Dicke states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to provide a complete structural characterization and recognition algorithm for W-state graphs, which are edge-colored graphs representing quantum photonic experiments that generate multipartite W-states.

**Main result.** The authors prove that W-state graphs can be decomposed into 3-connected building blocks called W-cones and provide an efficient O(|V|*|E|) recognition algorithm. They also prove that verifying the corresponding condition for Dicke states is coNP-complete.

**Method.** The study uses classical matching theory, specifically the decomposition of matching-covered graphs into 3-connected components (using SPQR-trees) and the properties of factor-critical graphs.

**Model / system.** The model is an experiment-graph framework for quantum photonics, where vertices represent optical modes, edges represent photon-pair sources (crystals), and half-edge colors represent the state of the photon in a specific arm.

**Key observables.** Coincidence events (existence of perfect matchings) and vertex coloring (representing excited vs. ground states).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Hamming weight k for Dicke state generalizations and the number of vertices n for W-states.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study abstracts away physical amplitudes and phases to focus on the purely combinatorial properties of the graph.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows examples of W-cones; Figure 2 and 3 illustrate the 2-vertex cut decomposition of a W-state graph into W-blocks; Figure 4 demonstrates the W-union operation; Figure 5 shows a construction for the complexity reduction proof.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with definitions of W-state graphs and W-cones, provides a structural characterization via 3-connected decomposition, develops recognition and W-colorability algorithms using dynamic programming on SPQR-trees, and concludes with a complexity analysis of Dicke state generalizations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the class of edge-coloured graphs arising from the graph-theoretic representation of quantum photonic experiments that generate multipartite W-states. Abstracting away physical amplitudes and phases, we introduce W-state graphs: matching-covered graphs equipped with a half-edge 2-colouring such that every perfect matching contains exactly one bichromatic edge and every vertex is incident with a red half-edge. Our main contribution is a complete structural characterization of W-state graphs. We show that a graph is a W-state graph if and only if each of its 3-connected components is a W-cone, a simple and rigid building block defined by a universal vertex and a factor-critical base. This characterization implies that no W-state graph is simple and yields a recognition algorithm running as fast as verifying whether a graph is matching-covered. We also show that the natural generalization to Dicke states encounters a complexity barrier: verifying one of the two Dicke state conditions is itself coNP-complete, resolving an open problem of Vardi and Zhang [IJCAI 2023]. Our results place W-state graphs firmly within classical matching theory and precisely delineate the combinatorial structures capable of realizing idealized W-states in the experiment-graph framework.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04582"></a>
### [Fundamental Limitations of Post-Quantum Cryptographic Architectures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04582v1)

**Authors:** Jiho Jung, Donghwa Ji, Mingyu Lee, Kabgyun Jeong  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04582v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04582_figures/2605.04582_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: The algebraic structure of the LWE problem, where an artificial error vector e is added to the linear combination of matrix A and secret vector s (b = As + e) to mimic a noisy linear system, rendering the exact inversion of s from b computationally intractable under standard classical algorithms.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04582_figures/2605.04582_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Conceptual diagram of computational complexity classes. The LWE problem belongs to NP, but it is not NP-Complete. The entire security paradigm of current post-quantum cryptography relies precariously on the unproven assumption that specific lattice problems do not intersect with the BQP class.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04582_figures/2605.04582_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Structural correspondence between Shannon’s Communication Model and LWE. While the algorithmic error term (e) is designed to obstruct cryptographic inversion, it functions strictly as channel noise from an information-theoretic standpoint. Shannon’s theorem dictates that noise within the channel capacity is fundamentally correctable through optimal decoding.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper argues that the security of lattice-based post-quantum cryptography is not mathematically absolute but depends on the current difficulty of performing complex quantum state preparations. By framing LWE noise as a type of error that can be corrected using GKP-style protocols, the authors suggest that advancements in quantum error correction could eventually undermine these cryptographic foundations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep connection between cryptographic noise and physical noise in continuous-variable quantum systems, suggesting that quantum error correction protocols could be used to break lattice-based encryption.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper challenges the claim that lattice-based cryptography, specifically the Learning with Errors (LWE) paradigm, is unconditionally secure against quantum adversaries.

**Main result.** The authors argue that LWE security relies on transient physical and engineering bottlenecks, such as the difficulty of state preparation and QRAM access, rather than an impenetrable mathematical boundary, as the injected noise can be treated as a correctable displacement error.

**Method.** The study uses a combination of quantum complexity theory (T-depth analysis), information-theoretic thermodynamics (Landauer's principle), and quantum error correction analogies (GKP codes) to analyze the vulnerability of LWE.

**Model / system.** The model treats LWE samples as a coherent physical superposition state and maps the cryptographic Gaussian noise to continuous-variable displacement errors in a quantum Hilbert space, comparable to GKP error-correcting architectures.

**Key observables.** Probability amplitude of the target basis state, interference patterns of the error distribution, and T-depth complexity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Integer modulus q, dimension n, error distribution chi, and the T-depth of quantum gate layers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The argument assumes that a sufficiently coherent quantum computer can successfully map classical LWE instances into a continuous-variable Hilbert space.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the algebraic structure of the LWE problem with added error; Figure 2 illustrates complexity classes and the LWE/BQP relationship; Figure 3 compares Shannon's communication model to the LWE framework.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from the algebraic definition of LWE to a quantum superposition model, applies the QFT to demonstrate error amplification, identifies state preparation as a physical bottleneck, and concludes by mapping LWE to error-correctable continuous-variable systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Modern lattice-based cryptography, particularly the learning with errors paradigm, relies on injecting artificial noise to secure data against quantum adversaries. This study systematically examines the theoretical and physical boundaries of this noise-reliant model across four interconnected domains: computational complexity, information-theoretic thermodynamics, quantum error correction, and quantum learning theory. Starting from the algorithmic foundation, our analysis notes that these frameworks rely on provisional complexity-theoretic assumptions that remain vulnerable to future quantum algorithmic advancements. Furthermore, by translating this cryptographic mechanism into physical thermodynamics, we illustrate that intentionally injected discrete Gaussian noise does not equate to the permanent erasure of information. Because the structural integrity of the cryptographic secret remains preserved within the ciphertext, advanced quantum error correction protocols and quantum learning models can efficiently extract the underlying mathematical kernel. Ultimately, we suggest that while lattice-based cryptography provides a robust transitional alternative, definitively classifying these frameworks as unconditionally post-quantum represents a premature classification relying on transient physical bottlenecks rather than impenetrable theoretical boundaries.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04951"></a>
### [Scalar and Vector Airborne Platform Calibration Using Quantum and Classical Magnetometers and Inertial Sensors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04951v1)

**Summary.** This paper evaluates how different magnetometer calibration models perform under realistic airborne conditions. It demonstrates that while 3D vector models are highly sensitive to orientation errors, 1D scalar models are much more robust, and it highlights the potential for NV sensors to improve sensor integration in magnetic navigation.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the fundamental error sources in airborne magnetic platform calibration, specifically comparing the robustness of 1D (scalar) versus 3D (vector) Tolles-Lawson calibration models against attitude and sensor errors.

**Main result.** The 1D scalar model is found to be highly robust to attitude errors, whereas the 3D vector model is intrinsically sensitive to first-order attitude inaccuracies. While NV sensors may not solve the 'attitude bottleneck,' they offer advantages in sensor integration and synchronization.

**Method.** The authors use a rigorous theoretical analysis of error dependencies combined with a simulation framework that employs realistic sensor noise models, frequency-domain noise synthesis, and simulated aircraft flight trajectories.

**Model / system.** The system is an airborne platform (aircraft/UAV) equipped with various magnetometers (OPM, Fluxgate, and NV diamond sensors) and an Inertial Navigation System (INS). The magnetic interference is modeled using the Tolles-Lawson model, accounting for permanent, induced, and eddy-current components.

**Key observables.** Calibration residuals (error in nT), total magnetic field, Earth's magnetic field, and attitude/angular errors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Earth's field magnitude (50,000 nT), platform interference field, angular error (alpha), and sensor noise parameters (white noise floor, corner frequency, and spectral slope).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on static platform field components and ignores time-varying electronic/temperature-induced nonlinearities; it also assumes a constant, uniform geomagnetic background field.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the impact of field angle on projection errors; Figure 3/4 detail the sensor noise and error generation pipeline; Figure 6 shows sensor stability (ASD/ADEV); Figure 7/12 illustrate simulated flight trajectories and calibration/validation results.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a theoretical derivation of error dependencies in scalar and vector models, followed by the description of the sensor and noise models, the simulation framework for flight trajectories, a comparison of calibration results, and a discussion of the utility of NV sensors.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Airborne magnetometry requires rigorous calibration to isolate geomagnetic signals from sensor errors and platform magnetic fields. This magnetic compensation is needed for applications like geophysical exploration and magnetic anomaly navigation. The standard approach utilizes a quantum scalar Optically Pumped Magnetometer (OPM) and a less sensitive fluxgate vector sensor for attitude information. This configuration typically results in a scalar approximation of the platform field.   Advancements in high-sensitivity Diamond Nitrogen-Vacancy (NV) vector magnetometers now enable a re-evaluation of the standard hardware configuration and full vector calibration models. We show through rigorous theoretical analysis that scalar calibration models are robust to misalignment. Vector calibration models, however, are intrinsically first-order sensitive to attitude errors, irrespective of the accuracy of the magnetic field measurements. These errors arise from inaccurate representation of the background field direction in the body frame, and can amplify small orientation errors into noticeable calibration residuals.   Using realistic sensor models and flight trajectories, we evaluate different sensor configurations for magnetic calibration and assess the use of onboard Inertial Navigation Systems (INS) as an independent attitude reference to enable stable compensation. Our results suggest that quantum vector magnetometers like NV sensors are not sufficient to solve the attitude bottleneck for airborne vector magnetic calibration. However, as a single sensor capable of providing both absolute field and directional measurements, they may offer benefits regarding sensor placement, synchronization, and scalar calibration accuracy.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04484"></a>
### [Confidence uncertainty: position and momentum can be jointly determined with a guaranteed probability](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04484v1)

**Authors:** Jia-Yi Lin, Xin-Yu Li, Wei Wang, Shengjun Wu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04484v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. Phase diagram for the confidence-uncertainty bound in the (θx, θp) plane. In the lower triangle θx + θp ≤1 no positive lower bound on ∆cx ∆cp exists (Theorem 1); in par- ticular both uncertainties can be made arbitrarily small si- multaneously. In the upper triangle θx + θp &gt; 1 Theorem 2 furnishes the strict positive bound g ≥2π( p</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04484_figures/2605.04484_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Lower bounds on ∆Ix ∆Ip/ℏ. Left: along θx = 1 the implicit Slepian bound 4ℏλ−1 0 (θp) together with the closed- form high-confidence asymptote −2ℏln(1 −θp) [Corollary 1]. Right: along the diagonal θx = θp = θ, the new Landau– Pollak bound strictly dominates the elementary bound, which is restricted to 2θx + θp &gt; 2 and degrades sharply when θx recedes from unity.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04484_figures/2605.04484_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Left: the largest Slepian eigenvalue λ0(c) on a lin- ear scale, with the small-c asymptote 2c/π and the large-c asymptote 1 −4√πc e−2c [27]. Right: 1 −λ0(c) on a log scale showing super-exponential decay. The variable c = LW/(4ℏ) parametrises the time-bandwidth product.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04484_figures/2605.04484_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3 compares the Landau–Pollak bound of Theo- rem 2 against the elementary bound (16). Both bounds agree on θx = 1, but the new one extends to all θx+θp &gt; 1 and is consistently strictly tighter on the diagonal. Ta- ble II samples the tight bound across the (θx, θp)-plane; Fig. 4 displays the resulting landscape.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04484_figures/2605.04484_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Numerical landscape of the tight bound on ∆Ix ∆Ip/ℏover (θx, θp) ∈[0, 1]2. The bound diverges as both confidences approach unity; the dashed line θx + θp = 1 separates the trivial region (Theorem 1).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new way to measure uncertainty based on the probability of finding a particle within specific intervals rather than just the spread of the distribution. It reveals that while position and momentum can be jointly localized to arbitrary precision if the total confidence is low, a strict limit emerges when the required confidence levels are high. This provides a tighter alternative to the traditional Heisenberg uncertainty principle for specific probability-based applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a more operational way to understand joint localization in phase space, which is highly relevant for quantum optics and precision measurements where the probability of finding a particle in a specific window is more critical than the variance.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the limitations of standard-deviation and entropic uncertainty principles by quantifying the probability of finding a particle within specific small regions of position and momentum.

**Main result.** The authors introduce 'confidence uncertainty' and prove a two-regime phase diagram: for confidence levels where theta_x + theta_p <= 1, position and momentum can be jointly localized arbitrarily well, whereas for theta_x + theta_p > 1, a strict positive lower bound applies.

**Method.** The study utilizes Lenard's projection inequality, the Donoho-Stark operator-norm bound, and Slepian-Pollak-Landau theory involving prolate-spheroidal wave functions.

**Model / system.** A quantum particle in a pure state described by position (x) and momentum (p) representations.

**Key observables.** Confidence uncertainty (minimal Lebesgue measure of the support set), interval confidence uncertainty, and the probability mass (theta_x, theta_p) within specific regions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Confidence levels (theta_x, theta_p), the time-bandwidth product (c), and the transition boundary defined by theta_x + theta_p = 1.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary results focus on pure quantum states, though the framework can be extended to mixed states.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a phase diagram of the (theta_x, theta_p) plane; Figure 2 displays the largest Slepian eigenvalue on linear and log scales; Figure 3 compares the new Landau-Pollak bound against the elementary bound.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces new uncertainty measures, establishes a phase diagram for two distinct regimes, provides analytical proofs for lower bounds using signal processing theory, performs numerical evaluations of Slepian eigenvalues, and compares the new bounds against established Heisenberg, entropic, and Donoho-Stark bounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Heisenberg's uncertainty principle states that the position and momentum of a particle cannot be sharply determined simultaneously. Standard-deviation and entropic formulations capture the spread of the probability distribution but say little about the probability itself contained in a small region. We introduce the "confidence uncertainty" $Δ^{c}x(θ_x)$ as the minimal Lebesgue measure of the support set in which the particle is found with probability at least $θ_x$, and the companion "interval confidence uncertainty" $Δ^{I}x(θ_x)$ which restricts the support to a single interval. We prove two complementary uncertainty inequalities. (i) For $θ_x+θ_p\le 1$ both confidence uncertainties can be made arbitrarily small simultaneously, so that no nontrivial product bound holds; in particular, position and momentum can be jointly localised with probability at least~$50\%$. (ii) For $θ_x+θ_p>1$ a lower bound holds: combining Lenard's projection inequality with the Donoho--Stark operator-norm bound we obtain $Δ^{c}x\,Δ^{c}p\geq 2π\hbar\bigl(\sqrt{θ_xθ_p}-\sqrt{(1-θ_x)(1-θ_p)}\bigr)^{\!2}$, and for the interval version we obtain the sharp implicit Landau--Pollak bound $Δ^{I}x\,Δ^{I}p\geq 4\hbar\,λ_{0}^{-1}\!\bigl((\sqrt{θ_xθ_p}-\sqrt{(1-θ_x)(1-θ_p)})^{2}\bigr)$, where $λ_{0}(c)$ is the largest prolate-spheroidal eigenvalue. We support the analytical bounds with numerical evaluation of $λ_{0}(c)$, provide closed-form small-$c$ and large-$c$ asymptotics, compute the optimal Slepian-superposition states that saturate the interval bound, and compare the resulting product against the variance Heisenberg--Kennard, the Białynicki-Birula--Mycielski entropic, and the Donoho--Stark concentration bounds.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04673"></a>
### [Dynamical pseudopotentials](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04673v1)

**Authors:** Matteo Quinzi, Tommaso Chiarotti, Nicola Marzari  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04673v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04673_figures/2605.04673_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Logarithmic derivative as a function of energy for the all-electron (AE) potential, and dynamical pseudopotential (dynPP) for the (left) copper d electrons at distance R = 2.1 (a.u.) and (right) erbium f electrons at distance R = 1.6 (a.u.). The reference energies are marked with black dots. In both cases the pseudopotential is built including up to seven reference energies, while only three projectors are used following the truncation strategy.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new class of energy-dependent pseudopotentials that improve the transferability of first-principles calculations to high-energy and excited states. By using a sum-over-poles approach, it achieves high accuracy without the numerical instabilities typical of multi-projector methods. This provides a unified and consistent treatment of all-electron and pseudo-atom properties within many-body theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of an embedding-problem formulation and the treatment of energy-dependent potentials are highly relevant to many-body dynamics and the development of frequency-dependent self-energies in correlated systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional static pseudopotentials struggle with transferability in excited-state calculations and high-energy states, often suffering from numerical instabilities when increasing the number of projectors.

**Main result.** The authors develop a framework for dynamical (energy-dependent) pseudopotentials using a sum-over-poles representation that decouples the number of reference energies from the number of projectors, ensuring accurate all-electron scattering over wide energy ranges.

**Method.** The method uses an embedding-problem formulation, a sum-over-poles (SOP) representation, and an algorithmic-inversion method to solve the nonlinear eigenvalue problem, combined with a variational strategy to optimize the basis set.

**Model / system.** The framework treats the electronic structure of atoms and solids as an embedding problem where valence electrons are coupled to an auxiliary bath representing core degrees of freedom via an energy-dependent potential.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic derivative of the potential, dynamical augmentation charges, radial momentum distribution, and spectral density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Core radius (Rc), reference energies, number of poles, and number of projectors.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes spherical symmetry for atomic calculations and assumes the availability of radial all-electron and pseudo-orbitals.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the logarithmic derivative vs. energy for Cu and Er, demonstrating the match between AE and dynPP; supplementary figures show radial momentum distributions and wavefunction profiles.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the dynamical embedding perspective, derivation of generalized norm-conservation, introduction of the sum-over-poles representation and algorithmic inversion, demonstration of numerical stability and efficiency, and validation via momentum-space analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Pseudopotential theory has greatly driven first-principles calculations in materials, replacing the explicit treatment of the chemically inert core electrons with an effective potential acting only on the valence states. This is inherently an embedding problem, where tracing out the core electrons can be formulated in terms of a dynamical embedding potential. Motivated by this perspective, we first introduce a framework for dynamical (i.e., energy-dependent) pseudopotentials, showing how this leads to generalized norm-conservation conditions. Then, adopting a sum-over-poles representation, we disentangle the number of reference energies from the number of projectors; this allows to reproduce all-electron scattering at many reference energies with great accuracy and over very extended energy ranges. We further show that these pseudopotentials enter naturally into many-body total energy functionals, leading for the first time to a consistent and unified treatment of the all-electron atom, the pseudo-atom, and the solid within the same electronic-structure theory.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05179"></a>
### [Estimating the expected output of wide random MLPs more efficiently than sampling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05179v1)

**Authors:** Wilson Wu, Victor Lecomte, Michael Winer, George Robinson, Jacob Hilton, Paul Christiano  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05179v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

</details>

<img src="2605.05179_figures/2605.05179_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.05179_figures/2605.05179_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Performance of the factorized version of our algorithm with K = 3 at low probability estimation. Error bars show ±1 standard error, with 205 random seeds used across the 7 buckets.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.05179_figures/2605.05179_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Learning curves for distillation of a random teacher MLP with width 16 into a student MLP with width 8, both with 4 hidden layers.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a way to calculate the expected behavior of large random neural networks without actually running samples through them. By propagating the statistical properties (cumulants) of the activations through the layers, the authors achieve much higher computational efficiency and better accuracy for predicting rare, high-risk events.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently estimating the expected output and loss of wide, random multilayer perceptrons (MLPs) without the high computational cost of Monte Carlo sampling.

**Main result.** The authors develop a sample-free cumulant propagation method that achieves a target mean squared error using significantly fewer FLOPs than Monte Carlo sampling, specifically outperforming it by a factor of n in the large-width limit.

**Method.** The method uses cumulant propagation via Hermite expansions and diagram summation to track the distribution of activations layer-by-layer, including factorized versions to reduce complexity.

**Model / system.** Wide, random, fully-connected feedforward MLPs with L hidden layers and width n, using He initialization and Gaussian inputs.

**Key observables.** Expected output (quenched average), expected loss, and higher-order cumulants (mean, variance, etc.).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Network width (n), network depth (L), maximum cumulant order (K), and error tolerance (epsilon).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes the large-width limit (n to infinity), fixed depth, and primarily focuses on polynomial or 'tame' activation functions like ReLU.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares MSE vs FLOPs for various algorithms against Monte Carlo; Figure 2 shows MSE vs FLOPs for different depths; Figure 3 shows MSE scaling with width n; Figure 4 compares relative RMSE for rare event probabilities; Figure 5 compares distillation learning curves.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of sampling inefficiency, presents the cumulant propagation algorithm and its factorized variants, provides theoretical complexity and error bounds using diagrammatic expansions, and demonstrates empirical superiority and applications in mechanistic distillation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

By far the most common way to estimate an expected loss in machine learning is to draw samples, compute the loss on each one, and take the empirical average. However, sampling is not necessarily optimal. Given an MLP at initialization, we show how to estimate its expected output over Gaussian inputs without running samples through the network at all. Instead, we produce approximate representations of the distributions of activations at each layer, leveraging tools such as cumulants and Hermite expansions. We show both theoretically and empirically that for sufficiently wide networks, our estimator achieves a target mean squared error using substantially fewer FLOPs than Monte Carlo sampling. We find moreover that our methods perform particularly well at estimating the probabilities of rare events, and additionally demonstrate how they can be used for model training. Together, these findings suggest a path to producing models with a greatly reduced probability of catastrophic tail risks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04492"></a>
### [Finite-size scaling properties of classical random walk on various two-dimensional lattices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04492v1)

**Authors:** Nimish Sharma, Tanay Nag  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04492v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

(Color online) (a) We show the variation of total number of squares ˜ Nc, designated by points, that only fill the circumference of the 2D closed curve associated with the ran- dom walk as a function of 2s for square (violet), honeycomb (yellow), Lieb (blue), Kagome (red) and dice (green) where s denotes the scale. <sub>FIG. 5. We consider 1000 × 1000 lattice points for all the lattices. The points rep- resent average df over 500 walkers.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper examines how the geometry of 2D lattices influences the fractal properties of classical random walks. It demonstrates that while lattice connectivity causes measurable differences in fractal dimensions in finite systems, these differences vanish as the system approaches the thermodynamic limit of Brownian motion.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how different 2D lattice geometries and connectivity patterns affect the finite-size scaling properties, mass fractal dimension, and hull fractal dimension of classical random walks.

**Main result.** The study finds that while RMSD remains universally diffusive, the mass and hull fractal dimensions exhibit a weak ordering correlated with the average coordination number, though they eventually converge to Brownian motion values in the thermodynamic limit.

**Method.** Numerical simulations of ensembles of random walkers on various 2D lattices using box-counting methods, log-log scaling analysis, and data collapse techniques.

**Model / system.** Unbiased, non-self-avoiding classical random walks on various 2D lattices including Square, Kagome, Lieb, Honeycomb, and Dice lattices.

**Key observables.** Root Mean Square Displacement (RMSD), mass fractal dimension (df), and hull fractal dimension (dh).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice connectivity (coordination number), number of steps (n), and the thermodynamic limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The random walks are assumed to be unbiased and non-self-avoiding.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the lattice structures; Figure 2 displays RMSD scaling; Figure 3 shows mass fractal dimension via box-counting; Figure 4 shows hull dimension scaling; Figure 6 illustrates the variation of hull dimension with step number and its data collapse.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces various 2D lattice geometries, presents simulation results for RMSD and fractal dimensions, analyzes the influence of coordination number on scaling, and concludes with a data collapse analysis demonstrating convergence to the thermodynamic limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider various two-dimensional lattices such as square, Kagome, Lieb, honeycomb, dice lattices of finite extent, to study the effect of lattice profile in terms of the number of nearest neighbour and connectivity patterns on the classical random walk in the unbiased scenario. We find that the standard deviation of distance travelled by the walker is insensitive to the non-uniformity of the lattice profile leading to diffusive transport even in the finite size lattices. Our study indicates that the mass fractal dimension varies within a window $1.50\pm 0.03$ for all finite-size lattices. A weak ordering within the above window, correlated with the average coordination number, is observed, while Lieb and square lattices yielding the minimum and maximum values, respectively. However, confidence intervals reveal substantial statistical overlap for several lattice pairs even though the lattice profiles vary as far as the average number of connecting bonds and directionality of bonds are concerned. We also study the scaling complexity of the circumference of the closed curve traced by the walker while investigating the hull dimension. We find similar trend for hull fractal dimension as well and that was found to within the window $1.37\pm 0.03$ for finite-size lattices. Within the above window, the ordering remains qualitatively unaltered as compared to mass dimension while the confidence interval rectifies the order quantitatively. The square lattice clearly exhibits the upper bound for hull fractal dimension and the remaining lattices show extensive statistical overlap within the above window. We exhibit a tendency of the mass and hull fractal dimension to reach their thermodynamic values given by Brownian motion when we allow more number of steps within the finite size of the lattice, as confirmed by a data collapse analysis.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04883"></a>
### [Magnetic Brightening and Nanoscale Imaging of Spin-Polarized Helical Edge Modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04883v1)

**Authors:** Samuel Haeuser, Richard H. J. Kim, Lin-Lin Wang, Thomas Koschny, Pedro M. Lozano, Genda Gu, Randall K. Chan, Joong-Mok Park, Martin Mootz, Liang Luo, Qiang Li, Jigang Wang

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates the ability to visually map spin-polarized edge states in a topological insulator using infrared near-field microscopy. By applying a magnetic field, the researchers induced a 'brightening' effect that reveals the underlying spin-splitting of the edge modes. This discovery highlights a regime where topological edge conduction remains robust at high frequencies, even when magnetic fields induce gaps that would otherwise suppress DC conduction.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of visualizing and characterizing the nanoscale spin-polarized helical edge modes and their magnetic-field-induced spin-splitting in the weak topological insulator ZrTe5.

**Main result.** The researchers observed 'magnetic brightening,' where an applied magnetic field enhances near-field conductivity at step edges by lifting spin degeneracy and creating imbalanced AC currents. They also demonstrated that these high-frequency edge states remain robust and scale linearly with the number of atomic layers, unlike DC transport which is suppressed by magnetic gaps.

**Method.** Cryogenic magneto-infrared scattering-type scanning near-field optical microscopy (cm-IR-sSNOM) was used at temperatures down to 1.8 K and magnetic fields up to 5 T.

**Model / system.** The physical system is ZrTe5, a weak topological insulator characterized by spin-polarized, counter-propagating helical edge modes at crystal step edges and domain walls.

**Key observables.** Near-field conductivity, infrared photocurrent oscillations, amplitude and phase contrast, and spatial width of edge modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperatures of 1.8-2 K, magnetic fields up to 5 T, photon energies around 100 meV, and atomic layer numbers ranging from 6 to 11.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The edge states are assumed to be effectively stacked and robust in multi-layer structures due to weak van der Waals coupling.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows a schematic of the AFM probe and the mechanism of magnetic brightening; Fig 2 displays AFM topography and sSNOM images showing enhanced signal at step edges under magnetic fields; Fig 4d visualizes the spatial extent of the magnetically separated edge modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of nanoscale transport, describes the experimental setup and ZrTe5 system, presents the discovery of magnetic brightening and spin-splitting, validates the results with layer-scaling and control experiments, and concludes with implications for future microelectronics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Efficient sub-10 nm electric transport remains a major challenge for nanoelectronics due to high losses and impedance mismatches in conventional Drude metals. Despite their promise of dissipationless, reflection-free conduction, topologically protected chiral edge modes remain little explored in their nanoscale spin polarized transport-particularly regarding real-space visualization, magnetic field tunability, and high-frequency edge conductivity. Here, we report magnetic brightening and nanoscale visualization of highly spin-polarizable infrared helical edge states using cryogenic magneto-infrared scattering-type scanning near-field optical microscopy (cm-IR-sSNOM). Our measurements reveal magnetic field-induced near-field conductivity at step edges, uncovering quantum spin Hall spin-splitting modes with enhanced infrared polarizability and slightly narrowed near-field profiles. In addition, the infrared edge electrodynamic response scales nearly linearly with atomic layer number, providing compelling evidence that magnetic-field-induced gaps do not disrupt individual-layer edge states at energies of around 100 meV. These results sharply contrast with microwave and DC transport, where even small magnetically induced gaps decrease edge conduction. Magnetically tunable, topologically robust high-frequency edge modes open a pathway toward ultralow-loss nanoscale interconnects and quantum logic architectures for next-generation microelectronics, spintronics and quantum information science.

</details>

### [Quantum Magic in early FTQC: From Diagonal Clifford Hierarchy No-Go Theorems to Architecture Design Blueprints](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04758v1)

This model captures the broad algebraic capacity of early FTQC but operates outside the restrictive Space-Time efficient Analog Rotation (STAR) paradigm (blue boundary). In contrast, ansatz 2 explicitly belongs to the STAR subset, utilizing an N-layer alternating sequence of Clifford operations (Ci) and localized single-qubit diagonal Z-rotation. Although kinematically...</sub>

<sub>FIG. 2. Schematic comparison of circuit setups used to cal- culate quantum magic. (a) Standard theoretical paradigm based on deep T-doped random circuits. In these commonly analyzed models, non-stabilizerness is analytically evaluated by averaging over ensembles of uniformly distributed random Clifford blocks Ci ∼Uniform(C(2) n ) to form unitary 3-designs that approximate Haar-random states [23, 25, 28]. However, this framework relies on the perfect gate accuracy of mature fault-tolerant hardware. (b) A deterministic early FTQC framework analyzed this work. The Lp</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04758_figures/2605.04758_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper provides a fundamental framework for maximizing non-stabilizer resources in early fault-tolerant quantum computers. It proves that gate hierarchy and state-independent sequences are insufficient for magic generation, establishing that optimal architecture design must be state-aware and utilize multi-qubit interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the generation of quantum magic (non-stabilizerness) in early fault-tolerant quantum computing (FTQC) architectures by selecting optimal gate sequences and input states.

**Main result.** The authors prove two no-go theorems: the Clifford hierarchy level alone cannot universally order magic, and no state-independent sequence of operations can guarantee monotonic magic improvement. They also identify a bottleneck in single-qubit rotation architectures and propose multi-qubit diagonal phases as a solution.

**Method.** The study uses the resource theory of magic, Pauli-spectrum analysis, and the algebraic properties of the Clifford hierarchy, employing symplectic formalism and phase-polynomial representations to derive analytical bounds.

**Model / system.** The paper analyzes early FTQC circuit models, specifically a shallow-layer model (one Clifford layer followed by a diagonal hierarchy gate) and an N-layer model (alternating Clifford and diagonal layers) inspired by the STAR architecture.

**Key observables.** Quantum magic (non-stabilizerness), Pauli spectrum (expectation values), stabilizer Renyi entropy, and Pauli rank (support size).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Clifford hierarchy level (k), number of pure-Z generators (r), number of layers (N), and phase-polynomial parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on pure quantum states and assumes ideal, high-fidelity Clifford operations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the structural classification of the analyzed architectures; Figure 2 compares standard T-doped random circuits with the early FTQC framework; Figure 3 illustrates the organization of magic measures; Figure 4 depicts the shallow-layer and N-layer circuit models.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by classifying magic measures and establishing a uniqueness theorem for Pauli-spectrum functionals. It then analyzes the shallow-layer model to derive zero-magic and maximal-magic conditions, followed by an investigation of the N-layer model to present no-go theorems regarding hierarchy and monotonic improvement. Finally, it identifies architectural bottlenecks and proposes multi-qubit rotation strategies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We address the circuit-design problem of maximizing quantum magic in early fault-tolerant quantum computing (early FTQC), where logical dynamics natively take the form of alternating Clifford layers and diagonal non-Clifford layers. To render this optimization analytically tractable, we first prove a uniqueness theorem: for operational magic functionals built from Pauli expectation values, the axioms of faithfulness and tensor-product additivity force a Rényi-type dependence on the Pauli-spectrum. Leveraging the closed phase-polynomial description of the diagonal Clifford hierarchy, we derive exact Pauli-spectrum expressions and tight bounds for a shallow-layer model. These bounds expose a zero-magic mechanism and prove that maximal magic strictly requires graph-state preconditioning. Consequently, we establish our first no-go theorem: hierarchy level alone cannot universally order operational magic. Extending our framework to the $N$-layer model motivated by the Space-Time Efficient Analog Rotation (STAR) architecture, we obtain an exact iterative update rule for the Pauli spectrum. This yields a second no-go theorem: no state-independent sequence of operations can guarantee monotonic magic improvement. Together, these theorems demonstrate that algebraic gate structures are fundamentally insufficient to dictate resource generation. To overcome this, we reframe early FTQC gate selection as a state-aware, differentiable optimization over continuous analog parameters. Finally, we identify a severe kinematic expressibility bottleneck in architectures restricted to single-qubit $Z$-rotations and show that introducing nonlinear diagonal phases, such as multi-qubit $Z$-rotation, shatters this bottleneck. This provides a fundamental principle for demonstrating early FTQC, establishing scalable magic generation as a foundational benchmark for evaluating early FTQC architectures.

</details>

### [Frustrated magnetic order in hybrid Kitaev spin-orbital models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05196v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores what happens when two different types of quantum spin liquids are merged into a single lattice. It reveals that the system can undergo 'magnetic fragmentation,' where magnetism and topological order coexist, and shows how tuning interactions can lead to complex phase transitions and restored exact solvability.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the emergent ground states and phase transitions that occur when two independent, exactly solvable spin-orbital Hamiltonians (Kitaev and Yao-Lee) are combined on a common lattice geometry.

**Main result.** The study discovers 'magnetic fragmentation,' where the spin sector develops magnetic order while the orbital sector retains topological order, and identifies a special point where the model restores exact solvability.

**Method.** Self-consistent Majorana mean-field analysis, perturbative calculations in the strong-Kitaev limit, and Luttinger-Tisza analysis for determining ordering wavevectors.

**Model / system.** A hybrid spin-orbital model on a modified honeycomb/square lattice featuring both Kitaev-type interactions and square-lattice-type interactions involving spin and orbital degrees of freedom.

**Key observables.** Magnetization (m_lambda), bond order parameter (chi_lambda), Majorana Dirac bands, Lifshitz transitions, and plaquette flux sectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kitaev coupling strength (J_K), square-lattice coupling strength (J_S), and the ratio of Yao-Lee to square-lattice couplings (J_Y/J_S).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Use of mean-field decoupling for quartic terms and a unit cell approximation to handle incommensurate magnetic structures.

**Figures summary.** Schematic of the modified honeycomb lattice, color plots of Majorana conduction bands showing Lifshitz transitions, and energy per site plots for the mean-field solutions.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to spin-orbital models, description of the hybrid Kitaev and Yao-Lee Hamiltonians, mean-field methodology, analysis of the Kitaev-square regime (magnetic fragmentation), analysis of the Yao-Lee-square regime (band evolution), and discussion of the exact solvability point.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin-orbital generalization of Kitaev model provides a robust extension to the original Kitaev model. However, real materials often exhibit competing interactions that break exact solvability which can give rise to new phases. Motivated by recent microscopic proposals of coexisting Yao-Lee and Kitaev couplings, we investigate the fate of the ground state when two independent exactly solvable spin liquid Hamiltonians each originally formulated on different lattice geometries are combined on a common lattice environment. We first focus on the hybrid Kitaev's honeycomb and square-lattice model. Using self-consistent mean-field analysis and perturbative calculation, we show that the strong-Kitaev regime yields magnetic order in the spin sector, while the orbital sector retains its topological order. We further analyze the hybridization of the Yao-Lee and square-lattice models and find that the model exhibits a rich evolution of Majorana Dirac bands and Lifshitz transitions. Remarkably, when the Yao-Lee and square-lattice couplings are equal and opposite, the model restores its exact solvability with a single itinerant Majorana flavor. These results demonstrate that hybrid spin liquid platforms may host various emergent phases beyond conventional exactly solvable limits.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05036"></a>
### [Block Permutation Routing on Ramanujan Hypergraphs for Fault-Tolerant Quantum Computing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05036v1)

**Authors:** Joshua M. Courtney  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05036v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Rydberg arrays` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.05036_figures/2605.05036_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Quotient graph construction. Left: the physical graph G = Gcl(H) with four blocks B1, . . . , B4 (colored rectangles), each containing d2 C atoms (dots), separated by guard distance g. Unoccupied vertices are shown in gray. Right: the (weighted) quotient graph Q(G, B), with one supervertex per block and edge weight AQ[i, j] equal to the average per-vertex inter-block edge count from Bi to Bj (Definition 2.8). Edge thickness is proportional to AQ[i, j]; only edges with AQ[i, j] &gt; 0 are drawn. The spectral ratio βQ = λ∗(AQ)/d′ Q &lt; 1 controls expansion.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.05036_figures/2605.05036_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Three-layer spectral argument for quotient graph expansion. Layer 1 (Theorem 4.4) gives the tightest bound with Haemers interlacing but requires an exactly equitable partition. Layer 2 (Theorem 4.11) handles near- equitable partitions with Weyl perturbation bounds. Layer 3 (Theorem 4.12) applies universally by higher-order Cheeger inequalities but gives the weakest bound. All three layers require (2) as an architectural prerequisite; the layers differ in the partition-structure assumption on B.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.05036_figures/2605.05036_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Numerical validation of the Θ(dC log NL) scaling. (a) Simulated routing depth Tsim versus the theoretical prediction dC log2 NL for dC ∈{3, 5, 7} and NL ∈{8, 16, 32, 64}. The dashed line is T = dC log2 NL; all points lie at or below this line, confirming the upper bound. (b) Normalized ratio α = Tsim/(dC log2 NL) versus NL. The ratio is bounded away from zero and bounded above by 1, consistent with the tight Θ(·) bound.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a theoretical framework for efficient qubit routing in next-generation quantum computers. It proves that using high-connectivity Ramanujan hypergraphs allows for moving large error-correcting code patches with a routing depth that scales linearly with the code distance, significantly reducing the overhead for fault-tolerant operations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the routing complexity and depth required to move large, rigid blocks of qubits (representing surface code patches) across reconfigurable quantum architectures without violating fault-tolerance constraints.

**Main result.** The authors establish that the block routing number scales as Theta(d_C log N_L), proving that routing depth scales linearly with code distance rather than quadratically, provided the hardware operates in a high-connectivity regime.

**Method.** The study employs spectral graph theory, specifically using Haemers interlacing, Weyl perturbation, and higher-order Cheeger inequalities on Ramanujan hypergraphs and their quotient graphs, combined with LMR scheduling and Valiant's routing algorithm.

**Model / system.** The model consists of surface code patches of size d_C^2 atoms moving as rigid blocks on a reconfigurable lattice modeled by the clique expansion of a Ramanujan hypergraph, applicable to neutral-atom and QCCD trapped-ion architectures.

**Key observables.** Block routing number (rt_B), routing depth, congestion, and spectral ratio (beta_Q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code distance (d_C), number of blocks (N_L), hypergraph rank (r), base degree (d), and the high-connectivity regime condition.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The blocks are assumed to be rigid during transport, and the hardware must satisfy a high-connectivity threshold to maintain the expansion properties of the host graph.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the mapping from physical graphs to weighted quotient graphs; Figure 2 illustrates the three-layer spectral argument hierarchy; Table 2 compares simulated routing depth against theoretical predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the routing problem, defines the Ramanujan hypergraph model, develops a three-layer spectral argument for quotient graph inheritance, establishes the routing complexity bounds, integrates error models and syndrome extraction protocols, and concludes with extensions to QCCD and deformable patches.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We analyze permutation routing of rigid blocks representing surface code patches of $d_C^2$ atoms on a reconfigurable lattice with hypergraph transformations. For a hypergraph $H$, code distance $d_C$, $s=d_C^2$, number of blocks $N_L$, and guard distance $g$, we show the block routing number $\mathrm{rt}_B(H, s, g) = Θ(d_C \log N_L)$. A spectral analysis of the quotient graph $Q(G_{\mathrm{cl}}(H), B)$ (blocks as supervertices) shows that the spectral ratio $β_Q < 1$ is preserved in the high-connectivity regime. Negative association of block permutations and congestion bounds are used for random intermediate configurations. Serialization establishes that each quotient routing phase requires $O(d_C)$ physical sub-steps due to the block footprint width. A lower bound $\mathrm{rt}_B = Ω(d_C \log N_L)$ follows from combining the spectral lower bound on quotient phases with the traversal cost per phase. We include error model analysis grounded in recent experimental results, syndrome extraction protocols (stop-and-correct, rolling active fault-tolerant (AFT) measurement, and adaptive deformation), and integration with lattice surgery compilation via the Litinski protocol. Composition with the correlated-decoding scheme reduces syndrome-extraction overhead from $O(d_C)$ to $O(1)$ per correction window, leaving routing as the leading-order contributor to the integrated $O(d_C \log N_L)$ depth. Spectral inheritance is organized in a hierarchy: exact (Haemers interlacing on equitable partitions), perturbative (Weyl bounds for near-equitable partitions, a practically relevant case for surface-code patches), and universal (higher-order Cheeger). Methods extend directly to QCCD trapped-ion architectures under the same regime condition, with junction crossings replacing AOD transports as the elementary single-hop translation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04329"></a>
### [Energy-error tradeoff in encoding quantum error correction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04329v1)

**Authors:** Josey Stevens, Sebastian Deffner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04329v1>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>FIG. 5. Error rates of computation a computation subject to a fixed channel rate of PX = 0.10. The computation is carried out on the bare qubit (blue) and circuits protected by the three (orange), five (purple), seven (yellow), and nine (green) qubit repetition code. Energies where the N-qubit repetition code reach an advantage over the (N −1)-qubit repetition code are highlighted with colored circles.
