</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that achieving higher precision in quantum error correction requires an exponential increase in control energy. It highlights that the physical implementation of QEC circuits is just as important as the code itself due to the energy-error tradeoff.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental physical constraint (energy scaling) on the scalability of quantum error correction, which is critical for designing realistic hardware for open quantum systems and many-body quantum processors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the fundamental energy-error tradeoff in Quantum Error Correction (QEC) and determining if the required energetic resources for encoding are physically feasible.

**Main result.** The study reveals a universal tradeoff where required energetic resources scale exponentially with the targeted precision of the encoding, and that different QEC circuit implementations significantly impact error performance.

**Method.** The authors use a precision-noise model to calculate a tight lower bound on control energy and perform numerical simulations comparing various QEC codes (repetition, perfect, and Steane) and encoding schemes.

**Model / system.** A precision-noise model where gate errors arise from quantum fluctuations in classical electromagnetic control fields, applicable to platforms like trapped ions, semiconductors, and superconducting qubits.

**Key observables.** Average gate fidelity (F_ave), computation error rate (Z_error and X_error), and control energy (E_G_L).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate-level noise (epsilon), channel-level bit-flip probability (P_X), code distance (N), and number of fault-tolerant validation rounds (v).

**Assumptions / limitations.** All gate coefficients are subject to the same noise distribution, and all input states are in the computational basis.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows gate error vs. control energy; Figure 2 illustrates the QEC process; Figure 3 compares encoding schemes for repetition codes; Figure 4 plots error rates vs. energy and channel noise; Figure 6 shows error rates for different perfect code encodings; Figure 7 compares various distance-3 codes; Figure 8 compares error rates for distance-3 codes at fixed noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the energy-error problem, defines the noise and energy models, analyzes gate-level energy bounds, compares different QEC codes and encoding schemes, and evaluates the overhead of fault-tolerant measurements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

While it has been widely recognized that genuine quantum advantage for practical problems might only be achieved with fault-tolerant quantum computers, it is still not entirely clear whether the required quantum error correction will be physically feasible. In the present work, we carefully analyze the required energy resources to encode the logical qubit states for repetition, perfect, and Steane codes. We find that there is a universal trade-off between the target precision and the required energetic resources. Importantly, we find that the energetic resources intimately depend on the specific physical realization of a quantum error correction code, and that the required resources scale exponentially with the targeted precision of the encoding.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05132"></a>
### [A Factor-Graph Formulation of CSS Syndrome Decoding: Joint BP and Four-State BP](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05132v1)

**Authors:** Kenta Kasai  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05132v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper provides a formal equivalence between two different factor-graph approaches for decoding CSS quantum error-correcting codes. It shows that using a joint belief propagation algorithm on two coupled Tanner graphs yields the same results as a single four-state belief propagation algorithm. This clarifies the theoretical relationship between different decoding implementations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the efficiency and formulation of syndrome decoding for CSS-type quantum error-correcting codes, specifically comparing different belief propagation approaches.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that the 'joint BP' algorithm, which uses two coupled Tanner graphs, is mathematically equivalent to the 'four-state BP' algorithm after relabeling Pauli states and marginalizing components.

**Method.** The study utilizes a factor-graph formulation, comparing the sum-product algorithm on a joint binary factor graph (joint BP) against a four-state Pauli-label factor graph (four-state BP).

**Model / system.** The system is a CSS (Calderbank-Shor-Steane) quantum error-correcting code, where errors are decomposed into two binary Pauli error components subject to parity-check constraints.

**Key observables.** Posterior weights, messages, and beliefs within the belief propagation algorithm.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local channel correlation between the two Pauli error components.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoding process assumes the error structure follows the CSS framework with local joint priors at each qubit.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the joint BP formulation for CSS decoding, defines the joint prior coupling, and provides a mathematical comparison/equivalence proof with the four-state BP method.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

For CSS syndrome decoding, the two check matrices impose binary parity-check constraints on the two Pauli error components. The posterior can therefore be written as a binary factor graph with two Tanner graphs coupled by the local joint prior at each qubit. We call the sum-product algorithm on this factorization joint belief propagation (joint BP). Joint BP retains the local channel correlation between the two Pauli components. This note compares joint BP with the four-state Pauli-label factor graph used for four-state BP. The two algorithms are shown to have the same posterior weights, messages, and beliefs after relabeling the four local Pauli states and marginalizing the irrelevant binary component.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [A Transferable Machine Learning Approach to Predict Optimized Orbitals for Electronic Structure Problems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04174v1)

**Authors:** Lucas van der Horst, Maniraman Periyasamy, Abhishek Y. Dubey, Davide Bincoletto, Jakob S. Kottmann, Daniel D. Scherer

**Summary.** The paper presents a Graph Neural Network approach to predict optimized molecular orbitals directly from geometry. This method allows for much faster setup of VQE simulations and generalizes to larger molecular systems without retraining, significantly accelerating the hybrid quantum-classical workflow.

**Why it may be interesting.** It addresses a critical scalability bottleneck in NISQ-era algorithms by using machine learning to automate the classical pre-processing required for variational quantum chemistry.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The computational bottleneck in VQE workflows caused by the need to re-optimize molecular orbital coefficients via expensive classical routines for every new molecular geometry.

**Main result.** A transferable Graph Neural Network (GNN) that predicts optimized orbital coefficients, achieving 30x faster inference than classical optimization and providing high-quality warm-starts that significantly reduce convergence iterations.

**Method.** A dual-scale GNN architecture using fine and coarse interaction cutoffs to predict the entries of an antisymmetric generator matrix, trained on small hydrogenic systems and tested on larger, unseen systems.

**Model / system.** Hydrogenic molecular systems (H4, H6, H8, H10, H12) using the Separable Pair Approximation (SPA) ansatz and the Hard-Core Boson (HCB) approximation within a VQE framework.

**Key observables.** Mean absolute energy errors (in milli-Hartrees) and reduction in optimizer iterations for ground-state energy convergence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction scales of r_fine = 2.5 A and r_coarse = 5.0 A; dissociation regime (1.5-4.0 A) and bonding regime (0.5-1.5 A).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model is currently limited to hydrogenic systems in a minimal STO-3G basis and assumes real-valued orthogonal orbital rotation matrices.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the GNN pipeline from mapping to energy computation; Figure 2 displays energy distributions for different geometries; Figure 3 compares potential energy curves for predicted vs. classical energies and shows the impact of warm-starting; Figure 4 visualizes orbital shape transformations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the VQE orbital optimization bottleneck, describes the GNN architecture and feature engineering (node/edge descriptors), presents training on small systems and transferability tests on larger systems, and concludes with error analysis and future directions for end-to-end automation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Variational quantum eigensolver ansätze hold considerable promise for ground-state energy calculations on near-term quantum hardware, yet most promising ansatz designs currently strongly depend on how well the molecular orbital basis captures the electronic correlation of the system. Computing optimized orbital coefficients via classical routines is computationally expensive and must be performed independently for each molecular geometry -- a bottleneck that limits scalability across chemical space. We present a graph neural network framework that predicts optimized orbital coefficients directly from molecular geometry and pair-wise bonding structure. Trained on hydrogenic systems of modest size ($H_4$ and $H_6$) across tens of thousands of geometries, our model transfers to larger, unseen systems ($H_8$, $H_{10}$ and $H_{12}$) without retraining -- demonstrating strong out-of-distribution generalization with respect to system size. When evaluating on structured and random configurations, and comparing against energies obtained with full classical optimization, our model reaches mean absolute energy errors $\mathcal{O}(10^2)$ and $\mathcal{O}(10)$ milli-Hartrees, respectively. Beyond energy estimation, the predicted orbitals serve as high-quality warm-start initializations that substantially reduce optimizer iterations to ground-state energy convergence. These results establish graph neural networks as an effective and scalable strategy for accelerating orbital optimization in hybrid quantum-classical workflows, directly reducing the classical pre-processing overhead that currently limits the practical deployment of variational quantum eigensolver on near-term quantum hardware.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive review and extension of CFT methods to calculate geometric properties of critical systems, such as SLE curves and FK clusters. It successfully derives new analytical formulas for cluster densities and pivotal points by solving differential equations derived from conformal symmetry. The work unifies several previously separate results in the literature into a single framework.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to provide a pedagogical review and systematic extension of Conformal Field Theory (CFT) techniques to compute Schramm-Loewner Evolution (SLE) observables and the densities of anchored critical clusters in the upper half-plane.

**Main result.** The authors derive explicit analytical expressions for various cluster densities and SLE excursions, and calculate exact values for structure constants governing the merging of SLE curves and bubbles.

**Method.** The study utilizes Conformal Field Theory (CFT) techniques, specifically solving Belavin-Polyakov-Zamolodchikov (BPZ) differential equations and applying the Coulomb gas formalism to represent physical observables as correlation functions.

**Model / system.** The models studied include the Fortuin-Kasteleyn (FK) random cluster model, O(n) loop models, and Schramm-Loewner Evolution (SLE) in the critical regime.

**Key observables.** Anchored cluster densities, Schramm's left-passage probability, SLE Green's functions, densities of pivotal points, and structure constants for boundary operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The SLE parameter kappa, loop fugacity n, cluster weight Q, central charge c, and the cross-ratio xi.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes the existence of full 2D conformal symmetry in the scaling limit and relies on the use of degenerate boundary operators.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate FK clusters and their boundaries, the difference between percolation clusters and their hulls, the insertion of boundary and bulk operators, and contour plots of cluster densities.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a review of CFT techniques for SLE observables, moves to the application of BPZ equations for various cluster densities, extends the scope to the general FK model, and concludes with the derivation of structure constants and new predictions for cluster boundaries.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We provide a pedagogical review of CFT techniques to compute certain Schramm-Loewner Evolution (SLE) observables in the upper half-plane. The approach relies on the ability to express the observables as bulk-boundary correlation functions that involve degenerate boundary operators and, therefore, obey certain differential equations. In particular, we recover Schramm's left-passage probability for SLE, the SLE Green's functions, and the generalized densities of ``anchored'' critical percolation clusters first obtained by Kleban, Simmons, and Ziff. We also obtain new formulas corresponding to the densities of pivotal points between critical Fortuin-Kasteleyn (FK) clusters.

</details>

### [Angular Gausslets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04517v1)

**Authors:** Steven R. White  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04517v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04517_figures/2605.04517_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Centered orthonormalized Gaussian functions on the periodic 1D line for β = 1, 2, 4, compared with the Fourier- localized packet formed from the same number of plane waves. The Fourier-localized function has long range oscillatory tails, while the orthonormalized Gaussians are much more local. In this test calculation, the length of the periodic line was 41, long enough to clearly show tails.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04517_figures/2605.04517_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Effect of injection for the sweet-spot case β = 2. The injected localized function lies almost on top of the original orthonormalized Gaussian, while now containing the exact low-momentum content of the injected subspace.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. Best fit to the constant function within the orthonor- malized Gaussian span. The Fourier curve is exactly constant. The narrow β = 4 Gaussians show strong oscillatory errors in the constant fit, while β = 2 and especially β = 1 represent the low-momentum sector much more accurately.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04517_figures/2605.04517_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Kinetic-energy eigenvalues for the angular basis with N = 32 optimized centers. This is a purely angular calculation on a sphere of radius R = 1, with no radial degrees of freedom, corresponding to the one-particle angular Hamiltonian used in spherium. The black circles show the exact spherical-harmonic shell sequence ℓ(ℓ+ 1)/2 with degeneracies 2ℓ+ 1, ordered by eigenvalue index n. The red stars are the eigenvalues of the final injected basis, and the blue diamonds are those of the corresponding basis without injection. For N = 32 the injected subspace has Linj = 3, so the first (Linj + 1)2 = 16 states, corresponding to the ℓ= 0, 1, 2, 3 shells, should be exact. This is seen...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04517_figures/2605.04517_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Great-circle cuts through representative localized angular basis functions for optimized sphere point sets with N = 15, 32, and 51 centers (top to bottom). For the three point sets shown, the injected spherical-harmonic subspace was chosen as the largest complete low-ℓblock satisfying (Linj+ 1)2 ≤N/2, giving Linj = 1 (NY = 4) for N = 15, Linj = 3 (NY = 16) for N = 32, and Linj = 4 (NY = 25) for N = 51. For each N, one final injected basis function is evaluated along four great circles through its center, with tangent directions separated by 45◦. The horizontal axis s is the signed angular distance along the great circle, in radians, and the curves for N = 32 and N = 15 are shifted...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces 'Angular Gausslets,' a new 3D localized basis set designed for efficient atomic electronic structure calculations. By using an integral diagonal approximation, the authors reduce the complexity of the electron-electron interaction, enabling high-precision DMRG simulations of atoms. This approach allows for the simultaneous treatment of static and dynamic correlation with microhartree accuracy.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly efficient numerical framework for solving 3D many-body problems, offering a scalable way to handle the computational bottleneck of the four-index Coulomb interaction in atomic systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Extending the 1D Gausslet basis construction to 3D to create a localized, orthonormal basis for atomic electronic structure that allows for an efficient, two-index approximation of the electron-electron interaction.

**Main result.** The development of 'Angular Gausslets' enables highly accurate DMRG calculations of first-row atoms, achieving microhartree precision for the Be atom by combining localized angular/radial bases with advanced extrapolation techniques.

**Method.** The method uses localized spherical Gaussians with an 'injection' technique to ensure exact low-angular-momentum coverage, combined with an Integral Diagonal Approximation (IDA) to compress the four-index Coulomb tensor into a two-index form, and utilizes ESVE for energy extrapolation.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the electronic structure of atoms, specifically the first-row atoms (B, C, N, O, F, Ne) and the Helium atom, using an atom-centered Hamiltonian with a one-electron part and a two-electron Coulomb interaction.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy, kinetic energy spectrum, Coulomb matrix elements, occupancy-weighted leakage, and local energy variance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Angular resolution (number of centers N_Omega), width parameter (beta), radial scale (s), and the angular momentum injection limit (L_inj).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes basis functions behave as localized bumps with short oscillatory tails and that the wavefunction varies slowly over the local neighborhood of the basis functions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares 1D Gaussian localization; Figures 2-3 illustrate the effectiveness of the injection method; Figure 4 shows the spatial localization of angular basis functions; Figure 5 shows the recovery of the kinetic energy spectrum; Figure 7 shows Hartree-Fock energy convergence; Figure 11 shows the final energy extrapolation.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the 1D Gausslet construction, moves to the 2D spherical angular Gausslet derivation and optimization, extends the basis to a 3D radial-angular combination, introduces the two-index IDA for the Coulomb interaction, and concludes with DMRG implementation, wavefunction transfer, and energy extrapolation benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Gausslets are one of the few basis constructions for electronic structure that combine locality, orthonormality, variable resolution, and an accurate diagonal approximation for the electron-electron interaction, but the original construction is tied to one dimension. Radial gausslets extended this idea to atoms while leaving the angular degrees of freedom in spherical harmonics, so the atomic interaction remained only partially diagonal in the combined basis. Here we introduce generalized gausslets on the sphere and combine them shell by shell with radial gausslets to form an atom-centered basis in which the electron-electron interaction takes a two-index integral-diagonal form. The angular basis starts from localized spherical Gaussians and uses injection to make a low-$\ell$ spherical-harmonic subspace exact. Tests of the kinetic spectrum, low-$\ell$ Coulomb matrix elements, spherium, first-row Hartree--Fock calculations, and He exact diagonalization show systematic convergence with increasing angular resolution. We also develop DMRG methods for this basis, including compact MPOs, correlated small-space starting states, Givens-rotation transfers between nearby angular sizes, and embedded sampled variance extrapolation (ESVE). We show that this combination of ingredients can be used to solve the Be atom, with extrapolations in the number of angular functions but with fixed radial resolution, to within about 0.1 mH of the complete basis set limit exact energy. This shows that DMRG calculations of first row atoms which include both static and accurate dynamic correlation on the same footing are feasible.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04748"></a>
### [Automated Circuit Depth Reduction of Quantum Subroutines via Compilation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04748v1)

**Authors:** Folkert de Ronde, Stephan Wong, Sebastian Feld  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04748v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04748_figures/2605.04748_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Circuit representation of a standard GHZ state generation in a configuration linear to the number of qubits. Gates in between dotted lines can be executed in parallel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04748_figures/2605.04748_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Circuit representation of an improved GHZ state generation scheme with depth logarithmic in the number of qubits. Gates in between dotted lines can be executed in parallel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04748_figures/2605.04748_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Circuit representation of an improved GHZ state generation scheme with constant depth. The design allows gates to be added fully in parallel as the number of qubits increases. The Z symbol denotes measurement in the Z basis.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04748_figures/2605.04748_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: (a) Circuit representation of a standard forward CNOT chain, exhibiting linear depth in the number of involved qubits. (b) Representation of an improved CNOT chain circuit by using the gate property shown in Fig. 5c that reduces the circuit depth by half. (c) Circuit representation of the recursive application of the method shown in (b), repeated until no further improvement is possible. The recursive approach achieves a circuit depth logarithmic in the number of qubits.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04748_figures/2605.04748_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: (a) Circuit representation of a standard reversed CNOT chain, exhibiting linear depth in the number of qubits. (b) Recursive implementation of the reversed CNOT chain, where the optimization process is applied iteratively until no further improvement is possible. This results in logarithmic depth. (c) Gate transformation rule used to obtain the improved CNOT chain solution.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents an automated compiler designed to reduce the circuit depth of fundamental quantum subroutines like GHZ state creation and CNOT/CZ chains. By transforming sequential gate structures into parallelized versions, the compiler significantly reduces execution time and noise susceptibility, though it introduces a trade-off by increasing the total gate count.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quantum algorithms suffer from increasing circuit depth as they scale, leading to higher susceptibility to noise and longer execution times. Specifically, fundamental subroutines like GHZ state creation and CNOT/CZ chains often use sequential implementations that are not optimized for parallelism.

**Main result.** The development of a compiler-driven approach that automatically detects and transforms sequential subroutines into parallelized versions, achieving logarithmic depth for CNOT chains and constant depth for CZ chains and GHZ state creation, albeit with an increased gate count.

**Method.** A configurable compiler uses a single-pass detection algorithm to identify CNOT/CZ chains and GHZ structures, applying recursive decomposition for CNOTs and parallel layering for CZs, with a preference-based strategy to trade off gate count against depth.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on quantum circuit compilation for NISQ-era hardware, specifically targeting architectures utilizing CNOT (CX), CZ, and single-qubit rotation gates (RX, RY, RZ).

**Key observables.** Circuit depth, gate count, measurement count, and error susceptibility (decoherence, depolarization, and measurement noise).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit counts ranging from 5 to 95; optimization modes (Conservative vs. Always); and VQE ansatz types (EfficientSU2, RealAmplitudes, TwoLocal).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The optimization is most effective for chains longer than 5 gates; the depth evaluation is limited to a local scope (the chain plus the next 100 operations) to maintain computational efficiency.

**Figures summary.** Figures 1-3 compare different GHZ generation schemes (linear, logarithmic, and constant depth); Figures 4-6 illustrate the evolution and decomposition of CNOT and CZ chains; Figure 7 shows commutation rules; Figure 8 shows intertwined chain optimization; Figure 9 is a detection algorithm flowchart; and Figures 10-14 present performance benchmarks across various VQE configurations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of circuit depth, describes the compiler's detection and transformation algorithms for specific subroutines, details the optimization strategies for GHZ and CNOT/CZ chains, presents performance analysis via benchmarks and VQE integration, and discusses the inherent trade-offs and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optimizing quantum circuits by reducing circuit depth is essential for improving the efficiency and scalability of quantum algorithms, particularly as quantum hardware continues to evolve. This can be achieved by restructuring quantum algorithms to allow more parallelism. A compiler is needed to automatically detect and apply these optimizations. In this work, we focus on the optimization of two fundamental quantum subroutines: GHZ state creation and CNOT/CZ chain decomposition. Traditional implementations of these subroutines suffer from linearly increasing circuit depth, which limits scalability. We propose a compiler-driven approach that automatically detects and optimizes these two fundamental quantum subroutines. Our approach reduces circuit depth through constant-depth GHZ state creation, constant depth CZ chain decomposition, and logarithmic depth recursive CNOT chain decomposition, which enhance parallel execution. Performance analysis of benchmarked algorithms shows significant reductions in depth. However, our solution also results in an increased gate count, which makes our optimization a trade-off. The gate count for the CNOT chains is doubled, where logarithmic depth reduction is achieved. The reduced circuit depth results in more efficient algorithms by reducing execution time.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04945"></a>
### [Beyond Gates: Pulse Level Quantum Fourier Models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04945v1)

**Authors:** Melvin Strobl, Maja Franz, Lukas Scheller, Eileen Kuehn, Wolfgang Mauerer, Achim Streit  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04945v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04945_figures/2605.04945_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Illustration of our contribution. The optimisation trajectory of a QFM in L can be improved by extending the gate parameters θ through access to pulse parameters Π. This allows to escape local minima and increases the rank of the coefficient Jacobian rank(J). Practically, the combined optimisation of θ ⊕Π consistently lowers the mean squared error (MSE) towards a global optimum for all ansätze examined in this work.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04945_figures/2605.04945_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Count of active frequencies, calculated by the number of high-variance coefficients (see Eq. 3) exceeding a threshold τ &gt; 5 · 10−6. Pulse parameter variance is indicated by color.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04945_figures/2605.04945_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: MSE for all ansätze examined in this work, trained either only using unitary gate parameters (without and with a decomposition into basis gates), or in combination with pulse parameters. Results represent the average over ten different seeds used for both the model and data initialisation. Standard deviation is reported via error bars.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04945_figures/2605.04945_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Fidelity between gate- and pulse-level simulations for different levels of distortion σ2 λ, coloured by the number of pulse parameters |Π|.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04945_figures/2605.04945_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: MSE over the course of training for different ansätze and using either standard or decomposed unitary gates, or pulse gates. The inset shows a larger view of the first 100 steps.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that optimizing microwave pulse parameters instead of standard quantum gates can significantly enhance the training performance of Quantum Fourier Models. By breaking the rigid parameter couplings found in gate-level circuits, pulse-level control provides new directions for gradient descent to escape local minima without altering the model's fundamental expressibility.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how pulse-level control (optimizing microwave parameters) affects the training landscape and trainability of Quantum Fourier Models (QFMs) compared to standard gate-level parameterization.

**Main result.** Pulse-level control does not significantly change global expressibility but fundamentally improves local trainability by providing higher-dimensional escape routes from local minima, particularly for composite gates.

**Method.** The authors use an analytical framework to link pulse parameters to Fourier coefficients and perform numerical simulations on a 3-qubit system using the Adam optimizer and the Rotating Wave Approximation.

**Model / system.** Quantum Fourier Models (QFMs) implemented on superconducting transmon qubits, where the Hamiltonian includes static, drive, and dynamic components, and unitaries are controlled via pulse envelopes.

**Key observables.** Mean Squared Error (MSE), Fourier Coefficient Correlation (FCC), Expressibility (KL-divergence to 2-design), Jacobian rank, and fidelity/trace distance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate-level angles (theta), pulse parameters (amplitude, width, duration), and pulse-area scaling (lambda).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes coherent pulse dynamics, ignores hardware-specific noise/crosstalk, and utilizes the Rotating Wave Approximation (RWA) in a closed-system model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows improved optimization trajectories and Jacobian rank with pulse control; Figure 2 compares MSE across different ansätze and implementation levels; Figure 3 illustrates frequency activation via pulse variance; Figures 4 and 5 show the sensitivity of fidelity and MSE to pulse parameter distortions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QFM framework, defines the pulse-level control model, provides an analytical proof regarding escape directions in the optimization landscape, presents numerical simulations comparing gate vs. pulse levels, and concludes with a sensitivity analysis of the metrics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the domain of variational quantum algorithms, quantum Fourier models (QFMs) provide a mathematically well defined structure for quantum machine learning (QML). There has been a substantial amount of work on the scalability and trainability of such models showcasing the potential but also the limitations for the prospective application of QFMs. However, much less is known in the context of pulse-level quantum computing, where the microwave parameters that implement unitary operations on the hardware are used to perform computations directly instead of through the interface of quantum circuits. In this work, we evaluate QFMs through the lens of pulse parameters and link metrics such as expressibility and Fourier coefficient correlation (FCC) to this extended set of variational parameters. We show that while control over pulse shapes does not significantly alter the global expressibility or structural correlations of the Ansatz, it fundamentally alters the local optimisation landscape. For composite gates, independent pulse scalings replace a single logical angle by multiple independently tunable sub-angles. This relaxes the rigid monomial couplings induced by the gate-level parameterisation, and provides gradient descent with higher-dimensional escape routes, decoupling local parameter constraints and significantly boosting performance during training. Following an analytical proof, we show numerical results validating our theory on training a QFM with an exponential (ternary) feature map on a Fourier series with the same frequencies.

</details>

### [Distributed Quantum Error Correction with Bivariate Bicycle Codes in a Modular Architecture](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04663v1)

**Authors:** Nitish Kumar Chandra, Eneet Kaur, Reza Nejabati, Kaushik P. Seshadreesan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04663v1>

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>Fig. 2. Remote CNOT between QPU A and QPU B using a shared Bell pair. Here, 𝑐is the control qubit, 𝑡is the target qubit, and 𝑎,𝑏are communication qubits that share the Bell pair.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Fig. 3. QPU partitioning of the [[144, 12, 12]] bivariate bicycle code for 𝑁QPU = 6. The 12 𝑥-columns are divided into six blocks of width 𝑐= 2, and the highlighted cell (5, 2) lies in QPU 2. The markers show the qubit types 𝐿, 𝑋, 𝑍, and 𝑅. The overlaid blue and red edges show the 𝑋- and 𝑍-check connectivity of the highlighted cell, respectively, as defined in Eqs. (7) and (11). <sub>Fig. 4. Star network architecture in which multiple QPUs (6) are connected through a central quantum switch. Each QPU contains data and ancilla qubits with intra-module connectivity, together with communication qubits used to generate entangled pairs with the switch. The switch uses these entangled pairs, together with Bell-state measurements (BSM), to enable nonlocal operations between QPUs.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04663_figures/2605.04663_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. BB144 threshold fitting with quadratic alpha dependence. Logical error rate per cycle (𝑝𝐿) vs. physical error rate (𝑝) for different 𝛼values. The curves represent the BB ansatz fit, and asterisks mark the pseudo-thresholds 𝑝0(𝛼). The dashed lines show extrapolation to lower 𝑝.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper studies the performance of high-rate Bivariate Bicycle codes when implemented in a distributed, modular quantum computing architecture. It quantifies the trade-off between splitting the code across multiple processors and the performance penalty caused by noisy nonlocal gates. The findings provide design guidelines for scaling quantum error correction in modular hardware like trapped ions or neutral atoms.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how partitioning Bivariate Bicycle (BB) codes across a modular, distributed architecture affects their error-correction performance, specifically considering the increased noise from nonlocal operations.

**Main result.** The pseudo-threshold decreases as nonlocal noise increases, and coarser partitions (fewer QPUs) are more effective at higher noise levels (alpha >= 3).

**Method.** Monte Carlo simulations using a circuit-level noise model and BP+OSD decoding, combined with a new quadratic alpha-dependent extension of the BB code ansatz for error rate extrapolation.

**Model / system.** A modular star network architecture where multiple quantum processors (QPUs) are connected via a central switch using entanglement-assisted Bell pairs, applicable to trapped ion and neutral atom platforms.

**Key observables.** Logical error rate (pL) and pseudo-threshold (p0).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of QPUs (4, 6, 12), nonlocal noise scaling factor (alpha), and the [[144, 12, 12]] BB code parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Processors have all-to-all internal connectivity; nonlocal noise is captured by a single scaling factor alpha; and the expansion of code coefficients is truncated at the quadratic term.

**Figures summary.** Circuit diagrams for syndrome extraction, schematics of remote CNOT gates and star network architecture, visualizations of code partitioning, and plots showing logical error rates and pseudo-threshold degradation.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the BB code and distributed architecture, describes the noise model and circuit implementation, presents Monte Carlo simulation results and the analytical fitting ansatz, and concludes with architectural design considerations and future work.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum low density parity check (qLDPC) codes, particularly bivariate bicycle (BB) codes, achieve competitive fault tolerance thresholds while offering substantially higher encoding rates than planar surface codes. However, their intrinsically long-range stabilizer structure makes them difficult to implement on monolithic devices with nearest neighbor connectivity and limited qubit capacity. In this work, we study the realization of a BB code in a modular multiprocessor architecture, where quantum processors are interconnected through shared Bell pairs. We consider processors with all to all internal connectivity, which is feasible on trapped ion and neutral atom platforms, enabling flexible local gate execution while inter-processor (nonlocal) gates are mediated by shared entanglement. We describe a star network architecture that can realize this distributed setting. We partition the qubits of the [[144,12,12]] BB code across 4, 6, and 12 quantum processors and analyze the resulting logical error rates and pseudo-threshold performance under circuit level noise by varying the number of processors and a scaling factor that captures the additional noise associated with nonlocal operations. We use Monte Carlo simulations with BP+OSD decoding and extend the previously known BB code ansatz to the distributed setting. Our results provide architectural insight and design considerations for distributed BB codes in modular quantum computing architectures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Exact SU(2) Yang-Mills Waves from a Simple Ansatz](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04964v1)

**Authors:** Yu-Xuan Zhang, Jing-Ling Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04964v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04964_figures/2605.04964_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04964_figures/2605.04964_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04964_figures/2605.04964_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04964_figures/2605.04964_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04964_figures/2605.04964_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method to find exact wave solutions for SU(2) Yang-Mills theory by using a specific algebraic ansatz. It identifies a new class of nonlinear waves that possess a constant field offset and violate the principle of superposition, providing new insights into non-Abelian self-interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Finding exact, time-dependent, propagating wave solutions to the (3+1)-dimensional SU(2) Yang-Mills equations, which are difficult to solve due to inherent nonlinearities.

**Main result.** The discovery of three distinct families of closed-form solutions: linear (Abelian) waves, genuinely nonlinear self-interacting waves with a constant field offset, and a pure gauge solution.

**Method.** The authors employ a new ansatz using a y-dependent rotated Pauli basis and a specific phase dependence to reduce the nonlinear partial differential equations to nine algebraic constraints.

**Model / system.** The model is a sourceless SU(2) Yang-Mills theory in (3+1)-dimensional Minkowski spacetime, characterized by the field strength tensor and the gauge coupling constant g.

**Key observables.** Constant color-electric field offset, energy density nodes, and the time-averaged color-electric field.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Wave number k, frequency omega, gauge coupling constant g, rotation parameter lambda, and discrete topological parity parameters xi and eta.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The solutions are derived based on a specific, simplified ansatz and are restricted to the sourceless (vacuum) case in (3+1) dimensions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of nonlinear YM waves, proposes a new ansatz using a rotated Pauli basis, reduces the field equations to algebraic constraints, classifies the resulting solutions into three families, and discusses their physical properties and topological signatures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a simple ansatz to construct exact wave solutions of the sourceless SU(2) Yang-Mills equations in (3+1) dimensions. The ansatz employs a $y$-dependent rotated Pauli basis and assumes a phase $θ=kz-ωt$ dependence for the gauge potentials. Owing to this ansatz, the nonlinear field equations reduce to nine algebraic constraints, whose complete solution yields three families of exact waves. Family I describes linear (Abelian) electromagnetic waves embedded in the non-Abelian theory; all commutator terms vanish and the dispersion relation is $ω=kc$. Family II represents genuinely nonlinear self-interacting waves that also propagate at the speed of light but exhibit a constant field offset, nonvanishing commutators, and do not obey superposition. The constant offset is gauge-invariant and gives rise to a non-zero time-averaged color-electric field. The energy density has nodes whose position ($θ=0$ or $θ=π$) is controlled by a discrete topological parameter $ξη=\pm1$, providing an observable signature. Family III is a pure gauge solution with vanishing field strengths, valid for arbitrary $k$ and $ω$ without any dispersion relation. All solutions are closed-form and provide new insights into how non-Abelian self-interactions fundamentally alter wave propagation.

</details>

### [Field-induced asymmetric band flattening and ideal quantum geometry in rhombohedral graphene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05199v1)

**Authors:** Hongyun Zhang, Jinxi Lu, Size Wu, Yijie Wang, Kai Liu, Fei Wang, Wanying Chen, Lingzhi Wen, Jinling Zhou, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Jose Avila, Pavel Dudin, Matthew D. Watson, Takafumi Sato, Pu Yu, Wenhui Duan, Zhida Song, Guorui Chen, Shuyun Zhou

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

**Summary.** This paper uses NanoARPES to show that applying a displacement field to rhombohedral pentalayer graphene causes the conduction band to flatten and develop ideal quantum geometry while the valence band becomes M-shaped. This asymmetric evolution provides a microscopic explanation for why exotic topological phases like the FQAHE are found exclusively in the electron-doped regime.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the microscopic origin of the electron-hole asymmetry in the phase diagram of rhombohedral graphene, specifically why topological phases like the FQAHE emerge only on the electron-doped side under large displacement fields.

**Main result.** The study reveals that a displacement field induces asymmetric band flattening, where the conduction band becomes extremely flat and acquires ideal quantum geometry, while the valence band develops an M-shaped dispersion.

**Method.** The researchers used nanospot angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (NanoARPES) with in situ electrostatic gating, combined with tight-binding modeling and calculations of the quantum geometric tensor.

**Model / system.** The system is rhombohedral pentalayer graphene (R5G) encapsulated in hexagonal Boron Nitance, modeled using a tight-binding Hamiltonian that includes interlayer hopping parameters and a gate-induced potential difference.

**Key observables.** Band dispersion, gap opening, spectral weight redistribution, layer polarization, band curvature, Berry curvature, and quantum geometry idealness (Ik).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Displacement field (D/epsilon0), interlayer potential difference (Delta), carrier density (ne), and the quantum geometric tensor.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model treats the graphene layer as an equipotential reference and assumes the electronic structure can be captured by a single-valley tight-binding model.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the unified phase diagram of RG; Fig 2 and 3 display experimental electronic structures, gap opening, and layer polarization under gating; Fig 4 illustrates field-tunable asymmetric band curvatures; Fig 5 maps the Berry curvature and quantum geometry idealness.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a phase diagram highlighting electron-hole asymmetry, proceeds to experimental NanoARPES observations of band evolution and layer polarization, follows with a theoretical analysis of band curvature and quantum geometry, and concludes by linking these microscopic features to the observed topological phases.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rhombohedral graphene exhibits an exceptionally diverse array of correlated phases that depend sensitively on the displacement field. Compiling reported phases into a unified phase diagram reveals a pronounced field-dependent electron-hole asymmetry: correlated states on the hole-doped side emerge at small displacement fields, whereas the fractional quantum anomalous Hall effect (FQAHE) is observed exclusively on the electron-doped side under large displacement fields. This stark asymmetry highlights the need to understand how flat bands evolve with displacement fields. Here, we directly visualize the field-induced electron-hole asymmetric band flattening in rhombohedral pentalayer graphene (R5G) using nanospot angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy with electrostatic gating. Beyond gap opening and spectral weight redistribution indicative of layer polarization, the gating field drives a strongly asymmetric modification of the flat bands: the flat valence band (FVB) evolves into an M-shaped dispersion at high field, whereas the flat conduction band (FCB) progressively flattens with increasing field. Comparison with calculations identifies critical parameters governing the band curvature of R5G, from which the resulting finite Berry curvature and near-ideal quantum geometry support the emergence of topological phases under electron doping at large fields. These results establish a direct link between the asymmetric phase diagram, band structure evolution, and quantum geometry, providing a microscopic framework for understanding correlated and topological phases in rhombohedral graphene.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04604"></a>
### [Generative Quantum-inspired Kolmogorov-Arnold Eigensolver](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04604v1)

**Authors:** Yu-Cheng Lin, Yu-Chao Hsu, I-Shan Tsai, Chun-Hua Lin, Kuo-Chung Peng, Jiun-Cheng Jiang, Yun-Yuan Wang, Tzung-Chi Huang, Tai-Yue Li, Kuan-Cheng Chen, Samuel Yen-Chi Chen, Nan-Yow Chen

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04604_figures/2605.04604_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Architecture of the Jiang–Huang–Chen–Goan Net- work (JHCG Net) [44]. The JHCG Net uses an encoder– processor–decoder structure, where a QKAN-based latent processor forms the Hybrid QKAN (HQKAN) module and introduces quantum-inspired nonlinear transformations in the latent space.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04604_figures/2605.04604_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Overview of the GQKAE framework. The HQKANsformer defines an autoregressive distribution over operator sequences, which are used to construct candidate quantum circuits. The generated circuits prepare trial states that are measured to obtain sampled Slater determinants. These determinants span a truncated subspace of dimension Ddmax, where the Hamiltonian is classically diagonalized to compute the QSCI energy. This energy is converted into a reward signal for updating the generative model via GRPO loss [47]. The proposed method modifies only the generative backbone, while preserving the QSCI-based evaluation workflow.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04604_figures/2605.04604_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Optimization history showing the best-so-far energy error relative to the CASCI baseline up to each iteration. Solid curves represent the mean values averaged over five independent optimization trials with different random seeds, while the shaded regions indicate one standard deviation. The horizontal dashed lines denote the chemical accuracy threshold (1.6 mHa).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04604_figures/2605.04604_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Potential energy surface of the six target molecules. Subfigures present the energy variations with respect to bond lengths, bond angles, and intermolecular distances. The predictions of GQKAE (purple squares) and GQE (green circles) are compared against HF (grey dotted lines), CCSD (grey dashed lines), SCI (red dash-dot lines), CASCI (black solid lines), and VQE (blue triangles). The sequence lengths (L) of the generated circuits were fixed based on computational efficiency tradeoffs: L = 20 for H4, L = 90 for N2, L = 70 for LiH, L = 20 for C2H6, L = 130 for H2O and L = 50 for (H2O)2.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04604_figures/2605.04604_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5 plots the absolute energy differences relative to CASCI on a logarithmic scale, demarcating the chemical accuracy threshold (1.6 mHa) alongside HF, CCSD, and VQE baselines. For H4, C2H6, and LiH (Fig. 5a, 5d, and 5c), both GQKAE and GQE exhibit outstanding stability and accuracy. Across all scanned distances and angles, the absolute errors remain firmly situated within or well below the chemical accuracy zone. In the case of the N2 dissociation profile (Fig. 5b), we observe a distinct phenomenon. Between the bond lengths of 1.0 ˚A and 2.0 ˚A, the standard deviations for both models expand significantly. This region corresponds to the breaking of the nitrogen triple bond, a classical...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a more efficient generative model for constructing quantum circuits used in electronic structure calculations. By replacing heavy transformer layers with quantum-inspired Kolmogorov-Arnold networks, the authors significantly reduce the classical computational cost while maintaining high accuracy for molecular energy estimations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The scalability and parameter overhead of GPT-style Generative Quantum Eigensolvers (GQE) in quantum chemistry workflows, particularly as molecular active spaces and circuit lengths increase.

**Main result.** The proposed GQKAE architecture achieves chemical accuracy comparable to GQE while reducing trainable parameters and memory by approximately 66% and improving wall-time performance.

**Method.** A generative framework that replaces standard transformer feed-forward networks with hybrid quantum-inspired Kolmogorov-Arnold network (HQKAN) modules using data re-uploading activations.

**Model / system.** The method targets molecular electronic structure problems, specifically estimating ground-state energies for systems including H4, N2, LiH, C2H6, H2O, and the H2O dimer using a UCCSD-derived operator pool.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy, chemical accuracy (error < 1.6 mHa), and potential energy surfaces (PES).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Subspace truncation dimension (d_max), number of measurement shots (N_shots), and circuit sequence length (L).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The circuit sequence length L must be manually specified; the method assumes the availability of a classical simulator like CUDA-Q for evaluation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the HQKANsformer architecture; Figure 2 illustrates the GQKAE workflow; Figure 3 displays optimization history for various molecules; Figure 4 shows potential energy surfaces; Figures 6 and 7 show error convergence with respect to shots and subspace truncation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability bottleneck of GQE, proposes the GQKAE architecture using HQKAN modules, details the training via GRPO and evaluation via QSCI, presents numerical benchmarks on various molecular systems, and discusses limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-performance computing (HPC) is increasingly important for scalable quantum chemistry workflows that couple classical generative models, quantum circuit simulation, and selected configuration interaction postprocessing. We present the generative quantum-inspired Kolmogorov-Arnold eigensolver (GQKAE), a parameter-efficient extension of the generative quantum eigensolver (GQE) for quantum chemistry. GQKAE replaces the parameter-heavy feed-forward network components in GPT-style generative eigensolvers with hybrid quantum-inspired Kolmogorov-Arnold network modules, forming a compact HQKANsformer backbone. The method preserves autoregressive operator selection and the quantum-selected configuration interaction evaluation pipeline, while using single-qubit DatA Re-Uploading ActivatioN modules to provide expressive nonlinear mappings. Numerical benchmarks on H4, N2, LiH, C2H6, H2O, and the H2O dimer show that GQKAE achieves chemical accuracy comparable to the GPT-based GQE architecture, while reducing trainable parameters and memory by approximately 66% and improving wall-time performance. For strongly correlated systems such as N2 and LiH, GQKAE also improves convergence behavior and final energy errors. These results indicate that quantum-inspired Kolmogorov-Arnold networks can reduce classical-side overhead while preserving circuit-generation quality, offering a scalable route for HPC-quantum co-design on near-term quantum platforms.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04402"></a>
### [Imaging GHz surface acoustic wave modes in electrostricted LaAlO$_3$/SrTiO$_3$ heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04402v1)

**Authors:** Ranjani Ramachandran, Sayanwita Biswas, Prithwijit Mandal, Kyoungjun Lee, Madeleine Msall, Chang-Beom Eom, Patrick Irvin, Jeremy Levy, Mingyun Yuan  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04402v1>

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

(d) S parameters measured from delay line DL2 of kSAW ¼ 2 lm with VDC1 ¼ VDC2 ¼ 30 V. <details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04402_figures/2605.04402_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1(c) shows an illustration of a typical sample layout and the experimental setup. We started with four unit cells of LAO on STO (001) grown as described in the supplementary material, Sec. I and selectively etched away the LAO by reactive ion etching47 to define the “canvases” containing the 2DEG, shown by the white dot­ ted line. The IDTs were patterned using standard electron beam lithography and metalized with a stack of Ti/Al/Ti with thicknesses of 10/50/10 nm, leading to a metallization thickness of t ¼ 70 nm. DC biases, VDC1 and VDC2, are applied across IDT fingers of opposite polarities in the input and output IDTs, respectively, to induce elec­ trostriction on STO. We...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04402_figures/2605.04402_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (a) S21 from DL4c with kSAW ¼ 4 lm and VDC1 ¼ 30 V and different values of VDC2, with a 10 dB offset between adjacent curves. Black arrow indicates the fslow mode. (b) S21max as a function of VDC2. (c) S21max decaying with time after zeroing VDC1 and VDC2 from 30 V. The initial response is fitted to an exponential decay S21max ¼ Ae−t=s with A ¼ −50:763 dBm and s ¼ 55:88 s. (d) Time-domain echoes of S21 from DL4c with 250 kSAW delay at VDC1 ¼ VDC2 ¼ 30 V. <sub>Figure 3(d) shows AAFM measurements of the SAW signal along the delay line at different frequencies. </details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how to generate high-frequency surface acoustic waves in LAO/STO heterostructures using electric-field-induced electrostriction. By identifying new acoustic modes and achieving extremely low signal loss, the work provides a new method for controlling electrons and other quantum states in oxide-based platforms.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of generating and controlling Surface Acoustic Waves (SAWs) in SrTiO3-based heterostructures due to the lack of intrinsic piezoelectricity at room temperature.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated GHz-frequency SAW generation via electrostriction in LAO/STO, identifying two distinct modes including a previously unobserved shear horizontal-type mode with ultra-low propagation loss.

**Method.** The study uses RF spectroscopy (VNA) to measure S-parameters and Atomic Acoustic Force Microscopy (AAFM) for sub-micron resolution imaging of SAW waveforms.

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of LaAlO3/SrTiO3 (LAO/STO) heterostructures where a DC bias induces electrostriction, creating a thin-film waveguide capable of supporting acoustic modes.

**Key observables.** S-parameters (transmission S21 and reflection S11), SAW propagation loss, SAW velocity, and spatial waveform imaging via AAFM.

**Important parameters / regimes.** GHz frequency range (up to 2.2 GHz), wavelengths of 2, 4, and 8 micrometers, and propagation loss on the order of 10^-3 dB per wavelength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The identification of the second mode as SH-type is noted as a possibility rather than a definitive classification.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the electrostriction mechanism, experimental setup, and S-parameter transmission peaks; Figure 2 shows the enhancement of transmission with DC bias.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for SAW-based electron shuttling, describes the electrostriction-induced SAW generation mechanism, presents RF and AAFM experimental results, and discusses the implications for future quantum device integration.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The LaAlO$_3$/SrTiO$_3$ (LAO/STO) interface hosts a gate-tunable superconducting two-dimensional electron gas (2DEG) which can be programmed to create quantum devices such as ballistic electron waveguides and quantum dots. To fully exploit this platform for quantum transport, a key requirement is the ability to shuttle single electrons, electron pairs, and other exotic states between spatially separated devices with precision. Surface acoustic waves (SAWs), which travel along the surface of a solid, offer a powerful route to achieve this through their moving electrical potential that captures and transfers electrons. %acoustoelectric coupling. In particular, SAWs in the GHz regime enable fast, controlled transport of individual quantum particles. Although this approach is well-explored in GaAs-based 2DEG, SAW generation in STO remains largely unexplored due to the lack of intrinsic piezoelectricity at room temperature. Here, we investigate room-temperature SAWs in LAO/STO and observe SAW modes up to 2.2 GHz with very low propagation loss of the order $10^{-3}$ dB per wavelength. To directly visualize these modes, we employ Atomic Acoustic Force Microscopy (AAFM), achieving sub-micron resolution imaging of the SAW wave forms, providing insight into the electrostriction-induced SAW generation mechanism. Our measurements indicate a shear horizontal-type mode, which provides the ability to couple to in-plane degrees of freedom for future acoustoelectric and quantum device applications. This work studies the fundamentals of SAW excitation and propagation on STO, a widely used and commercially available substrate, enabling straightforward coupling of SAWs to a broad range of materials that can be grown or transferred onto STO.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04825"></a>
### [Improving FMQA via Initial Training Data Design Considering Marginal Bit Coverage in One-Hot Encoding](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04825v1)

**Authors:** Taiga Hayashi, Yuya Seki, Kotaro Terada, Yosuke Mukasa, Shuta Kikuchi, Shu Tanaka  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04825v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04825_figures/2605.04825_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Comparison of the optimization processes of each method for HPA103-1. The horizontal axis represents the number of function evalua- tions, and the vertical axis represents the best value found. The gray shaded region corresponds to the evaluations used to construct the initial training dataset.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04825_figures/2605.04825_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Comparison of the optimization processes of each method for HPA103-2. The horizontal axis represents the number of function evalua- tions, and the vertical axis represents the best value found. The gray shaded region corresponds to the evaluations used to construct the initial training dataset.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04825_figures/2605.04825_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Comparison of the number of times each bit takes the value one in the bit arrays obtained by one-hot encoding for the datasets generated by (a) Conv-FMQA, (b) LHS-FMQA, and (c) Sobol’-FMQA on HPA103-2. The horizontal axis represents the number of function evaluations, and the vertical axis represents the distribution of the number of times each bit in the dataset takes the value one at each function evaluation. Red indicates bits that never take the value one, green indicates bits that take the value one only once, blue indicates bits that take the value one two to nine times, and black indicates bits that take the value one ten or more times.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.04825_figures/2605.04825_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. The horizontal axis represents the number of function evaluations, and the vertical axis represents the distribution of the number of times each bit in the dataset takes the value one at each function evaluation. Red indicates bits that never take the value one, green indicates bits that take the value one only once, blue indicates bits that take the value one two to nine times, and black indicates bits that take the value one ten or more times.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper improves the FMQA optimization framework by ensuring that all binary variables in a one-hot encoded system are represented in the initial training dataset. By using Latin Hypercube and Sobol' sampling, the authors prevent biased parameter updates, leading to more effective searches on Ising machines.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** In Factorization Machine with Quadratic-optimization Annealing (FMQA), one-hot encoding of variables can lead to 'uncovered' binary bits that never take the value one in the initial training data, causing vanishing gradients and biased QUBO coefficients.

**Main result.** The proposed LHS-FMQA and Sobol'-FMQA methods, which ensure complete marginal bit coverage, achieve higher mean final cruising speeds in wing-shape optimization benchmarks compared to the baseline FMQA.

**Method.** The authors use space-filling sampling techniques, specifically Latin Hypercube Sampling (LHS) and Sobol' sequences, to design initial training data that guarantees every binary variable in the one-hot encoding is activated at least once.

**Model / system.** The framework uses a Factorization Machine (FM) as a surrogate model for black-box optimization, which is then mapped to a Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) form compatible with Ising machine hardware.

**Key observables.** Final mean cruising speed (m/s) and the optimization gain (improvement over initial best value).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of design variables (17 and 32), number of discrete levels (M=32), and initial training sample size (N0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the use of one-hot encoding for discretized variables and focuses on marginal bit coverage rather than pairwise bit coverage.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the optimization progress for a 17-variable problem; Figures 3 and 4 show the distribution of bit occurrences, highlighting the reduction of 'never active' bits in the proposed methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the gradient vanishing problem in FMQA, proposes LHS and Sobol' sampling methods to ensure marginal bit coverage, validates these methods using HPA wing-shape optimization benchmarks, and discusses limitations regarding pairwise coverage and space-filling effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Factorization machine with quadratic-optimization annealing (FMQA) is a black-box optimization method that combines a factorization machine (FM) surrogate with QUBO-based search by an Ising machine. When FMQA is applied to integer or discretized continuous variables via one-hot encoding, uniform random initial sampling can leave many binary variables never active in the initial training data, and the corresponding FM parameters receive no direct gradient updates from the observed responses. We address this by designing the initial training data to achieve complete marginal bit coverage, namely, ensuring that every binary variable obtained by one-hot encoding takes the value one at least once. We use two space-filling sampling methods, Latin hypercube sampling (LHS) and the Sobol' sequence, yielding LHS-FMQA and Sobol'-FMQA. On the human-powered aircraft wing-shape optimization benchmark with 17 and 32 design variables, both proposed methods achieved numerically higher mean final cruising speeds than the baseline FMQA, with the advantage more pronounced on the 32-variable problem.

</details>

### [Interaction-controlled localization in one-dimensional chain: From edges to domain walls](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05064v1)

**Authors:** Rahul Samanta, Sudin Ganguly, Santanu K. Maiti  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05064v1>

The insets show the corresponding local probability amplitudes |ψi|2 for these two states, represented by the red and green curves, re- spectively. Site-resolved (b) charge density ni and (c) spin density mi plotted as a function of site index. The alternat- ing red and blue dots in (b) and (c) distinguish adjacent site indices.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that in an extended Hubbard SSH chain, the spatial character of localized states is determined by the competition between on-site and nearest-neighbor interactions rather than the band topology. It identifies a transition from edge-localized spin-density waves to mid-chain charge-density-wave domain walls based on the $2V/U$ ratio. This establishes that these localized excitations are intrinsic, correlation-driven phenomena.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether localized states in a 1D chain are driven by underlying band topology (SSH model) or by electron-electron interactions (Hubbard $U$ and $V$).

**Main result.** The localization of states is controlled by the ratio $2V/U$ rather than band topology, leading to edge-localized spin-density-wave (SDW) modes for $U > 2V$ and mid-chain charge-density-wave (CDW) domain walls for $U < 2V$.

**Method.** The authors use a self-consistent generalized Hartree-Fock mean-field approximation to solve the Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** A half-filled, one-dimensional Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) chain described by an extended Hubbard model featuring alternating hopping amplitudes ($t_1, t_2$), on-site repulsion ($U$), and nearest-neighbor interaction ($V$).

**Key observables.** Energy spectrum (eigenvalues), local probability amplitudes, site-resolved charge density, and site-resolved spin density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** On-site interaction $U$, nearest-neighbor interaction $V$, the ratio $2V/U$, and hopping amplitudes $t_1$ and $t_2$ (covering topological, critical, and trivial regimes).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study relies on the Hartree-Fock mean-field approximation, which neglects certain many-body correlation effects beyond the decoupling of interaction terms.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the energy spectrum, spatial localization of wavefunctions, and density profiles (charge and spin) across different interaction regimes and hopping phases, including the transition from edge modes to mid-chain domain walls.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SSH-Hubbard model, details the Hartree-Fock method, presents results for different interaction ratios ($U$ vs $V$) across topological, critical, and trivial hopping regimes, and concludes with a summary of interaction-driven localization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using Hartree-Fock mean-field approach, we study the role of on-site ($U$) and extended ($V$) Hubbard interactions on the existence and evolution of edge modes in a half-filled Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) chain. We analyze the energy spectrum, local probability amplitudes, and site-resolved charge and spin density profiles across topological, critical, and trivial hopping regimes. We find that the localization of bound states is controlled by the ratio $2V/U$, with edge spin-density-wave modes for $U>2V$ and mid-chain charge-density-wave domain walls for $U<2V$, independent of band topology. These results establish the correlation-driven origin of localized states in finite one-dimensional chains.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05137"></a>
### [Lattice Tadpoles](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05137v1)

<sub>Figure 2 is a sketch of a polygon containing P1. P1 occurs three times on at least one self-avoiding walk so P1 is a Kesten pattern and it occurs on all except exponentially few sufficiently long polygons. For the proof of this theorem we could take m = 2. If pn( ¯P1) is the number of polygons on which P1 does not occur than lim supn→∞n−1 log pn( ¯P1) &lt; κ2. We can convert a polygon containing P1 into a tadpole by either (a) deleting the edge (0, 0) −(0, 1) and adding the edge (0, 0) −(1, 0) or (b) by deleting the edge (2, 0) −(2, −1) and adding the edge (1, 0) −(2, 0). See Figure 3 for a sketch of case (a). Each construction gives a tadpole and one gives a tadpole with the tail inside the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.05137_figures/2605.05137_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: A tadpole where the head is a trefoil and the tail pierces the head once.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides rigorous mathematical proofs regarding the growth rates of lattice-based tadpole structures. It demonstrates how topological constraints, such as requiring the head of a tadpole to be unknotted, influence the asymptotic counting of these configurations in 2D and 3D lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the asymptotic counting and growth rates of 'tadpoles' (graphs consisting of a cycle/head and a path/tail) embedded in d-dimensional hypercubic lattices, specifically looking at how topological constraints like knot type and surface piercing affect these rates.

**Main result.** The author proves that the exponential growth rate of tadpoles is generally the same as self-avoiding walks, but identifies specific scaling regimes where constraints like an unknotted head or a fixed head-to-tail ratio alter the growth rate.

**Method.** The study uses rigorous combinatorial and probabilistic methods, including Kesten's Pattern Theorem, Madras' End Pattern Theorem, and construction techniques involving the concatenation of polygons and walks.

**Model / system.** The model consists of self-avoiding walks (SAWs) and polygons on d-dimensional hypercubic lattices (Z^2 and Z^3), specifically focusing on the topology of tadpoles (lassos).

**Key observables.** The growth rate (connective constant) of the number of tadpoles, the knot type of the head, and the number of times the tail pierces the surface spanned by the head.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Total number of edges (n), number of edges in the head (h), the ratio of head to total edges (alpha), and the knot type (K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results assume the structures are embedded in hypercubic lattices and analyze specific scaling regimes where the head and tail grow linearly with n.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 illustrates a tadpole with a trefoil knot head and a piercing tail; Figure 5 displays various graph topologies including twin-tailed and two-tailed tadpoles.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents rigorous proofs for the asymptotic behavior of tadpoles, moving from general growth rates to specific constraints on knot types and geometric configurations like surface piercing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We prove several rigorous results about the asymptotic behaviour of the numbers of tadpoles (or lassos) embedded in a lattice, including cases where the head is subject to a constraint like being unknotted, or where the tail pierces the surface spanned by the head.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04611"></a>
### [Melting upon cooling in a quantum magnet](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04611v1)
