**Summary.** This paper reports the discovery of a 'spin Pomeranchuk effect' in the quantum magnet ErTa7O19, where cooling the system causes a long-range ordered magnetic phase to melt into a short-range correlated state. This counterintuitive phenomenon arises from the competition between exchange and dipolar interactions. The findings provide a new way to understand phase transformations in frustrated magnetic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the 'spin Pomeranchuk effect' in the quantum magnet ErTa7O19, where the system undergoes an unconventional transition from a long-range ordered state to a short-range correlated state upon cooling.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that the Ising-like triangular-lattice antiferromagnet ErTa7O19 exhibits 'melting upon cooling,' transitioning from a three-sublattice long-range ordered phase to a short-range correlated spin-stripe state due to the competition between exchange and dipolar interactions.

**Method.** The researchers used inelastic neutron scattering, electron spin resonance, and SQUID magnetometry for experimental characterization, combined with Crystal Electric Field (CEF) modeling, Finite-Temperature Lanczos Method (FTLM), and Monte Carlo simulations for theoretical analysis.

**Model / system.** The system is ErTa7O19, a rare-earth heptatantalate featuring Er3+ ions on a triangular lattice with strong Ising-like anisotropy. The model is an XXZ exchange Hamiltonian supplemented by long-range dipolar interactions.

**Key observables.** Magnetic propagation vectors (qK and qM), magnetic structure factor, magnetic entropy, specific heat, g-factors, and magnetization curves.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Néel temperature TN = 100 mK, transition temperatures Th = 65 mK and Tl = 25 mK, and high anisotropy ratio (gc/gab approx 10).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a 2D model to describe the physics, with 3D ordering treated via a mean-field approximation for interlayer interactions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1A shows a schematic of Er3+ magnetic moments and interactions; Figure S1 shows XRD Rietveld refinement and Laue diffraction; Figure S2 shows low-temperature magnetization and specific heat; Figure S3 shows CEF-related data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of inverse melting, describes the crystal structure and magnetic properties of ErTa7O19, presents experimental neutron and magnetic data, provides theoretical modeling via FTLM and CEF to explain the phase sequence, and concludes with the implications of the spin Pomeranchuk effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Heating enhances thermal fluctuations and typically leads to melting of solids, but in exceptional cases, heating can also cause liquids to solidify. The paradigm of this counterintuitive phenomenon is solidification of liquid $^3$He upon increasing temperature, known as the Pomeranchuk effect. Here we show that such inverse melting also appears in quantum magnetism. We find that, on cooling, the Ising-like triangular-lattice antiferromagnet erbium heptatantalate first develops a three-sublattice long-range magnetic order -- analogous to a solid -- which then, unexpectedly, melts at even lower temperatures into a short-range correlated spin-stripe state -- analogous to a liquid. We propose that such an unprecedented ``spin Pomeranchuk effect" can generically arise from strong competition between spin-spin interactions in frustrated magnets, and provides a novel avenue to transformations between exotic magnetic phases.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.05101"></a>
### [Microscopic evidence for imaginary charge density wave in a kagome metal](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05101v1)

**Authors:** S. Suetsugu, F. Hori, M. Shibata, S. Kitagawa, K. Ishida, T. Asaba, S. Nakazawa, Q. Li, H. -H. Wen, T. Shibauchi, H. Kontani, Y. Matsuda  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05101v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. Crystal structure, loop current, and charge density wave orders. a, b, Crystal</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.05101_figures/2605.05101_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Temperature dependence of 121Sb2 NQR spectra. a, 121Sb2 NQR spectra measured</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.05101_figures/2605.05101_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Loop current order and internal magnetic fields. a, Triple-q iCDW state. This</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports the discovery of microscopic evidence for an imaginary charge density wave in the kagome metal CsV3Sb5. By using NMR/NQR spectroscopy, the authors identified spontaneous local magnetic fields arising from chiral loop currents. This establishes a new form of quantum order characterized by broken time-reversal symmetry.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining whether the anomalous electronic phenomena in the kagome metal CsV3Sb5 arise from real charge density waves or a controversial 'imaginary charge density wave' (iCDW) associated with spontaneous time-reversal symmetry breaking.

**Main result.** The study provides microscopic evidence for a pure iCDW state characterized by chiral loop currents, evidenced by spontaneous local magnetic fields of ~1 mT and a triple-q configuration.

**Method.** Nuclear Quadrupole Resonance (NQR) and Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (NMR) spectroscopy on the 121-Sb site, utilizing field-dependent and temperature-dependent measurements to disentangle magnetic and electric effects.

**Model / system.** The kagome nonmagnetic metal CsV3Sb5, specifically focusing on the Sb1 and Sb2 sites within the 2D V-Sb kagome layers, modeled via a triple-q loop current configuration.

**Key observables.** Spectral linewidth (FWHM), spectral centroid asymmetry, local hyperfine field strength (h_loc), and transition frequency shifts.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition temperatures T_CDW (~94 K), T* (~120 K), and local magnetic fields (~1 mT).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The assumption that the observed spectral asymmetry follows a triple-q distribution where sites experience hyperfine fields in a 1:3 ratio.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the temperature dependence of NQR linewidth broadening, the splitting of resonant frequencies under a c-axis magnetic field, and the successful fitting of experimental spectra using a triple-q model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the controversy of iCDW, presents NQR/NMR evidence of anomalous broadening at T*, demonstrates the magnetic origin of this broadening through field-dependent NMR, validates the triple-q model, and concludes with the identification of spontaneous time-reversal symmetry breaking.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dissipationless charge transport without any energy loss is one of the most fascinating phenomena in condensed matter physics. This extraordinary state manifests in two well-established systems: superconductors and quantum Hall systems. A proposed third category is associated with chiral loop current order, characterized by the spontaneous formation of microscopic electric current loops. The microscopic origin of these currents stems from imaginary hopping terms, conceptualized as an imaginary charge density wave (iCDW). Despite extensive investigations, its existence remains highly controversial. Here we report site-selective spectroscopic evidence for a pure iCDW in the kagome nonmagnetic metal CsV$_3$Sb$_5$. Nuclear quadrupole resonance spectra at out-of-plane $^{121}$Sb site sensitive to in-plane currents reveal anomalous broadening below $T^*\approx$120 K, coinciding with the nematic transition well above the real charge density wave (CDW). Under magnetic fields, the spectra exhibit asymmetric lineshapes, demonstrating that this broadening purely originates from magnetic effects rather than from electric quadrupolar effects associated with CDW fluctuations. The observed lineshapes are quantitatively consistent with ~1 mT local fields induced by chiral loop currents, indicating spontaneous time-reversal symmetry breaking. This microscopic identification of the long-sought pure iCDW establishes a novel form of quantum order, potentially revolutionizing our understanding of exotic electronic states in quantum materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new quantum algorithm for solving partial differential equations using the SLAC derivative discretization. By combining LCU-based block-encoding with the Quantum Shannon Wavelet Transform, the authors achieve an efficient, multi-scale approach that avoids the unphysical artifacts of standard lattice methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and field theory simulations, as it provides a way to implement non-local operators (essential for avoiding fermion doubling) on quantum hardware with efficient scaling.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing efficient quantum algorithms to solve partial differential equations (PDEs) using SLAC derivatives, which are non-local and result in dense matrices that are difficult to simulate classically.

**Main result.** The authors construct efficient LCU-based block-encodings for first-order derivative and Laplacian operators in the SLAC representation, achieving polylogarithmic gate complexity and reduced condition numbers via wavelet preconditioning.

**Method.** The approach utilizes Linear Combination of Unitaries (LCU) for block-encoding, the Quantum Shannon Wavelet Transform (QSWT) for multi-scale representations, and a nested-box inequality-test framework for efficient state preparation.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to discretized continuum field theories and many-body quantum systems on a finite lattice, specifically focusing on translation-invariant pseudodifferential operators like the SLAC Laplacian and first-order derivative.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Complexity metrics including subnormalization factor (alpha), gate complexity (O(n^2)), and error scaling (epsilon); momentum scales (IR and UV sectors).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes coefficient functions are smoothly decaying, the lattice size is a power of two, and the right-hand-side state has support on the invertible subspace of the operator.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a quantum circuit implementation of the QSWT; Figure 2 illustrates a high-level circuit diagram for the state-preparation protocol; Figure 4 depicts the SELECT operation as a black box of adder circuits.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of SLAC discretization, proposes LCU-based block-encodings for derivatives, details a state-preparation protocol using nested-box inequality tests, implements multi-scale representations via QSWT, introduces a wavelet preconditioner, and concludes with a complexity analysis and comparison to general PDO frameworks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present the construction of efficient linear-combination-of-unitaries (LCU)-based block-encodings for the first-order derivative and Laplacian operators in the SLAC representation. We use state-preparation techniques designed for smoothly decaying functions to efficiently prepare the dense LCU amplitudes with high success probability and low gate cost. Furthermore, we demonstrate how Shannon wavelet transforms can be applied to these block-encodings to efficiently obtain multi-scale representations of the SLAC derivative operators. We then show how to apply a diagonal preconditioner that reduces the condition number of these matrices in the multi-scale wavelet basis to a small constant. This approach enables the solution of partial differential equations with SLAC-discretised derivative operators on a finite lattice using the quantum linear solving algorithm (QLSA). Throughout this work, we analyse the computational complexity and error scaling of each implementation.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses advanced Monte Carlo techniques to study the topological and scaling properties of self-avoiding theta-graphs. It provides high-precision estimates for critical exponents and supports long-standing theoretical conjectures regarding polymer networks in two dimensions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the scaling behavior, critical exponents, and geometric properties of self-avoiding theta-graphs (spatial graphs with two vertices of degree 3) on 2D square and 3D cubic lattices.

**Main result.** The authors found evidence supporting Duplantier's conjecture for the entropic critical exponent in 2D monodisperse theta-graphs and provided precise estimates for exponents governing arm-length distributions and branch point separation.

**Method.** A combination of the Wang-Landau Monte Carlo algorithm for density of states estimation and a variant of the BFACF algorithm adapted for degree-3 vertices was used.

**Model / system.** The model consists of self-avoiding theta-graphs (polydisperse and monodisperse) embedded in 2D square and 3D simple cubic lattices, focusing on unknotted configurations in 3D.

**Key observables.** Critical exponents (zeta, beta, sigma, tau, eta, nu), growth rate (mu), arm-length distributions, and mean squared distance between branch points.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Total graph size (n), arm lengths (l1, l2, l1+l2), and lattice dimensionality (2D vs 3D).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on unknotted theta-graphs in 3D and assumes ergodicity within the expanded BFACF move set.

**Figures summary.** Figures include lattice topology illustrations, examples of theta-graphs, schematics of BFACF moves, and various plots of scaling ratios and error convergence used to estimate critical exponents.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the theta-graph model, describes the modified BFACF and Wang-Landau algorithms, presents numerical results for 2D and 3D scaling exponents, and concludes with comparisons to theoretical conjectures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Theta-graphs are a type of spatial graph with two vertices connected by three edges. We investigate embeddings of theta-graphs in the square and simple cubic lattices, using a combination of the Wang-Landau Monte Carlo method with a variant of the BFACF algorithm which accommodates vertices of degree 3. This allows us to estimate the critical exponents governing the number of theta-graphs and the distributions of the different arm-lengths. For the cubic lattice these values can be compared to the corresponding exponents for prime knots. We also study the number of `monodisperse' theta-graphs where the three arms have the same lengths, and find evidence supporting a conjecture for the critical exponent in two dimensions.

</details>

### [Regulating oxygen content and superconductivity in La$_3$Ni$_2$O$_{7+δ}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04562v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how precise control of oxygen stoichiometry in La3Ni2O7+d can be used to regulate structural intergrowths and tune the superconducting properties. By establishing a phase diagram, the authors show that oxygen content directly modulates the upper critical field and phase purity in these nickelates. This provides essential insights for the synthetic control and mechanistic understanding of high-temperature superconductivity in the Ruddlesden-Popper series.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the difficulty of synthesizing high-quality Ruddlesden-Popper nickelates due to uncontrolled oxygen content and the presence of structural intergrowth phases, which complicates the understanding of high-Tc superconductivity in La3Ni2O7+d.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that oxygen content regulates both the structural phase purity (controlling bilayer, hybrid-1212, and trilayer intergrowths) and the superconducting strength, specifically modulating the upper critical field (Hc2) of the bilayer phase.

**Method.** The work employs solid-state and sol-gel synthesis with oxygen tuning via annealing, structural characterization using XAFS, Neutron Powder Diffraction, and STEM, and high-pressure electronic transport measurements in a diamond anvil cell.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the Ruddlesden-Popper nickelate La3Ni2O7+d, specifically focusing on the bilayer phase and its associated intergrowth phases (hybrid-1212 and trilayer) under high pressure (up to ~37.6 GPa).

**Key observables.** Oxygen stoichiometry (delta), Ni valence state, superconducting transition temperature (Tc), upper critical field (Hc2), Ni-O-Ni bond angle, and NiO6 octahedral distortion.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Oxygen content range (-0.34 to +0.08), pressure regime (>25 GPa), and Ni valence state.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Ni K-edge XAFS spectra, Ni valence determination, and correlation between crystal field splitting and oxygen content; Fig 2: TGA curves for oxygen-doped samples; Fig 3: Temperature-dependent resistance under pressure showing superconductivity; Fig 4: Characterization of specific samples.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the characterization of Ni valence and structural distortions via XAFS, followed by the verification of oxygen stoichiometry via TGA, then moves to the identification of structural intergrowth phases using diffraction and microscopy, and concludes with high-pressure transport measurements and the establishment of a phase diagram relating oxygen content to superconductivity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The synthesis of high-quality Ruddlesden-Popper (RP) nickelates remains challenging due to variations in oxygen content and the prevalence of intergrown RP phases. Precisely controlling the stoichiometry and characterizing the resulting physical properties are essential for understanding the mechanism of high-$T_c$ superconductivity in these materials. In this work, we synthesize a series of La$_3$Ni$_2$O$_{7+δ}$ samples with systematically controlled oxygen content and perform comprehensive structural and compositional analyses. Precise oxygen tuning enables us to tailor the microstructure, yielding a pure bilayer phase, a mixture of bilayer and hybrid single-layer-bilayer phases, and a predominantly bilayer phase containing trilayer intergrowths. High-pressure transport measurements reveal distinct superconducting transitions with contrasting $T_c$ values, corresponding to the bilayer phase, the hybrid phase, and trilayer inclusions. Notably, we find that oxygen content not only governs the phase purity$-$i.e., the presence of intergrowth phases$-$but also directly modulates the upper critical field ($H_{c2}$) of the bilayer superconductivity. By establishing a phase diagram of $T_c$ and $H_{c2}$ as functions of oxygen content in La$_3$Ni$_2$O$_{7+δ}$, this work advances synthetic control and provides new insights into the superconducting mechanism of RP nickelates.

</details>

### [Scalable Quantum Reservoir Computing over Distributed Quantum Architectures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04991v1)

**Authors:** Ioannis Liliopoulos, Georgios D. Varsamis, Konstantinos Rallis, Evangelos Tsipas, Ioannis G. Karafyllidis, Georgios Ch. Sirakoulis, Panagiotis Dimitrakis

<sub>Fig. 5: Mean Absolute Error and Root Mean Square Error distribution for all Architecture 2 (MRSR) models considered.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes and evaluates scalable, distributed quantum reservoir computing architectures for time-series forecasting. It demonstrates that partitioning quantum resources across multiple QPUs can mitigate noise and improve prediction accuracy compared to classical and single-node quantum methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of training recurrent neural networks (RNNs) using backpropagation through time and the need for scalable, modular quantum architectures for time-series forecasting in the NISQ era.

**Main result.** Quantum-enhanced distributed architectures consistently outperform classical baselines, with peak reductions in MAE and RMSE of up to 78.8% and 72.3%, respectively.

**Method.** The study evaluates four architectures (SRSR, MRSR, SRMR, MRMR) using quantum reservoir computing with quantum-kernel-based ridge regression, utilizing both ideal and hardware-informed noisy simulations.

**Model / system.** A modular, multi-QPU pipeline consisting of quantum neurons (4-qubit circuits with feature maps and ansatze) and quantum kernel-based readout layers, simulated using Qiskit and CUDA-Q with noise models from IBM and IONQ hardware.

**Key observables.** Mean Absolute Error (MAE), Root Mean Squared Error (RMSE), and Coefficient of Determination (R2).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of neurons, number of reservoirs, number of readout instances, and number of qubits in the quantum feature map.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the NISQ era constraints, specifically focusing on small-scale, noisy, and limited-qubit hardware environments.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the different reservoir architectures; Figure 2 shows the quantum neuron structure (feature map and ansatz); Figure 3 details the quantum kernel feature map; Figures 4-7 show the distribution of error metrics across different architectures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of RNN training, defines the four proposed distributed quantum reservoir architectures, details the quantum neuron and kernel construction, presents performance benchmarks across ideal and noisy simulations, and concludes with a discussion on scaling and noise robustness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reservoir computing provides an alternative to recurrent neural networks by overcoming the common problems of backpropagation through time and by training only a simple readout layer. The emerging field of quantum computing offers a new computing paradigm that promises to enhance learning through richer feature representations. In this work, we investigate quantum reservoir computing for time-series forecasting. We explore and benchmark four different architectures that combine single or multiple (distributed) reservoirs with single or multiple (distributed) ridge-regression readout layers. We evaluate these architectures using ideal and hardware-informed noisy simulations, and include both hybrid and fully quantum variants, with classical reservoir counterparts serving as a baseline. The results indicate that quantum-enhanced configurations consistently improve forecasting accuracy by reducing the mean absolute error (MAE) and the root mean squared error (RMSE) up to 78.8% and 72.3%, respectively, while distributed architectures effectively enable scaling by utilizing multiple quantum resources in a hardware-agnostic manner. These findings support distributed quantum reservoir computing as a promising, modular approach for forecasting on the quantum platforms of the noisy intermediate-scale quantum (NISQ) era.

</details>

### [Scattering-Induced Loss in Ferroelectric Photonic Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04353v1)

**Summary.** The paper develops a theory to quantify photon loss in ferroelectric waveguides caused by interface roughness and domain disorder. It demonstrates that loss peaks when domain sizes match the wavelength and suggests using sub-micron domains to enable low-loss quantum photonic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum optics and quantum networking as it provides a theoretical framework for minimizing photon loss in high-nonlinearity materials essential for scalable quantum photonic integrated circuits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the high optical loss in ferroelectric photonic devices, specifically Barium Titanate (BTO) waveguides, which hinders their integration into quantum photonic chips and the quantum internet.

**Main result.** The study identifies that loss is maximized in the Mie regime (domain size ~ wavelength) and provides a strategy to reduce loss by operating in the Rayleigh regime (sub-micron domains) or using single-domain waveguides.

**Method.** A perturbative electrodynamical theory using the sourceless Ampere-Maxwell law to treat permittivity fluctuations as effective volume currents, integrated with electron microscopy data.

**Model / system.** Barium Titanate (BTO) on Silicon Dioxide (SiO2) slab waveguides, featuring spatial fluctuations in electric permittivity due to interface roughness and ferroelectric domain disorder.

**Key observables.** Attenuation coefficient (alpha)

**Important parameters / regimes.** Domain length scales (L), wavelength of light (lambda), refractive indices of core and cladding, Mie regime, and Rayleigh regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of near-field effects in unguided mode loss calculation; use of a free-space approximation for unguided modes; and a simplified model for domain loss using only primary peaks.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a waveguide diagram with roughness and domain distribution; Figure 2 compares 1D and 2D roughness models; Figure 3 displays spectral density of fluctuations; Figure 4 illustrates the transition between Mie and Rayleigh regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of loss in BTO waveguides, presents a perturbative theory for scattering, applies the theory to interface roughness and domain disorder, compares 1D and 2D models, and concludes with design implications for low-loss devices.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ferroelectric materials have colossal optical nonlinearities, but their integration into quantum photonic chips is made challenging by the additional loss mechanisms that they introduce. Here we present a perturbative theory that expresses non-absorptive (elastic) photon scattering-induced loss as a functional of a general spectral density for spatial fluctuations of electric permittivity. We apply the theory to calculations of attenuation coefficients $α$ in slab waveguides in order to compare two distinct loss mechanisms: Interface roughness and ferroelectric domain disorder. our theory can account for realistic roughness without special symmetry considerations, and it demonstrates how to use electron microscoopy images of ferroelectric domains to obtain explicit numerical predictions for $α$. Loss is maximum when the mean domain length is comparable to the wavelength of light (Mie regime), indicating that, for telecom wavelengths, sub-micron domains (Rayleigh regime) or single domain waveguides provide equivalent strategies for reducing loss.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04767"></a>
### [Scheduling Entanglement Flows in Multi-channel Quantum Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04767v1)

**Authors:** Gongyu Ni, Lester Ho  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04767v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04767_figures/2605.04767_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIGURE 1: Entanglement request queue and example quantum network topology. The requests (A, B), (D, C), (B, D), and (F, B) are selected for execution and processed in parallel. The request (D, C), however, fails to meet the required entanglement fidelity by the end of the time slot and is moved, along with (B, E), to the pending group for the next slot. At each time slot, network resources are refreshed, making all feasible links available. The request set comprises both newly arrived and pending requests, after which a scheduler determines which requests are executed and which remain pending.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04767_figures/2605.04767_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIGURE 2: Comparison of path allocation for accommodating entanglement requests (D, C), (A, B), and (B, E) in sequence. In the static efficient allocation, paths are predetermined, which prevents request (B, E) from being parallelized at step 3 due to the unavailability of its predefined path [B, A, E]. In contrast, the dynamic efficient allocation selects paths iteratively based on the updated sub-graph; a feasible path [B, D, E] remains available, allowing (B, E) to be served in parallel with the other requests.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04767_figures/2605.04767_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIGURE 3: The PPO model structure.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04767_figures/2605.04767_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIGURE 4: Network topologies of varying sizes and types. Links are colour-coded by photon loss rate [dB/km]: green (0.15), yellow-green (0.20), orange (0.25), red (0.30), and dark red (0.35). Warmer colours indicate higher photon loss rate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04767_figures/2605.04767_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIGURE 5: Average capacity utilization over 50 time slots with different network size.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes new strategies for managing entanglement distribution in complex quantum networks. By using reinforcement learning, the authors demonstrate a way to maximize successful entanglement requests and network efficiency while minimizing latency in multi-channel architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing resource allocation and scheduling for entanglement flows in multi-channel quantum networks to balance delay, success rates, and capacity utilization.

**Main result.** A PPO-based reinforcement learning approach provides the best overall balance by improving capacity utilization while maintaining low delay and high success rates, outperforming classical greedy and static algorithms.

**Method.** The study compares several allocation strategies, including Dynamic/Static Efficient, Success Enhancement (multipath), and a Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) reinforcement learning framework, using multi-slot simulations.

**Model / system.** A multi-channel quantum network model featuring heterogeneous links with photon loss, quantum repeaters for entanglement swapping, and quantum memories subject to dephasing and depolarizing errors. The network topologies include Watts-Strogatz and Random Geometric graphs.

**Key observables.** Request delay, total number of successful entanglement requests, network capacity utilization, and entanglement request handling rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fibre attenuation (0.15 to 0.35 dB/km), dephasing and depolarizing rates, fidelity threshold (0.5), and varying network sizes (10, 20, 30 nodes).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scheduling framework is centralized, and resources (links and memories) are replenished at the start of each time slot.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the request queue and topology; Figure 2 compares path allocation between Static and Dynamic methods; Figure 3 illustrates the PPO architecture; Figure 4 visualizes different network topologies; Figure 5 shows performance scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the resource allocation problem, defines the physical and network models, details the proposed allocation algorithms (including the PPO RL approach), presents simulation results across various topologies and loads, and concludes with a discussion on scalability and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper addresses resource allocation for entanglement distribution in multi-channel quantum networks. A system model is proposed that integrates a multi-channel quantum network architecture with heterogeneous link characteristics and user-centric entanglement request handling, including queuing and retry mechanisms. Classical allocation methods for assigning channels and quantum processors to generate entanglement between end nodes are implemented, including the Dynamic Efficient algorithm, Static Efficient algorithm, and the Success Enhancement algorithm. In addition, a Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO)-based allocation approach driven by a reward function is proposed. These methods are evaluated through multi-slot simulations using metrics such as request delay, total number of successful entanglement requests, network capacity utilization, and the entanglement request handling rate. The results show that Dynamic Efficient achieves the lowest delay, while Success Enhancement improves the number of successful requests through multipath allocation. The PPO-based method provides the best overall balance by improving capacity utilization and achieving both low delay and a high number of successful entanglement requests.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.04253"></a>
### [Second-Order FALQON Parameter Transfer for the Max-Cut Problem on 3-Regular Graphs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04253v1)

**Authors:** Gabriel Fernandes Thomaz, Eduarda Rodrigues Monteiro, Jerusa Marchi, Marcelo Zen Pretto, Alisson dos Passos Fumaco, Evandro Chagas Ribeiro da Rosa  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04253v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04253_figures/2605.04253_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Average optimal time step (∆t) that maximizes the approximation ratio after 16 layers, plotted on a log-log scale as a function of the 3-regular graph size (n). The monotonically decreasing trend exhibits a strict power-law decay with a fitted regression of ∆t ≈0.4984·n−0.5110, indicating that the time step scales approximately as 1/(2√n).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04253_figures/2605.04253_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. FALQON parameter transferability matrix. The heatmap displays the average approximation ratio achieved when evaluating 16-layer FALQON circuits using parameters optimized on training graphs (ntrain, y-axis) applied to the Hamiltonians of target graphs (ntarget, x-axis).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04253_figures/2605.04253_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. Approximation ratio of natively optimized parameters (solid red line) versus parameters transferred from smaller instances (dashed lines). The native performance decays significantly as the target graph size (n) increases due to the algorithm selecting progressively smaller time steps (∆t) that restrict exploration within the fixed 16-layer limit. In contrast, transferring parameters from smaller graphs (particularly n = 6) significantly outperforms native optimization on large graphs.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that parameters for the FALQON quantum optimization algorithm can be effectively transferred from small graphs to larger ones. This strategy avoids the need for deep circuits on large instances by using 'aggressive' time steps learned from small-scale simulations, significantly improving the approximation ratio for the Max-Cut problem.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The FALQON algorithm faces a scalability bottleneck where increasing problem size requires smaller time steps to ensure convergence, leading to prohibitively deep quantum circuits.

**Main result.** Transferring second-order FALQON parameters (time steps and feedback coefficients) from small graphs to larger ones yields significantly higher approximation ratios than native optimization on the larger graphs.

**Method.** The authors use a parameter transfer strategy where feedback parameters are optimized on small-scale 3-regular graphs and applied to larger target graphs, utilizing a second-order feedback rule.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the Max-Cut problem on 3-regular graphs, using a cost Hamiltonian based on Pauli-Z operators and a mixing Hamiltonian based on Pauli-X operators.

**Key observables.** Approximation ratio (the ratio of the final expectation value to the exact ground state energy).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Time step (delta t), feedback parameters (beta_k), circuit depth (number of layers), and graph size (number of nodes n).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The success of the transfer assumes local structural similarities between graphs and is tested on 3-regular graphs with uniform local structure.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the power-law decay of the optimal time step as a function of graph size; Figure 2 is a heatmap showing the average approximation ratio achieved via parameter transfer.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the FALQON scalability problem, describes the second-order algorithm and the parameter transfer strategy, presents numerical experiments on 3-regular graphs, and discusses the scaling advantages and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Feedback-based Algorithm for Quantum Optimization (FALQON) offers a deterministic alternative to variational quantum algorithms by bypassing classical optimization loops. However, maintaining convergence on large problem instances often requires restricting the time step, necessitating quantum circuit depths that exceed Noisy Intermediate-Scale Quantum (NISQ) hardware capabilities. This paper investigates the parameter transferability of second-order FALQON applied to the Max-Cut problem on 3-regular graphs. Through numerical experiments evaluating quantum circuits up to 16 layers on graphs up to 24 nodes, we demonstrate a highly advantageous scaling behavior: transferring feedback parameters optimized on small instances to larger target graphs yields significantly higher approximation ratios than natively optimizing the parameters directly on the larger graphs. This performance advantage arises because parameters trained on smaller instances can safely adopt aggressively larger time steps. By offloading the expensive parameter discovery phase to small-scale instances, this transfer strategy simultaneously reduces computational overhead and enhances the approximation ratio, thereby bringing FALQON closer to practical viability on near-term quantum architectures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04151"></a>
### [Topological subsystem bivariate bicycle codes with four-qubit check operators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04151v1)

**Authors:** Zijian Liang, Yu-An Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04151v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04151_figures/2605.04151_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Weight-12 stabilizers and representative weight-5 dressed logical operators of the [[75, 10, 5]] SBB code. Panels (a) and (b) show local X- and Z-type stabilizers, respec- tively. These stabilizers are products of nearby gauge gen- erators and lie in the center of the gauge group; hence they commute with all gauge operators. Panels (c) and (d) show representative minimum-weight dressed logical operators. In panel (c), red and purple both denote Pauli-X operators and represent two inequivalent weight-5 logical-X operators. The 5 translations of each operator in the y direction give 10 in- dependent dressed logical-X operators modulo multiplication by gauge operators. Similarly, in...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04151_figures/2605.04151_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Four types of local gauge generators of the [[75, 10, 5]] SBB code. All other gauge generators are obtained by lattice translations. In the convention of Eq. (1), they are specified by polynomials: (a) (f1, g1, h1) = (x2, y2, x + x2y); (b) (f2, g2, h2) = (1 + y2, x + y, 0); (c) (f3, g3, h3) = (y2, y+xy2, x2); (d) (f4, g4, h4) = (1+x2, 0, x+ y).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04151_figures/2605.04151_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Clifford reduction of the [[75, 10, 5]] SBB code. (a)(b) The circuit U = U2U1 consists of two translation-invariant layers of CNOT gates. Within each layer, the CNOT gates mutually commute, so both U1 and U2 are depth-3 circuits. The circuit maps the gauge pair GX,1, GZ,1 to a single-qubit Pauli X, Z pair on the vertex gauge qubit. These gauge qubits can then be decoupled from the protected subsystem. (c)(d) After removing the vertex gauge qubits, the transformed stabilizers act on the edge qubits and have weight 8, reduced from weight 12 in the subsystem description. On the 5 × 5 lattice, the resulting stabilizer code has parameters [[50, 10, 5]].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.04151_figures/2605.04151_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Examples of Laurent-polynomial representations of Pauli operators on the square lattice. Each unit cell contains three qubit positions, labeled v1, v2, and v3. Pauli operators on these qubits are encoded as six-component vectors over Z2[x±1, y±1], as in Eq. (B1). Multiplication by xnym translates an operator by (n, m) lattice units. For example, translating the operator supported on the three qubits at the origin to (−1, −1) multiplies its vector by x−1y−1. Sums of such translated basis vectors give the polynomial-vector representation of gauge generators.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04151_figures/2605.04151_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Inverse construction of the [[75, 10, 5]] SBB code from the corresponding BB code. The upper row shows the gauge-qubit Pauli pair on each vertex together with the BB stabilizers decorated by these gauge qubits. Applying the inverse Clifford circuit U † gives the four weight-4 SBB gauge generators shown in the lower row. Thus the weight-8 BB stabilizer checks are realized through local weight-4 gauge measurements in the SBB description.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new class of quantum error-correcting codes called SBB codes that reduce the complexity of syndrome extraction by using local weight-4 gauge measurements. These codes maintain the high encoding rates of bivariate bicycle codes while significantly outperforming surface codes in terms of qubit efficiency. The work provides a rigorous algebraic framework to ensure these codes remain topological and scalable.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** High-rate bivariate bicycle (BB) codes are promising for quantum memory but suffer from high-weight stabilizer checks (weight 6 or higher), making syndrome extraction difficult.

**Main result.** The authors introduce Subsystem Bivariate Bicycle (SBB) codes that achieve local weight-4 gauge measurements while retaining the high-rate logical structure of BB codes, including efficient examples like [[108, 12, 6]].

**Method.** The construction uses a translation-invariant CSS subsystem approach on a square lattice, employing Laurent-polynomial representation and a determinantal-ideal criterion to ensure the absence of nonlocal stabilizers.

**Model / system.** A 2D square lattice with three qubits per unit cell (on vertices and edges) used for constructing CSS subsystem codes.

**Key observables.** Code parameters including block length (n), logical dimension (k), and dressed distance (d).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gauge weight (weight-4), encoding rate (k/n), and distance efficiency (kd/n and kd^2/n).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction assumes a translation-invariant structure and relies on the characteristic 2 field for algebraic operations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows weight-4 gauge generators; Figure 2 displays weight-12 stabilizers and logical operators; Figure 3 details the Clifford reduction circuit; Figure 4 illustrates the Laurent-polynomial representation; Figure 5 shows the inverse construction of an SBB code.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SBB construction, establishes a mathematical framework using Laurent polynomials, proves a determinantal-ideal criterion for topological stability, demonstrates a Clifford correspondence between SBB and BB codes, and concludes with a search for specific high-performance code examples.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-rate bivariate bicycle (BB) codes are promising low-overhead quantum memories, but their stabilizer checks typically have weight $6$ or higher, making syndrome extraction challenging. We introduce subsystem bivariate bicycle (SBB) codes, a translation-invariant CSS subsystem construction that realizes BB-code logical structure using local weight-$4$ gauge measurements. Their stabilizer syndromes are inferred by multiplying the corresponding gauge outcomes. We further show that nonlocal stabilizers in translation-invariant CSS subsystem codes can be detected using a determinantal-ideal criterion based on the gauge-operator commutation matrix. When this criterion excludes nonlocal stabilizers, a finite-depth Clifford circuit decouples gauge qubits and identifies the protected subsystem with a corresponding BB stabilizer code. An SBB code is topological, meaning that it has no nontrivial local logical operators, if and only if the corresponding BB code is topological. A finite search yields low-overhead examples including $[[27,6,3]]$, $[[75,10,5]]$, and $[[108,12,6]]$; the latter encodes six times more logical qubits than a subsystem surface code at the same block length and distance. These results show how gauge degrees of freedom can make high-rate BB logical structure compatible with local weight-$4$ syndrome extraction.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04459"></a>
### [Triage: An Adaptive Parallel Window Decoding Scheduler for Real-time Fault-Tolerant Quantum Computation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04459v1)

**Authors:** Jiahan Chen, Chenghong Zhu, Ge Bai, Xin Wang

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>Fig. 5. Spatio-temporal partitioning of a lattice surgery operation. (a) The monolithic operation volume. (b) It is decomposed into a graph of causally- constrained slices, where red edges represent mutual exclusion constraints. (c) The graph is 2-colored, which partitions all slices into two independent sets, and each set can be decoded in parallel.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes 'Triage,' an adaptive scheduler designed to manage the classical computational load of real-time error correction in large-scale quantum computers. By dynamically allocating a finite pool of decoders between routine tasks and critical operations, it prevents the accumulation of syndrome backlogs. This approach significantly reduces logical error rates in resource-constrained environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The real-time decoding bottleneck in fault-tolerant quantum computing (FTQC), where classical decoding latency leads to syndrome backlogs and logical operation stalls, especially during critical non-Clifford gates.

**Main result.** The Triage scheduler achieves an average reduction in logical error rate of 52.6% compared to standard temporal parallelism by adaptively managing decoder resources.

**Method.** A dual-mode adaptive parallel window decoding scheduler that uses a heuristic-based 'Steady Mode' for routine tasks and a priority-aware 'Emergency Mode' to resolve critical causal cones.

**Model / system.** A scalable FTQC architecture utilizing surface codes and lattice surgery for logical operations, modeled using a spatio-temporal 'slice' and 'tile' framework.

**Key observables.** Logical Error Rate (LER), decoding latency, decoding throughput, algorithm/logical operation stalls, and number of inserted idle syndrome layers.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decoder count (M), number of logical patches (N), code distance (d), decoding time vs. syndrome generation time, and the causal cone of critical operations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoder latency follows a mean-preserving lognormal distribution; the focus is primarily on surface code architectures.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares traditional vs. Triage decoding and shows the resource landscape; Figure 2 shows surface code stabilizer extraction; Figure 3 abstracts patches as tiles; Figure 4 illustrates T-gate implementation; Figure 6 shows a causal cone; Figure 11 shows decoder utilization; Figure 14 shows an optimal scheduler map.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the decoding bottleneck, defines the spatio-temporal scheduling problem, proposes the Triage dual-mode architecture (Steady and Emergency modes), details the algorithmic implementation (Predictive Causal Cone Coloring), and validates the approach through large-scale simulations across various resource-constrained regimes and benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fault-tolerant quantum computation (FTQC) critically depends on real-time classical decoding, which is rapidly emerging as a system bottleneck. As quantum systems scale, decoding latency and throughput limitations lead to exponential syndrome backlogs and logical operation stalls. While hardware accelerators and parallel windowing offer pathways to speed up decoding, dynamically deploying a finite pool of decoders across a vast quantum error correction architecture remains an unresolved resource allocation problem.   To address this, we formulate FTQC decoding as a constrained dynamic scheduling problem by utilizing a spatio-temporal framework based on slices. We propose Triage, a dual-mode architecture that mitigates operation stalls by adaptively combining a cost-efficient heuristic scheduler with a priority-aware emergency mode to rapidly resolve the causal cone of critical operations. Our evaluation shows that Triage maintains low algorithm stalls and logical error rates even under scarce classical resource constraints. Across various benchmarks, Triage achieves an average logical error rate reduction of 52.6% compared to standard temporal parallelism, enabling an efficient classical control plane for scalable FTQC architectures.

</details>

</details>

### [Efficient Quasi-Resonant, Polarization-Selective Excitation of GaN Quantum Emitters](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04927v1)

**Authors:** Nilesh Dalla, Paweł Kulboka, Michał Kobecki, Jan Misiak, Paweł Prystawko, Tomasz Kazimierczuk, Piotr Kossacki, Henryk Turski, Tomasz Jakubczyk  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04927v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to significantly boost the brightness of GaN-based single-photon emitters by tuning excitation to specific quasi-resonant energy levels. By utilizing localized vibrational modes, the researchers achieved an order of magnitude increase in emission intensity with polarization selectivity. This provides a more efficient route for generating photons for quantum optical applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to quantum optics and photonic quantum information as it provides a practical method to increase the brightness and control the polarization of single-photon sources using quasi-resonant pathways.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the lack of efficient excitation techniques and the unresolved energy-level structure of defect centers in GaN used as single-photon emitters.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that quasi-resonant excitation via localized vibrational modes can enhance photoluminescence intensity by up to an order of magnitude and allow for polarization-controlled enhancement.

**Method.** The researchers used cryogenic confocal microscopy, photoluminescence excitation (PLE) mapping, and second-order correlation measurements ($g^{(2)}$) to characterize the emitters.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of single-photon emitters in Gallium Nitride (GaN) hosted in a carbon-doped layer on sapphire, likely originating from point-defect complexes.

**Key observables.** Zero-phonon line (ZPL) intensity, second-order correlation function $g^{(2)}(	au)$, photoluminescence excitation (PLE) amplitude, and dipole orientation patterns.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cryogenic temperatures (4 K to 180 K), resonance energy offsets of 140-200 meV, and resonance linewidths of approximately 0.5 meV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors assume the observed resonances are due to localized vibrational modes (LVMs) rather than bulk phonons or donor-acceptor pairs based on energy scale and temperature stability.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows PL spectra and $g^{(2)}$ statistics; Figure 2 displays ZPLs and PLE maps; Figure 3 illustrates angle-resolved PLE and dipole patterns for specific emitters; Figure 4 shows temperature dependence of resonance positions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of GaN emitter efficiency, presents experimental characterization of single-photon statistics, details the discovery of quasi-resonant enhancement, analyzes the polarization and temperature dependence of these resonances, and concludes with a model proposing localized vibrational modes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Defect centers in GaN emerge as bright sources of single-photons which recently have been demonstrated to optically interface a localized spin. However, the structure and composition of these defects as well as their efficient excitation techniques were not a subject of thorough studies. This work presents evidence that by tuning the excitation laser energy to specific resonance values the excitation efficiency can be enhanced, resulting in relative increase of photoluminescence intensity by up to an order of magnitude. The resonances can be selectively addressed with linearly polarized light, while the emission dipole remains unchanged, enabling polarization-controlled enhancement. These results establish an efficient way of excitation for GaN-based emitters, thereby increasing the generation rate of photons. The data is consistent with excitation via localized vibrational modes associated with point-defect complexes, establishing a practical quasi-resonant route to brighter, polarization-addressable operation of GaN defect emitters and clarifying their energy-level structure.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Spin Dynamics from Atomistic Quantum Simulations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04294v1)

**Authors:** Enrico Drigo, Marquis M. McMillan, Benjamin Pingault, Yinan Dong, F. Joseph Heremans, David D. Awschalom, Giulia Galli  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04294v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method to simulate the spin dynamics of NV centers in diamond at high temperatures using machine learning-accelerated molecular dynamics. By applying Kubo linear-response theory, the authors accurately predict relaxation and decoherence times that match experimental results. This approach allows for the inclusion of complex anharmonic effects and large-scale lattice dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful bridge between atomistic simulations and open quantum system dynamics, specifically demonstrating how ML can enable the study of complex, anharmonic, many-body phonon-spin interactions that are otherwise computationally inaccessible.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified theoretical framework to predict spin dynamics, specifically spin-lattice relaxation (T1) and decoherence (T2), for solid-state spin defects at high temperatures.

**Main result.** The authors developed a framework using Kubo linear-response theory and machine learning-accelerated molecular dynamics that accurately predicts T1 and T2 times for NV centers in diamond, showing excellent agreement with experimental data.

**Method.** The study combines Kubo linear-response theory with large-scale molecular dynamics simulations accelerated by MACE machine learning interatomic potentials and a neural network for computing ZFS tensors.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the nitrogen-vacancy (NV) center in diamond. The model uses a spin Hamiltonian with zero-field splitting (ZFS) and accounts for spin-lattice interactions through fluctuations in the ZFS tensor.

**Key observables.** Spin-lattice relaxation time (T1), transverse decoherence time (T2), vibrational density of states (VDOS), and phonon dispersion.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range of 400 K to 1000 K; crossover from T5 to T2 scaling in T1 inverse at approximately 500 K; Debye temperature of diamond (~2000 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the high-temperature limit, linear order in external perturbation, and second order in the spin-lattice interaction term.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares VDOS via ML-MD vs crystalline models; Figure 2 shows convergence analysis of T1 with respect to cell size; Figures 5 and 6 show analytical temperature scaling for spin-one and spin-two phonon processes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of high-temperature spin dynamics, derives a theoretical framework using Kubo LRT, describes the ML-accelerated MD and NN methods, presents validation against DFPT and experiments, and concludes with an analysis of phonon-driven scaling laws.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optically active solid-state spin defects are promising candidates for quantum applications, however a unified theoretical framework to predict their spin dynamics at high temperatures is not yet available. Here, using Kubo linear--response theory, we derive expressions of spin-lattice and decoherence times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\) in terms of correlation functions of spin--lattice couplings. We then evaluate \(T_1\) and \(T_2\) from molecular dynamics and spin--lattice interaction time--series generated by state--of--the--art machine learning models trained on {\it ab--initio} data. Finally we measure \(T_1\) times for the NV center in diamond and compare experimental and theoretical results, showing excellent agreement.

</details>

### [Quantum Coherence Reshapes Thermodynamic Bounds for Thermal Machines](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04648v1)

**Authors:** Sergi Vidal, Alba Mayor-Fernandez, Rosa Lopez  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04648v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. a) Schematic representation of the two-terminal co- herent thermal machine considered in this work. A single- level conductor with energy ϵd is tunnel coupled to left and right reservoirs characterized by chemical potentials µL, µR and temperatures TL, TR, respectively, and is driven out of equilibrium by a voltage bias V and a temperature bias θ that determine the operating mode (heat engine, refrigerator, or heat pump). b) Operating modes of the system in the (V , θ) plane with parameters: T0 = 1, ΓL,R/T0 = Γ/T0 = 0.5, ϵd/T0 = 1.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.04648_figures/2605.04648_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 presents the thermal counterpart of F I TUR shown in Fig. 2, namely the saturation parameter F J TUR for the heat current JQ L from the left reservoir. Remarkably, the qualitative behavior is inverted compared to Fig. 2. Now, in Fig. 3 the TUR is best saturated in the engine regime, while larger values of F J TUR are found when the dot op- erates as a refrigerator. In the engine domain, the region of small F J TUR indicates that the heat flow from the hot reservoir is close to the TUR equality limit. Thus, in engine mode, the thermal input JQ L is the current that is made as regular as possible given the entropy produc- tion. This is precisely the thermodynamic resource that fuels...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how thermodynamic uncertainty relations constrain the performance of a quantum dot thermal machine. It demonstrates that the machine selectively stabilizes the fluctuations of its thermodynamic input and that quantum cross-correlations play a vital role in optimizing joint charge and heat current precision near equilibrium.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides fundamental insights into the trade-offs between precision and thermodynamic performance in non-equilibrium quantum transport, which is highly relevant to the study of open quantum systems and the stability of quantum heat engines.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how Thermodynamic Uncertainty Relations (TUR) and their multidimensional generalization (MTUR) constrain the performance and precision of quantum thermal machines in different operational modes.

**Main result.** The study reveals a role reversal of precision where the input resource is stabilized (e.g., charge current in refrigerators, heat current in engines), and shows that cross-correlations between charge and heat fluctuations allow the system to approach the MTUR bound more tightly near equilibrium.

**Method.** The authors use the Landauer-Büttiker scattering formalism and the Breit-Wigner transmission model to calculate currents and their full covariance matrix, evaluating saturation parameters for TUR and MTUR.

**Model / system.** A two-terminal quantum dot (single-level conductor) coupled to two electronic reservoirs characterized by different temperatures and chemical potentials.

**Key observables.** Charge current, heat current, electrical work power, entropy production, efficiency, Coefficient of Performance (COP), and current-current correlations/noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Voltage bias (V), temperature bias (theta), tunneling coupling (Gamma), energy level (epsilon_d), and the linear-response vs. far-from-equilibrium regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Wide band limit approximation (constant tunneling coupling), steady-state operation, and symmetric electrical/thermal biases around average values.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the device schematic and a phase diagram of operational modes; Figs 2-3 show colormaps of electrical and thermal current precision (TUR) across the bias plane; Fig 4 shows the joint MTUR saturation; Fig 5 shows efficiency and COP heatmaps.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the thermal machine model, defines the TUR/MTUR framework, analyzes single-current precision in different modes, extends the analysis to the multidimensional MTUR including cross-correlations, and concludes with a symmetry analysis of the device limits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Thermodynamic Uncertainty Relations (TURs) set universal bounds linking current fluctuations to entropy production in nonequilibrium steady states. Their multidimensional generalization (MTUR) introduces matrix inequalities connecting current covariances and mean values. We analyze these bounds in a paradigmatic quantum thermal device, a two-terminal conductor, operating as a heat engine, refrigerator, or heat pump. We show that classical performance limits on efficiency and coefficient of performance remain constrained by the TUR when finite power or heat flow from cold to hot reservoirs is maintained, even in regimes dominated by coherent transport. We further identify the conditions that optimize TUR and MTUR violations, demonstrating that cross-correlations can enhance the joint precision of charge and heat currents near the linear-response regime.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.04283"></a>
### [An extended ab initio theory of the V$_{\text{B}}^-$ center in hBN: excited states, Jahn-Teller distortion, and pressure dependence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.04283v1)

**Authors:** Zsolt Benedek, Ádám Ganyecz, Oscar Bulancea-Lindvall, Gergely Barcza, Viktor Ivády  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04283v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.04283_figures/2605.04283_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Structure and electronic spectrum of the V− B. a Structure of the V− B center in hexagonal</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.04283_figures/2605.04283_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Spectrum and structural relaxation of the excited states. a Energy spectrum of the V− B</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.04283_figures/2605.04283_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Excitation and decay pathways of the V− B center. The figure summarizes the transition</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses advanced ab initio methods to model the electronic and structural properties of the V_B- defect in hBN. It reveals how Jahn-Teller distortions and external pressure influence the spin-dependent optical transitions. These findings are essential for advancing the use of hBN defects in nanoscale quantum sensing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental understanding of the spin-dependent decay pathways and the role of electron-phonon coupling in a 2D defect, which is critical for developing 2D quantum sensors and spin-photon interfaces.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in providing a comprehensive description of the optical cycle and ODMR signal in the V_B- center of hBN due to the strongly correlated nature of its excited electronic states.

**Main result.** The study identifies the optical transitions, characterizes the Jahn-Teller distortions, and quantifies how external pressure and strain affect the zero-field splitting and intersystem crossing rates.

**Method.** High-level wave-function-based electron correlation method using CASSCF-NEVPT2 and quasi-degenerate perturbation theory for spin-orbit coupling.

**Model / system.** The negatively charged boron vacancy (V_B-) center in a hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) 2D sheet, modeled using a B60N60H27 cluster.

**Key observables.** Zero-phonon line (ZPL) energy, radiative transition rates, zero-field splitting (D and E tensors), and intersystem crossing (ISC) rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Jahn-Teller stabilization energy (355 meV), transition energy (~2.3 eV), and temperature regimes (static JT below 200 K, dynamic JT above 200 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study uses a 1D effective phonon model to approximate transition rates and assumes a cluster model to represent the 2D defect environment.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the structural representation and orbital visualization of the defect; Figure 2 illustrates the temperature dependence of ZFS; Figure 3 provides a summary diagram of excitation and decay pathways.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the electronic structure and orbital basis, moves to the analysis of excited state fine structure and Jahn-Teller effects, then investigates the impact of temperature on ZFS, and concludes with the effects of external pressure and strain on the optical and spin properties.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ensembles of negatively charged boron vacancy (V$_{\text{B}}^-$) centers in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) have emerged as a two-dimensional spin qubit system interfaced with optics to advance nanoscale quantum sensing. However, a comprehensive description of its optically detected magnetic resonance (ODMR) signal remains challenging due to the strongly correlated nature of the excited electronic states involved in its optical cycle. In this work, we model the energetics, structural relaxation, and transition rates of the V$_{\text{B}}^-$ center using a high-level wave-function-based electron correlation method (CASSCF-NEVPT2). We provide a thorough analysis of the excited state fine structure and pseudo Jahn-Teller effects, singlet-triplet quasi-degeneracies, photoluminescence parameters, intersystem crossing pathways, and stress-dependence of the fine structure and decay parameters. Our findings not only clarify the fundamental behavior of the V$_{\text{B}}^-$ center in hBN but also establish the theoretical foundation for advancing the V$_{\text{B}}^-$ center's readout for integrated 2D quantum sensors.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.05065"></a>
### [Planar chiral nanoantenna for excitation-chirality-controlled hot spot modulation and emitter-coupled circularly polarized emission](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05065v1)

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.05065_figures/2605.05065_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1c shows that ɡ at 842 nm is approximately −2, which is the maximum possible value. Spatial IE maps at 842 nm calculated in a plane situated at mid-height of the nanoantenna reveal a strong R-CPL-induced hot spot with IER-CPL exceeding 800 within the nanogap (Figure 1d), in contrast to negligible IEL-CPL (Figure 1e). This is further confirmed in Figure 1f by the differential intensity enhancement map ΔIE (IEL-CPL − IER-CPL) calculated at the same plane and wavelength. It can be seen that ΔIE is confined to the nanogap with its magnitude exceeding 800. This means that the designed nanoantenna is not only capable of yielding a single hot spot with maximal relative contrast in intensity...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.05065_figures/2605.05065_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2 Simulated amplitude enhancement (AE) of the x-polarized electric near field with respect to wavelength recorded at the center of the nanogap of (a) a fully symmetric, (b) a partially symmetric, and (c) a planar chiral double-V nanoantenna illuminated with p- (blue) and s-polarized (red) excitation. The insets show the nanoantenna geometries in all three cases, where the lines indicate the mirror planes in (a) and (b). (a, b) x- polarized AE spectra in the gaps of the fully symmetric and partially symmetric nanoantennas under p-polarized illumination, but no such response is observed under s-polarized illumination. (c) x-polarized AE spectra in the gap of the planar chiral...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.05065_figures/2605.05065_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3 (a) Poincaré sphere representation of IE at the nanogap at the wavelength of 842 nm showing continuous modulation with varying excitation polarization state. A trajectory from R-CPL to L-CPL via D+ polarization yields continuous and near-complete tunability, with IE peaking at 931.68 (approximated to 932) under R-CPL and decreasing monotonically to 0.98 (approximated to 1) under L-CPL. This corresponds to a modulation depth of approximately 100 %. (b) ɡ as a function of excitation ellipticity, parameterized by the phase difference |𝜙s −𝜙p|. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.05065_figures/2605.05065_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3 presents a comprehensive analysis of the chiral near-field response enabled by the planar chiral nanoantenna. In Figure 3a, a Poincaré sphere representation illustrates the modulation of IE at the center of the nanogap as a function of the excitation polarization state</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents a design for a chiral plasmonic nanoantenna that allows for the active switching of electromagnetic hot spots using circularly polarized light. By controlling the light's handedness and ellipticity, researchers can modulate the near-field intensity by up to 100%. This architecture also enables the generation of nearly perfectly circularly polarized light from embedded quantum emitters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics as it provides a method for engineering the spin-state of light-matter interaction at the nanoscale, specifically for creating scalable, nanoscale circularly polarized single-photon sources.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for active, deterministic control over light-matter interaction at the nanoscale, specifically moving beyond passive sensing to active near-field switching and modulation of plasmonic hot spots.

**Main result.** A planar chiral nanoantenna was designed to switch a plasmonic hot spot 'on' or 'off' by changing the handedness of circularly polarized light, and to generate highly circularly polarized emission from an embedded quantum emitter.

**Method.** 3D electromagnetic simulations using the Finite-Difference Time-Domain (FDTD) algorithm with a 1 nm spatial mesh resolution.

**Model / system.** A planar chiral plasmonic nanoantenna consisting of two asymmetric V-shaped gold elements with a 10 nm nanogap, placed on a glass substrate, potentially hosting a dipolar quantum emitter.

**Key observables.** Intensity Enhancement (IE), near-field dissymmetry factor (g), Degree of Circular Polarization (DoCP), and Amplitude Enhancement (AE).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Wavelength of 842 nm, nanogap width of 10 nm, and numerical aperture (NA) up to 0.9.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a monocrystalline gold structure and uses a simplified dipole model for the quantum emitter.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the antenna geometry, intensity enhancement spectra, and spatial distribution of the near-field; Figure 2 details the design criteria and modal interference; Figure 3 uses a Poincare sphere to show continuous modulation; Figure 4 shows the degree of circular polarization spectra.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of active near-field control, describes the chiral nanoantenna geometry, presents the numerical results for hot spot switching and modulation, analyzes the underlying modal interference mechanisms, and demonstrates the application to circularly polarized single-photon emission.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A planar chiral plasmonic nanoantenna exhibiting an excitation-chirality-dependent hot spot in a nanogap is numerically investigated. Additionally, the underlying design principles are examined, providing a broadly applicable framework for engineering chiral nanoantennas through controlled geometrical or modal asymmetry. The hot spot can be turned on and off by changing the handedness of the exciting circularly polarized light (CPL). This effect stems from the rationally designed interference of plasmonic modes excited by the linearly polarized orthogonal components of CPL. The hot spot exhibits maximal near-field dissymmetry factor (about -2) at a wavelength of 842 nm. The intensity at the hot spot can also be continuously modulated by varying the excitation ellipticity and handedness, approaching a modulation depth of 100%. These attributes enable chirality- and ellipticity-dependent switching and dynamic modulation of the plasmonic near field. Moreover, placing a quantum emitter in the gap generates almost perfectly circularly polarized emission, offering a simple yet effective avenue to realize nanoscale circularly polarized single-photon sources.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.05063"></a>
### [Patterns in Time and Space from a Single Morphogen via Nonlinear Layering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.05063v1)

**Authors:** N. Mahashri, Andrew L. Krause, M. Chandru, Thomas E. Woolley  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05063v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.05063_figures/2605.05063_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: A diagram illustrating the model given by equations (1)-(3) for N = 2 and N = 3 layers.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.05063_figures/2605.05063_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Long-time solutions to equations (1)-(3) for the autocatalytic model given in Table 1. The parameters are given by a = 0.05, b = 1.2, D1 = 1, D2 = 30, and L = 20.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.05063_figures/2605.05063_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Temporal dynamics comparing equations (1)-(3) for the autocatalytic model given in Table 1 (solid lines) with the corresponding one-dimensional reduction in Equation (23) (dashed lines). The parameters are given by a = 0.05, D1 = D2 = 1, H = η = 0.1, and L = 20. In both models, we plot the spatial averages ¯ui, given by ¯ui = 1</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.05063_figures/2605.05063_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Two-dimensional Hopf dynamics for b = 0.8. (a) Relative variation in each layer over time, given by Vi(t) = 100 (max ui −min ui)/mean(ui), with the circle- and square-marked peaks corresponding to the selected times shown in the snapshots (b) and (c), illustrating pronounced vertical variation during the oscillation cycle.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.05063_figures/2605.05063_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
